Carlos Basso alerta sobre Peter Thiel, Palantir y el nuevo poder de Silicon Valley

El Mostrador(72.8K) ·28:24 ·804 views · 2026-05-08
Carlos Basso (Editor, El Mostrador) Peter Thiel (Cofounder, Palantir Technologies)
The emergence of "Techno-fascism" and the "Dark Enlightenment" ideology, led by figures like Peter Thiel and Palantir Technologies, represents a global shift toward governance by "CEO monarchs" and AI-driven military deterrence, with active expansion efforts now reaching Chile and Argentina.
TL;DR The emergence of "Techno-fascism" and the "Dark Enlightenment" ideology, led by figures like Peter Thiel and Palantir Technologies, represents a global shift toward governance by "CEO monarchs" and AI-driven military deterrence, with active expansion efforts now reaching...

TL;DR

The emergence of "Techno-fascism" and the "Dark Enlightenment" ideology, led by figures like Peter Thiel and Palantir Technologies, represents a global shift toward governance by "CEO monarchs" and AI-driven military deterrence, with active expansion efforts now reaching Chile and Argentina.

The Ideology of the "Dark Enlightenment"

  • Rejection of Democracy: Carlos Basso describes a paradigm shift where Silicon Valley "techno-barons" view the post-Enlightenment democratic model (separation of powers, checks and balances) as obsolete.
      • The Neo-Feudal Model: Proponents like Peter Thiel advocate for a return to pre-Enlightenment structures, where states are managed like corporations by a "CEO" or "monarch" rather than an elected president.
      • Techno-fascism: This movement argues that democracy is no longer compatible with capitalism or individual liberty, favoring efficiency and personal interest over collective representation.
  • Political Influence: These figures are no longer just tech providers but ideological architects.
      • US Politics: Peter Thiel was the primary financier for current VP candidate J.D. Vance and remains a pivotal figure in the MAGA movement’s intellectual foundation.
      • The "CEO Monarch": Basso notes that Donald Trump’s governance style aligns with this corporate-state vision, treating citizens as clients and international relations as business negotiations.

Palantir and AI-Driven Warfare

  • The Palantir Manifesto: Recently published on X, the 22-point document synthesizes the views of CEO Alex Karp, emphasizing that the era of atomic deterrence has ended, replaced by AI deterrence.
  • Gotham Software: Palantir’s primary platform, named after Batman's city, is designed for defense and security.
      • Capabilities: It processes massive data sets to identify targets and make strategic battlefield decisions.
      • Operational Use: The software has been extensively used in Gaza and by various global powers.
      • Risks and Errors: Basso highlights the "muddy" ethical grounds of AI, citing reports of a software error that allegedly misidentified an Iranian girls' school as a military target, resulting in 160 deaths.

Peter Thiel’s Presence in Chile and the Region

  • South American Tour: Thiel recently visited Quito, Buenos Aires (meeting with Javier Milei), and Santiago.
      • Chilean Contacts: In Chile, Thiel met with José Piñera and allegedly with deputy Johannes Kaiser, signaling an effort to network with actors aligned with his "Dark Enlightenment" views.
  • Palantir’s Legal Footprint in Chile:
      • Trademark Status: While Palantir Technologies does not yet exist as a legal person in Chile, it successfully registered its trademark with INAPI in 2023 after a legal battle with the owners of *The Lord of the Rings* intellectual property.
      • Potential Defense Ties: Basso suggests that due to Chile's Intelligence Law, specific contracts with the Armed Forces or Police (PDI/Carabineros) would be classified, making it difficult to track the current level of Palantir’s integration into national security.

The Massive Scale of "Techno-Baron" Power

  • Financial Dominance: The wealth of these individuals now rivals or exceeds the budgets of entire nations.
      • Elon Musk: With a fortune estimated at $500 billion, his net worth represents five to six years of the total Chilean national budget.
      • Alex Karp: Palantir’s CEO holds a fortune of $18 billion, comparable to the wealthiest families in Chile, such as the Luksics.
  • Data as Power: Beyond money, Palantir acts as a "data mining" giant. The sheer volume of personal and strategic data they control gives them unprecedented influence over global policy and security.

Notable Quotes

"They believe the most efficient forms of state administration are the old pre-Enlightenment forms... it should return to the era of fiefdoms, where a feudal lord managed everything based on personal interest." — Carlos Basso
"Thiel has said it: Democracy is practically over; it is a failed experiment. He has stated that democracy is not compatible with capitalism or freedom." — Mirna Schindler
"Today, deterrence is no longer about who has the biggest atomic weapon, but basically about who has the greatest Artificial Intelligence capacity." — Carlos Basso
"These people, with that economic capacity... having the data they have, are the people who are molding the future of humanity." — Carlos Basso
Hola, ¿cómo están? Muy buenos días. Iniciamos el último capítulo de la semana aquí en Al Pan-Pan por El Mostrador.cl. Finalmente, el proyecto de reconstrucción nacional logró ser aprobado en su idea de legislar, por cierto, en la Comisión de Hacienda, con los votos del oficialismo. Contó con un PDG que no participa directamente de la votación, es decir, no entrega su apoyo, porque recordémonos lo que declaraba la directiva respecto de no haber logrado acuerdo con dos temas que son esenciales para esta colectividad, como es el tema de devolverle a las personas el IVA a los pañales y el tema de los medicamentos, una canasta de medicamentos. Pero las conversaciones están ahí y el PDG sigue siendo ahí un actor importante para La Moneda. Sin perjuicio de lo cual, el oficialismo y voces del oficialismo, pero entre ellos ni más ni menos que el presidente del Partido Republicano, Arturo Squella, están señalando que ojo con las negociaciones con un partido que, en el fondo, lo que está haciendo es subir el precio. Es más, Arturo Squella es partidario de negociar con la ex Concertación, dice, con la Democracia Cristiana y el PPD. Vamos a estar hablando precisamente con el diputado independiente por el PPD, por la región de Antofagasta, que además integra la Comisión de Seguridad, Jaime Araya. Así que hablaremos de todo lo que tienen que saber respecto de esta ley miscelánea y de cómo se ve aspectado antes de la cuenta pública del presidente, eh, José Antonio Kast. Pero ahora nos vamos a concentrar en lo que hablábamos hace algunos días, esto que dio bastante que hablar en redes sociales, que es el manifiesto de 22 puntos que fueron publicados en la red social X por parte de la empresa de Silicon Valley Palantir Technologies. Entre los fundadores de esta empresa se encuentra Peter Thiel. Peter Thiel, que estuvo en Chile y en Argentina, que se reunió con José Piñera y con el diputado Johannes Kaiser, es un personaje muy, muy relevante respecto de lo que se ha llamado la "ilustración oscura" y también el tecnofascismo. De esto y más vamos a conversar a esta hora con el editor de la Unidad de Investigación de El Mostrador, Carlos Basso. Carlos, un gusto de tenerte una vez más por acá. "Hola Mirna, ¿cómo estás?", decía él. Quería partir por el tema del manifiesto de Palantir. Es importante porque esto en Europa y en general en el hemisferio norte ha sido muy comentado. ¿Qué hay ahí? ¿Qué significa que una empresa tecnológica emita un manifiesto que tiene un fuerte contenido político? Bueno, esa es una evidencia más de cómo estamos viviendo un mundo donde están cambiando muchas cosas y muchos paradigmas. Están cambiando muchas situaciones que dábamos como objetivas o como sentadas. Y una de esas es justamente esto que mencionas tú. Si vamos un poquito más atrás, nos vamos a dar cuenta que no es un proceso que haya comenzado este año, sino que ya lleva unos buenos años. Hoy día estamos viendo compañías de un tamaño gigantesco dedicadas al tema de las tecnologías de información y datos. Estas se han convertido, por distintos motivos, en las compañías más importantes de este mundo en que vivimos. Tienen una incidencia ideológica gigantesca en esta especie de bolsillo payaso gigantesco que muchas veces denominamos ultraderecha, pero que ya tiene una cantidad de derivadas que hace que ese concepto no sea ya exacto. Hace un año y tanto, cuando asume su segunda presidencia el presidente de Estados Unidos, Donald Trump, ¿quiénes están en su ceremonia? Además de los senadores y los secretarios de Estado, están lo que se denomina los tecno-barones de Silicon Valley. Está Mark Zuckerberg, está Peter Thiel y Jeff Bezos, el dueño de Amazon. Uno se da cuenta ahí, en ese hecho, del peso que ellos tienen. Hay una foto en el acto de inauguración de la presidencia donde están todos ellos juntos. De a poco uno va conociendo otros detalles, como que Peter Thiel es el dueño de Palantir y uno de los creadores de PayPal junto con Elon Musk. No nos olvidemos que Elon Musk fue parte del gabinete de Donald Trump un tiempo. Todos estos personajes están ahí por un motivo muy importante. Peter Thiel fue el principal financista de la campaña a senador del actual vicepresidente de Estados Unidos, J.D. Vance. Es gente que viene interviniendo en política desde hace muchos años. Aquí es donde se produce este cambio de época que estamos viendo. Ellos son personas que, si tú lo ves desde una perspectiva típica sudamericana, son de lo que nosotros considerábamos antes la derecha clásica o la extrema derecha. Pero la verdad es que ellos no adhieren exactamente a esa noción. Adhieren a nociones que son incluso más extremas de lo que nosotros consideramos extremas en este continente. Por ejemplo, el concepto de la Ilustración Oscura lleva casi veinte años apareciendo en distintos manifiestos. Básicamente dice que las formas más eficientes de administración de un Estado son las antiguas formas pre-Ilustración. No nos olvidemos que la Ilustración es el periodo donde aparecen grandes pensadores como Montesquieu, Rousseau, Locke, y aparecen las nociones de separación de poderes, balance y democracia. Ellos dicen que esto no funciona así y que se debiese volver a la época de los feudos. En ese modelo había un caballero feudal que administraba todo en función de sus intereses personales y gracias a eso generaba bienestar hacia quienes vivían en su castillo. Ellos hacen el símil entre este caballero feudal y el jefe de un Estado. Dicen que sería más eficiente un CEO que un presidente. Consideran que la democracia está excedida y sobrevalorada, por lo tanto dicen que no se necesita. En ese contexto aparece este manifiesto de Palantir, que es básicamente una síntesis de un texto que publicó el año pasado el CEO de la empresa, Alex Karp. Él es un personaje bastante peculiar. Pese a que proviene de este cuño de extrema derecha, tiene un doctorado en filosofía en Alemania, donde estudió con Jürgen Habermas de la Escuela de Frankfurt. Ese texto dice básicamente: "Avanza más allá". Señala que hoy día la era de disuasión atómica ya no existe. Ya no existe aquel equilibrio que vivimos los más viejos, con la confrontación abierta entre Estados Unidos y la Unión Soviética. Hoy día la disuasión se trata básicamente de inteligencia artificial. En el caso de Palantir, uno de sus softwares principales es una plataforma que se llama Gotham. Gotham hace alusión a la ciudad de Batman. Lo mismo pasa con Palantir, que es el nombre de una piedra que ve el futuro en El Señor de los Anillos. Es un software que permite que las fuerzas que lo usan tengan absolutamente medidos todos los movimientos de sus objetivos para tomar decisiones estratégicas. Se atribuye, y aquí entramos en una zona pantanosa, la duda sobre qué capacidad de discernimiento tiene una IA que sabemos que sufre de alucinaciones. Una de las equivocaciones que se le atribuye es el ataque a una escuela de niñas en Irán, en donde fallecieron más de 160 niñas. La explicación que se ha dado a través de trascendidos es que el software se equivocó e identificó esa escuela como un sitio beligerante. Sin perjuicio de eso, softwares de Palantir han sido usados en Gaza y en otros países, como en las fuerzas de Putin o en China. Ellos dicen que la disuasión ya no tiene que ver con quién tiene el arma atómica más grande, sino con quién tiene mayor capacidad de inteligencia artificial. Plantean que hay que abandonar los prejuicios "buenistas" de Silicon Valley y concentrarse derechamente en el sector de defensa. Debe haber un trabajo conjunto entre el Estado y las capacidades de defensa creadas por estos particulares. Otro aspecto relevante es qué estaba haciendo Peter Thiel en Chile. Sabemos que se juntó con José Piñera y hay trascendidos sobre una reunión con Johannes Kaiser, aunque nadie lo ha confirmado. Todo indica que andaba en una gira sudamericana; antes de estar en Buenos Aires y Santiago, estuvo en Quito visitando a gobiernos afines a las ideas de derecha. En Buenos Aires permaneció más de una semana, se dice que compró una casa y se sacó una famosa foto con Milei. Aparentemente está intentando expandir las operaciones de su compañía hacia este continente. Hicimos un trabajo de búsqueda de huellas de Palantir en Chile y, hasta hace una semana, no existía como persona jurídica en el país, a diferencia de lo que ocurre en México y Brasil. Sin embargo, sí tiene inscrita la marca en el INAPI desde el año 2023. Esa inscripción estuvo en medio de un litigio con la compañía de marketing de El Señor de los Anillos, pero finalmente el tribunal decidió que Palantir Technologies podía quedarse con la marca. Esto indica una intención clara de establecerse acá. Este tipo de software entra dentro de la Ley de Inteligencia, por lo que costará saber si hubo contacto con las Fuerzas Armadas o las policías. Hace unos diez años hubo una filtración de una compañía italiana donde aparecía la compra de un software llamado Phantom por parte de la PDI. Cuando se les preguntó, la PDI se amparó en la Ley de Inteligencia. Este dato es importante porque, aunque hay listados públicos de proveedores de las Fuerzas Armadas, existen estas excepciones. Surge la pregunta de si se habrá reunido con José Antonio Kast, pero no hay datos objetivos que lo indiquen. Viendo la dinámica de su viaje, debiese haber sido así, pero es difícil que haya sucedido sin que se filtrara. Tiendo a pensar que fue un viaje básico para entender el ambiente de negocios acá y que muy pronto lo vamos a ver de nuevo. Esto no se trata de un grupito de excéntricos rayados. Tienen injerencia hoy en la seguridad y en la política exterior del país más poderoso del mundo. Tienen poder económico y manejan una cantidad tremenda de datos personales. Para ellos, el Estado debe ser como una empresa y los ciudadanos son clientes. Peter Thiel ha dicho que la democracia es un experimento fallido. Cuando eso entra en el corazón del gobierno más poderoso del mundo, hay que preocuparse. Nosotros tendemos a motear al gobierno de Trump como de ultraderecha, pero esa calificación está superada. Hay una ideología que está sucediendo frente a nuestros ojos y que explica muchas decisiones en Estados Unidos. La derecha más dura agrupada en el movimiento MAGA tiene diferencias con la derecha conservadora tradicional. Peter Thiel, por ejemplo, es gay y está casado con un hombre, algo incompatible con el conservadurismo decimonónico del sur de Estados Unidos. Pero para él, eso ya no es tema. En la noción del Estado de Estados Unidos hay principios que eran intransables, como la democracia. Pero estos tecno-barones poseen las mayores fortunas de la historia. Antes de Elon Musk, la familia Rockefeller tenía unos 300.000 millones de dólares; hoy, la fortuna de Musk se tasa en 500.000 millones. Alex Karp, de Palantir, tiene 18.000 millones, una cifra cercana al patrimonio de la familia Luksic en Chile. Los 500.000 millones de Musk equivalen a cinco o seis años del presupuesto completo de nuestro Estado. Estas personas están moldeando el futuro de la humanidad. Tienen conceptos que inciden en personajes como Donald Trump. Si analizas el concepto del Estado manejado como empresa y el "CEO monarca", eso es precisamente cómo Trump se ve a sí mismo. Él establece sus relaciones internacionales sobre la base de cómo negocia con sus empresas. Efectivamente, su conducta es más cercana a la de un CEO que a la de un presidente tradicional. No sé hasta qué punto lo de Trump es doctrinario o personalista, pero J.D. Vance sí es un hombre imbuido en esta ideología y él es el proyecto de continuidad. Si le va bien, veremos una profundización de estas políticas. Es un tema interesantísimo al que hay que seguirle la pista. Carlos Basso, como siempre, muy agradecidos de tenerte en el programa. Que estés muy bien. "Igualmente", respondió él. Bien, nos vamos a ir al corte, pero antes les contamos sobre "Aquí Magallanes", el nuevo newsletter regional de El Mostrador. Te contamos lo que pasa en la región del Estrecho de Magallanes, la Patagonia, Puerto Williams, Torres del Paine, Punta Arenas y la Antártica Chilena. Todo directo a tu correo cada miércoles porque Santiago no es Chile. Inscríbete gratis. Al regreso nos vamos a meter de lleno en la tramitación de la ley miscelánea. Ayer fue aprobada la idea de legislar en la Comisión de Hacienda sin los votos del Partido de la Gente. Vamos a ver si el ministro Quiroz logra llegar a la cuenta pública del 1 de junio con este proyecto aprobado. Estaremos conversando con el diputado independiente pro PPD Jaime Araya. Ya volvemos en instantes con una nueva entrevista en Al Pan-Pan con Mirna Schindler por El Mostrador Podcast. El poder de la transparencia.

Democrats Vs. Themselves On Gas Prices | FOX Across America

FOX News Podcasts(115.0K) ·43:22 ·3.6K views · 2026-05-08
Host Jimmy Failla and guests criticize Democratic leaders and the media for perceived hypocrisy regarding gas prices and Fed independence, arguing that progressive tax-and-spend policies are driving a wealth exodus from cities like New York and Los Angeles to Florida.
TL;DR Host Jimmy Failla and guests criticize Democratic leaders and the media for perceived hypocrisy regarding gas prices and Fed independence, arguing that progressive tax-and-spend policies are driving a wealth exodus from cities like New York and Los Angeles to Florida. ...

TL;DR

Host Jimmy Failla and guests criticize Democratic leaders and the media for perceived hypocrisy regarding gas prices and Fed independence, arguing that progressive tax-and-spend policies are driving a wealth exodus from cities like New York and Los Angeles to Florida.

Energy Policy and Media Narrative Shifts

  • Iran Nuclear Negotiations: Reporter Trey Yingst details a new U.S. proposal requiring Iran to hand over enriched material, accept a 20-year enrichment ban, and dismantle key facilities. Failla suggests the media is preparing to frame these potential diplomatic wins as failures.
  • Gas Price Hypocrisy: Failla highlights a contradiction in political messaging regarding fuel costs.
      • The "Biden Defense": In 2021, Secretary Jennifer Granholm and the White House stated the President does not control gas prices.
      • The "Trump Critique": Failla notes that the same pundits now blame Trump for current gas prices, despite higher domestic production under his administration compared to the "mismatch between supply and demand" caused by Biden-era production cuts.
  • Domestic Production: Failla argues that Biden’s policies—outsourcing production to Venezuela and Iran—directly spiked markets, whereas Trump’s "net exporter" status provided stability.

Economic Policy and Federal Reserve Independence

  • Senator Elizabeth Warren’s Rhetoric: Failla highlights a "montage of hypocrisy" regarding the Federal Reserve.
      • Under Biden: Warren publicly pressured Fed Chair Jerome Powell for "nice big rate cuts" during an election year to bolster the economy.
      • Under Trump: Warren now characterizes presidential calls for rate cuts as the behavior of a "two-bit dictatorship," arguing the Fed must remain independent of politics.
  • Corporate Failures: Failla disputes Warren's claim that the Trump administration caused Spirit Airlines' bankruptcy, noting that Warren herself led the effort to block the airline's merger with JetBlue.

The "Blue City" Exodus and Fiscal Management

  • New York's Tax Burden: Guest Karol Markowicz and Failla discuss the departure of major firms like Citadel (Ken Griffin) from New York to Miami.
      • Spending vs. Revenue: Failla notes the U.S. collects record tax revenue but remains $37 trillion in debt, suggesting the issue is "spending," not a lack of taxation on the wealthy.
      • AOC ("Mom Dani") Critique: Failla mocks Rep. Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez’s calls for "fair share" taxation, arguing that increasing taxes on the wealthy in response to them leaving for lower-tax states is counterproductive.
  • Urban Decline: The speakers contrast the "safe and hopeful" New York of the Bloomberg era with current conditions.
      • "AOCs" Red-Light Districts: Failla uses "taxi parlance" to describe new open-air solicitation areas in Elmhurst, Queens, as a result of progressive city management.
      • Suicidal Empathy: Markowicz argues that progressive voters often choose policies that destroy their quality of life because they believe it makes them look "empathetic" to their peers.

California’s Humanitarian Crisis

  • San Francisco and Sunnyvale: Comedian Ryan Reiss and Failla discuss the "disturbing" state of California’s Mission District and the Tenderloin.
      • Drug Policy: They criticize the state for funding drug use through safe injection sites and free needle exchanges, leading to what they describe as "zombie" encampments.
      • Homelessness Statistics: Failla asserts that while the public perceives homelessness as "tough luck," the vast majority of the crisis in California is driven by drug addiction (claiming a 93% rate).
  • The "Line to Get Out": Reiss notes that U-Haul trucks are often free to drive into California because there is such a massive surplus of equipment moving one-way toward states like Nevada and Texas.

Notable Quotes

"Inflation is made in Washington because only Washington can create money... What produces it is too much government spending and too much government creation of money and nothing else." — Milton Friedman (Clip)
"The people who said the president don't control the gas prices are now suddenly concerned about the price of gas. Democrats are so full of crap... it’s the craziest thing in the world that they talk as if we're all Amish and we haven't had the ability to record what was previously said." — Jimmy Failla
"If we understand that... interest rates in the United States are subject to a president who just wants to wave his magic wand, this does not distinguish us then from any other two-bit dictatorship around the world." — Senator Elizabeth Warren (regarding Trump)
"You're not suffering because you’re dumb or because you don’t work hard, you’re suffering because of [the rich guy]. It's a much easier [message]." — Karol Markowicz

Because America, which was a net exporter of oil when Trump left office, handed the keys to the car to Joe Biden, and we all know what happened next to domestic energy production. **Sound Effect:** Sound of car tires screeching and a loud crash. **Jimmy Failla:** But again, back then, they were like "the president's got nothing to do with gas prices." The Right After Trump (Ft. Benjamin Teitelbaum)

1Dime Radio(19.0K) ·80:17 ·1.2K views · 2026-05-08
Benjamin Teitelbaum (Professor)
The contemporary far-right is a fractured coalition of "Traditionalists" seeking to restore a structured past and "Futurists" pursuing a borderless, tech-driven hierarchy.
TL;DR The contemporary far-right is a fractured coalition of "Traditionalists" seeking to restore a structured past and "Futurists" pursuing a borderless, tech-driven hierarchy. Understanding these movements requires moving beyond the "fascism" label to analyze their specific...

TL;DR

The contemporary far-right is a fractured coalition of "Traditionalists" seeking to restore a structured past and "Futurists" pursuing a borderless, tech-driven hierarchy. Understanding these movements requires moving beyond the "fascism" label to analyze their specific anti-liberal philosophies and the novel strategies they use to challenge mainstream democracy.

Ethnography and the "Deep Hanging Out" Method

  • The Ethnographic Approach: Professor Benjamin Teitelbaum utilizes "deep hanging out"—a form of long-term immersive research—to understand far-right actors as people rather than just studying their texts or music.
      • This method seeks a "knowledge of someone that comes when you know them well" through formal interviews and informal social interaction.
  • Critique of "Activist Scholarship": Teitelbaum distinguishes his work from "activist scholars" who enter research with pre-determined political conclusions.
      • He argues that starting a project with the intent to combat the subject prevents true understanding, as the researcher is no longer open to learning unexpected insights.
  • Mainstreaming of the Fringe: Teitelbaum notes that groups like the Sweden Democrats moved from the radical margins to the center of national politics, meaning that to study the far-right today is to study the state of Western society as a whole.

The Ideology of Steve Bannon and "Traditionalism"

  • Bannon as a Contradiction: Teitelbaum describes Bannon as an "American nationalist anti-liberal."
      • He attempts to fuse the American revolutionary spirit (which is forward-looking and emancipatory) with "Capital T" Traditionalism (which is backward-looking and focuses on inherited identity).
  • Capital "T" Traditionalism: This philosophical school, rooted in the work of René Guénon, posits that a "true religion" existed in the past but has been degraded over time.
      • Cyclic Time: Traditionalists reject linear progress, believing instead in a cycle of decline from a Golden Age to a Dark Age (the Hindu "Kali Yuga").
      • Hierarchy and Borders: They believe a virtuous society requires rigid structure and caste-like hierarchies. A "Dark Age" is defined by borderlessness, intermixing, and the triumph of materialism over spirituality.
  • The "Legacy American" Focus: While Bannon has recently reached out to the Black working class for electoral reasons, his core philosophy prioritizes "legacy Americans"—those whose families participated in the historical construction of the U.S. state.

Geopolitical Tensions: Bannon vs. Alexander Dugin

  • Civilizational Disagreement: While Bannon and Russian philosopher Alexander Dugin both hate globalist liberalism, they disagree on the role of the United States.
      • Dugin views the U.S. as the "purest embodiment" of the disease of liberalism.
      • Bannon argues for a "tribal Americanism" that is inherently Christian and nationalist, suggesting the U.S. and Russia should be allies against non-Christian powers.
  • The China/Iran Divide: The collaboration between Bannon and Dugin collapsed over other nations.
      • Bannon’s View: Wants a global alliance of Christian nations to exclude China and Iran.
      • Dugin’s View: A religious pluralist who values theocracy over democracy; he views Iran as a natural ally because it prioritizes the "spiritual and eternal" over liberal materialism.

The Future: Traditionalist Right vs. Futurist Right

  • The Tech-Right Schism: A major rift exists between Bannon’s "nostalgic reactionary right" and the "futurist right" (represented by figures like Peter Thiel and Elon Musk).
      • Nostalgic Right: Focuses on "Make America Great *Again*," seeking to restore order through historical models.
      • Futurist Right: Seeks a "future arrived early," characterized by the disintegration of inherited social orders and a hierarchy based on technological performance rather than tradition.
  • Disavowal of Democracy: Both factions are united by a skepticism of democracy, though for different reasons. Bannon wants archaic, "non-bureaucratized" communities, while the Tech-Right views democracy as an inefficient process that is "100% dispensable" if it fails to deliver specific products or outcomes.

Re-evaluating the "Fascism" Label

  • The Problem with "Crying Fascism": Teitelbaum argues the term "fascist" is often used as a tool to exclude people from political life rather than to describe them accurately.
      • De-stigmatization: Overusing the label can lead to "ironic" embrace or nihilism among young right-wingers, such as Nick Fuentes, who use taboo-breaking as a communication strategy.
  • Focus on Novelties over Redundancies: Teitelbaum warns that labeling figures like Bannon as "fascists" suggests we already understand them perfectly because we have seen fascism before.
      • He insists that the most important things to learn about the contemporary radical right are its *novelties*—the ways it defies historical rules and anticipates a future that opponents are not prepared for.

***

"I believe that the most important things to learn and to understand about the contemporary radical right are its novelties, not its redundancies... The novelties are what we don't want to face up to." — Benjamin Teitelbaum

It acknowledged in some sense the permanence or the enduring nature of the far-right's ideals and agendas in Danish politics, at least for the near future. "One of the names, yeah." So that's a funny way to talk about it, but maybe that's the first thing: what made your approach to studying the far-right different and what exactly did you do? My approach to studying the far-right has been, as you say Tony, as an ethnographer, and that's changed over the years. I think for a lot of people, the way you could relate to that is to say that I've been kind of like a long-form or a recurring investigative journalist as opposed to a scholar who would just study people from a distance. I don't just study the things that they make, the music they make, or the texts. Instead, I've made a point not only of meeting them for interviews, but the real goal is to get to know them in the colloquial sense of that term. I want to be familiar with their lives, to not only interview them in a formal setting but also to spend a lot of time around them, hence the reference to "deep hanging out" that you talked about. That's the basics of the method. The idea behind it is that there are certain types of knowledge that we can have about another person, but the type of knowledge I was interested in is the knowledge of someone that comes when you know them well. It comes when you can have a conversation with them about their lives, basically. That's a different type of understanding. It's not the only way to know who somebody is or how they see the world, but it's one vital way, I think. It was once upon a time very rare in the study of the far-right, and I still feel like there are relatively few people who do everything that I just mentioned. Yeah, you explicitly said that you don't take the activist approach which is trying to understand for the purposes of combatting it. And that doesn't necessarily mean that you are against combatting it, but rather you avoid this kind of propagandistic sort of approach which is trying to create a narrative that will push people away. Your goal is first and foremost understanding. And correct me if I'm wrong, it's like you spent time mostly with, at least with the book before the Bannon one, Scandinavian far-right groups and white supremacist groups who went from being super fringe and a little bit bad on optics to being very well organized, right? And powerful. I mean, I think if you look at Swedish politics today, for example, it all orbits around the far-right party that managed to change itself enough to really collect a lot of electoral support and then come into the parliament; that's the Sweden Democrats, by the way. So yeah, they moved from the margins to the mainstream. To study them—and maybe we'll get into this: why would someone want to study the far-right?—really encapsulates the benefit of doing it. Because you study the far-right in a lot of Western democracies, you end up studying the society as a whole and where they are right now. Sweden was a big example of that. Can I pick up something that you mentioned though, Tony? You said that I'm not an activist scholar and that's true. You were defining activist scholarship as understanding for the purposes of conducting activism against the people who you're studying. I actually think the problem starts before then. I don't think that one goes into a research project as an activist scholar wanting to understand. I think an activist goes into a project with the answers. They know what they want the outcomes of their research to be, so I would have a lot of arguments with them. I have a lot of friends who are activist scholars and most of my PhD students are activist scholars, so these are not people that I dismiss. But the arguments that I have with them actually begin at the very beginning. I don't think you can both be open to learning potentially anything from someone and be committed to having an outcome to that project that is going to be politically palatable for you if you're wanting your research and your politics to align. Yeah, I've been familiar with the term "activist historian" before. Gordon Wood likes to use that phrase because there's a lot of history books that are written with the conclusion that was there well before they began any research, which is to create a sort of narrative as opposed to just ironing out the details and trying to really accurately portray what happened. Now, what interests me first to get into, because I really want to kind of go through the various factions of the right, because when we talk about the right, this is obviously a huge swath. We're focusing on obviously the radical right, but even that is super diverse. Let's start with Bannon, just because that was obviously one of your biggest projects. This is obviously a huge question, but you spoke to the guy for a very long time: what does he actually believe? What is his political philosophy? I say that because I saw so many contradictory things in the interviews. It seems like he's influenced by American populist movements, to some extent even American labor populist movements, and by very obscure far-right theories like Evola and Traditionalism. It all seems very contradictory and yet this is the guy who supposedly was the mastermind behind Trump's first campaign. Right. What does he believe? What does he actually believe? So I wrote a book on him. I don't know of a simple way to answer that question that is not going to have a lot of qualifiers on it. If you ever hear somebody give you a simple answer to that question, please doubt them. I promise you. And that's not to make him into some sort of superhuman figure, to just say that his complexity means he's great. He's conflicted and hypocritical like everyone else. He is. It's just that I think he's especially noteworthy for wanting to put together a bunch of ideas that don't actually fit together. You could say that he is an American anti-liberal, an American nationalist anti-liberal. That's one way to invite everybody to think about the contradictions that could exist in this particular person. I say that with the base understanding that Americanism, the US revolutionary spirit, is in some way defining of him. That spirit has an alignment with liberalism; that is to say with new beginnings, with an emancipatory cause, to look at the past primarily as a story of oppression and to look to the future to rectify that. It suggests that we will really be able to improve our lives and the lives of those around us if we can tear ourselves up from our inherited identities, places, and things like that. If all of that is Americanism, Bannon is trying to in some way combine that with an entirely opposed set of ideas: that what is inherited is good, that the key insights about our lives and about the universe actually already existed in the past. It suggests that the way to understand them and utilize them is actually to turn our gaze backwards, not forwards. It is not to emancipate ourselves from our history and our context and the circumstances of our birth, but instead to lean into them and to make them our future. Those two things do not combine very well. And all of the latter, all of that lowercase "l" anti-liberal thought and thinking, that's the eccentric stuff. That's Julius Evola, that's Alexander Dugin, that's René Guénon. But Traditionalism by René Guénon is something altogether very different. Can you explain that? Mm-hm. The only clue that we get that we're talking about something specific and distinct is that it's almost always written with a capital "T." This type of Traditionalism I'll refer to is not a political ideology initially, at least. It's a philosophical and religious school that is centered around a French Orientalist—although he did not want to call himself an Orientalist—who was living during the turn of the 20th century in France. He was born René Guénon and died Sheikh Abdel Wahid Yahya in Egypt, having been born a Catholic and died a Sufi sheikh. And what he and his followers were principally interested in was the possibility that once upon a time in the past there was a true and actual religion that got everything right and that was passed through initiation and through generations more or less authentically and correctly. But as time went on—and this is part of the actual content of that religion—as time went on it was gradually forgotten, gradually degraded, gradually splintered. Pieces of it then exist and are preserved in a number of different religious traditions around the world, principally the esoteric, the mystical, the initiatory branches of major religions. Hinduism stands out as being the religion that most of these figures think preserved the most of the original capital "T" Tradition. And so a lot of the work of Traditionalists is comparative religion. It's trying to kind of reconstruct and understand what once was and what the truths and the secrets of the universe are. That's the big picture. The way that this ends up in the hands of people like Bannon and Alexander Dugin has to do with the content of the actual Tradition itself. I mean I can briefly name the two ideas probably to go through if you could name those. I'll be brief. Yeah, it's really just two concepts when I'm trying to explain how Traditionalism becomes politicized on the right. One of them is cyclic time. If you think most modern political ideologies and a lot of the Judeo-Christian tradition posits that time moves from past to future in a linear motion and we escape the past and we never return to it, Traditionalism asserts something quite different. It says that we're always moving through time in a cycle, and more specifically moving in a cycle of degradation. It says that we move from a golden age toward a silver, a bronze, a dark and back to a golden. And that say for one little minute, that little move from dark to golden, which is cataclysmic and destructive, time is basically withering away at us. We're getting worse and worse, the only solace being that the worst things get for us, the closer we are to a restart and rejuvenation. So that's one part of it. The other thing is a belief that what makes society good, what defines a golden age, is that society is structured and that boundaries and borders between people, an organizational structure, reigns in a golden society. Whereas a dark age society, a society that is depraved and at the furthest reaches of this cycle of decline, is going to be defined by complete borderlessness and a complete intermixing of people. That is captured with the Hindu concept of the caste system and a caste hierarchy, specifically one that borders and prioritizes a priestly caste, the Brahmins in the case of Hinduism, on top of more materially oriented castes like merchants and slaves. But the broader principle is that if you're living in the dark age, you're going to see an age where there is no prioritization or separation or distinction given to spiritual causes. Instead, everybody is going to be commingling in the most likely and most numerous ideas—materialism in particular—are going to reign and everybody is going to be part of a kind of gray mass. That's the dark age. So you get from that a sort of anti-progressivism, and you also get from that an anti-egalitarian ethos that really says inequality and differences between people are good and it is a depraved society that would try to eradicate those differences. So someone could make a very good case that- that fascism is impossible outside of early 20th-century Europe. And then if you're looking at something, it might be fascist-like, but it's not going to be the actual thing. There's a lot of mud thrown into- into this question. It's very difficult to- to come out of it with a lot of certainty. So then the question becomes to me, okay, if I'm not as a scholar feeling like I'm standing on really solid ground if I call someone a fascist, is there some other benefit to it? And the best I can come up with these days myself is that if I'm lucky it's meaningless, because the word it is used quite a bit. Most often I think that the actual intention of using the term fascism is to push someone out of the- out of political consideration. Right. When you call someone a fascist, what you're saying is that our democratic system, our community, our society cannot tolerate their ideas being in power. And we have to draw a line somewhere and so we're going to identify someone who stands outside that we're going to call them a fascist, put them out. That in and of itself is- I don't think an- an inherently invalid thing to be doing and thinking, necessarily. But where it is troubling is when we are in a situation where there are a bunch of people are in power, they're I think winning cultural battles in ways that they haven't before, they're part of our immediate future. They're acting in new and different ways and by calling them fascist, what we're essentially also saying is that we know what they are. We understand this thing. I've seen it before. The reason I can tell you that we need to exclude this from our political life is because I get this thing, I understand it. And that is the exact wrong message for us today when we're speaking about someone like, let's say, Steve Bannon, who orchestrated one of the most- I don't- I shouldn't say he orchestrated, I'm not- I'm not sold on that, but at least participated in and has supported and been a contributor to a political cause that is remarkably resilient, that has defied all of the rules, is operating according to a set of laws that we did not anticipate and- and I think its opponents were not prepared for. The last thing we need to do in that situation is to prevent ourselves from not learning to give ourselves an excuse for not paying attention to it. Thin Harness, Fat Skills: The New Way To Build Software

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·41:30 ·12.1K views · 2026-05-08
AI agents have transitioned software development from a manual "writing" task to a high-leverage "director" role, enabling founders to achieve a 400x increase in output by "tokenmaxxing" and utilizing agentic workflows.
TL;DR AI agents have transitioned software development from a manual "writing" task to a high-leverage "director" role, enabling founders to achieve a 400x increase in output by "tokenmaxxing" and utilizing agentic workflows. The Comeback: After a 13-year hiatus from active...

TL;DR

AI agents have transitioned software development from a manual "writing" task to a high-leverage "director" role, enabling founders to achieve a 400x increase in output by "tokenmaxxing" and utilizing agentic workflows.

The Return of the Builder: Garry Tan’s 400x Productivity Leap

  • The Comeback: After a 13-year hiatus from active coding, YC CEO Garry Tan returned to building software, shipping hundreds of thousands of lines of code and launching viral open-source projects like *gstack*.
  • Cost and Velocity Collapse:
      • In 2008, building a blogging platform (Posterous) took $4M and 1.5 years.
      • In 2024, rebuilding a more advanced version with agentic research capabilities took $200 in tokens and 5 days.
  • The 400x Output Metric: Tan calculated that by directing 15 agents simultaneously, his logical lines of code (LLOC) production is 400 times higher than his peak output in 2013.
  • The Ferrari Analogy: Tan describes using modern AI agents as "driving a Ferrari"—exhilarating and incredibly fast, but requiring the user to be a "mechanic" who can pop the hood and fix the system when it breaks down.

"Tokenmaxxing" and Agentic Engineering

  • Defining Tokenmaxxing: The philosophy of intentionally spending heavily on high-quality model calls (e.g., $500/day) to "boil the ocean" of research, context, and testing.
  • Journalistic Software: Tan's project *Garry’s List* acts as an investigative journalist rather than just a CMS. It uses APIs (Perplexity, X, Grok) to ingest dozens of sources, cross-reference arguments, and produce sourced reports.
  • Latent Space vs. Deterministic Code: Success in AI engineering depends on knowing which tasks belong in "LLM land" (handling generic cases, user intent) and which belong in "code land" (deterministic logic).
  • The "Rent" Mental Model: Spending on tokens should be viewed like San Francisco rent for a startup—it is an expensive but necessary investment to create the serendipity and utility required for high-speed growth.

The gstack Workflow: Thin Harness, Fat Skills

  • Thin Harness: The core loop that manages LLM inputs/outputs and tool calls (e.g., Claude Code).
  • Fat Skills: The high-quality markdown, prompts, and logical checklists supplied by the human "director" to guide the agent.
  • The Multi-Stage Pipeline: Tan uses a specific sequence of "skills" to ensure code quality:
      • ASCII Visualization: Forcing the AI to create data flow diagrams before coding to ensure it understands the architecture.
      • The CEO Skill: A meta-prompt inspired by Brian Chesky’s "10-star experience" that asks the AI to find a path to 10x value for only 2x effort.
      • The Eng Review: Automating the creation of unit and integration tests, reaching 80-90% coverage—a task human developers often neglect.
      • Codex/QA: Using high-IQ models to find bugs and Playwright-wrapped agents to perform automated browser-based testing of UIs.

The Future of Personal AI and Human Agency

  • Personal vs. Corporate AI: Tan argues the defining question of the era is whether individuals will control their own tools and data or be controlled by corporate-hosted algorithms (similar to a Facebook feed).
  • Open Source Golden Age: Using tools like *Conductor* and *G Brain*, developers can now "extract" logic from existing repos and use AI to build complex RAG systems in a single weekend.
  • The AI Time Dividend: AI allows busy executives to "buy back time" by utilizing machine consciousness to handle the "boring" parts of engineering (QA, boilerplate, research), leaving humans to provide taste and product intuition.
"Using OpenClaude these days is like driving a Ferrari... it’s exhilarating. It figures things out you would never think a machine could figure out... But then it's also like a Ferrari in that you better be a mechanic. You’re going to have to fix it yourself." — Garry Tan
"If you can tokenmax, it’s like... you could buy millions of years of consciousness, of machine consciousness. Now I can be a time billionaire. It’s not my own time; it’s the time of a machine doing work for me." — Garry Tan
"Markdown is actually code; it’s just compiled in a different way. You can get the computer to do really astonishing things... I don’t use Visual Studio at all. There’s no reason to when I can talk to my agent." — Garry Tan

This is the thing I feel like people missed about Garry's List or don't fully get is that it's like the classic thing we've been talking about here which is like, software was you built software to let people use it. So it was like you built a blogging platform and people like write blogs and maybe like they'd start their own Substack eventually or they like write articles, but Garry's List is both a blogging platform and it actually does the work of a high-quality investigative journalist. It's not just something that a journalist uses to publish their articles. Yeah, I mean basically for the equivalent of like $5 or $10 of Opus calls, I mean I would estimate that it does the work of like you know a real human being that would have to like go painstakingly through dozens of articles, read entire books about certain subjects, annotate them. I mean going back to the CaseText example, like the thing that Jake taught me was that you need to think about what a human would do with the context given. Like what would it retrieve, like does it go to the library, what kind of book would it look for, what does it search on for... search you know on the web? I mean the great thing now is like you don't have to just do that, like you can get Perplexity's API and you can do deep research there. You have X's API, you can do deep research there. You know, Grok's API, if you need to like do research on X, using the Grok API is actually very, very good. And you can just grab all of the context. This is sort of going back to the philosophy of boil the ocean, which is one of my essays. It's like, particularly when building agentic software now, you don't have to settle for what we did when we were humans writing the code. Like and that goes for research as well. What if you absolutely boiled the ocean? Like what is you know the total completionist... like if you were a human, this would take you about a month to do this research. You can just you know zap the rocks harder. You know, you pay more money and you might be token maxing, but you should token max. Basically if there is incremental work that makes something more complete, more awesome, more... you know, in the case of this type of writing, like we want it to be more representative of reality. Like how... you know, we don't just settle for one source when we can get 20 sources and we can cross-reference them. We can figure out like, well these 13 sources say this and the seven sources disagree with that. And then you know, you want to feed all of that context into like your core prompt and then you can basically make a better decision than what you would like just, you know, a human being clicking on a link, reading a headline and that's all you understand. And I think if you token max, like that's actually the coolest thing you can do now and it's not just in you know generating articles, it's not... you know, it's clearly in writing code, right? I think now it's... it's going to permeate every part of society. Why Private Credit Is Facing Its Biggest Test Yet

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M) ·10:51 ·40.7K views · 2026-05-08
The $1.8 trillion private credit market is facing an unprecedented "crisis of confidence" as rapid expansion into the software sector is threatened by AI disruption, leading to record redemption requests from retail investors and growing concerns over opaque valuations.
