TL;DR

The $1.8 trillion private credit market is facing an unprecedented "crisis of confidence" as rapid expansion into the software sector is threatened by AI disruption, leading to record redemption requests from retail investors and growing concerns over opaque valuations.

Market Expansion and Regulatory Shift

  • Rapid Growth: The private credit market has ballooned from roughly $500 billion in 2015 to $1.8 trillion today, with some managers estimating a total addressable opportunity of $40 trillion across infrastructure, music royalties, and railcar financing.
  • Post-2008 Landscape: Growth was fueled by post-financial crisis regulations that forced traditional banks to reduce risky lending. Private credit firms stepped in to provide speed and certainty that banks could no longer offer.
  • Dominant Players: The sector is led by major alternative asset managers including Blackstone, Apollo, Blue Owl, KKR, and Ares.
  • Geographic Concentration: Approximately two-thirds of the private debt market is concentrated in North America, followed by Europe and the Asia-Pacific region.

The AI "Existential Risk" to Software Lending

  • Sector Exposure: Private credit firms became major lenders to software companies—often backed by private equity—due to their strong revenue growth, even if they lacked profitability.
  • Disruptive Impact: The rise of generative AI (OpenAI, Anthropic) has fundamentally challenged the long-term viability of legacy software business models, turning what were considered "durable" loans into high-risk exposures.
  • Underwriting Scrutiny: Investors are now questioning if these loans were underwritten based on "overly optimistic" growth trajectories that failed to account for rapid technological shifts.
  • Wider Credit Failures: Recent collapses in adjacent sectors, such as subprime auto lender Tricolor and First Brands, have fueled fears of hidden leverage and poor underwriting standards across the broader credit ecosystem.

Liquidity Stress and the Retail Exodus

  • Redemption Requests: High-net-worth retail investors, who recently flooded into tailored private credit products, are now seeking the exits at an "unprecedented" rate.
  • Liquidity Gating: To preserve fund stability, firms including Blackstone, Blue Owl, and Apollo have been forced to enforce withdrawal caps, denying billions of dollars in redemption requests.
  • Corporate Response:
      • Blue Owl allowed investors to withdraw 15% of shares from a specific fund and sold assets to return capital.
      • Blackstone employees reportedly contributed personal capital to help meet redemption demands and stabilize investor sentiment.
  • Market Performance: Share prices for major firms like KKR and Blue Owl fell by as much as 10% earlier this year as the crisis intensified.

Transparency and Systemic Risk

  • Opaque Valuations: Unlike public bonds, private loans are bespoke and do not trade on open markets. Critics argue this lack of transparency allows firms to hide declining asset values until they are forced to slash them to zero.
  • The "Cockroach" Theory: JPMorgan CEO Jamie Dimon has warned that isolated failures in the credit market often signal deeper, systemic issues that have yet to surface.
  • Comparison to 2008: While some observers see parallels to the subprime mortgage crisis, proponents argue the risk is better contained today because loans are funded by institutional and private investors rather than sensitive bank deposits.
"Credit quality is excellent in our book and that doesn't mean no defaults. Everyone's gonna have defaults now and then. You just can't have many and you have to get good recoveries and that’s exactly what we do." — Marc Lipschultz, Co-CEO of Blue Owl
"My antenna goes up when things like that happen. And I probably shouldn't say this but when you see one cockroach there are probably more." — Jamie Dimon, CEO of JPMorgan Chase
"Private credit's been around for decades... but people have been talking for years about how private credit has been untested. This is the first time that they’ve really been under this amount of pressure." — Bloomberg Narrator/Analyst
It’s been a tough few months for the 1.8 trillion dollar private credit market. The market is going through its biggest crisis of confidence in its history. Private credit firms make loans directly to companies, giving them a way to borrow outside of banks and bank-led channels. They use money predominantly from institutional investors, but recently from some wealthy retail investors too. They became major lenders to software companies, especially those backed by private equity firms, which by and large had seemed to be going pretty well. Now you just have this existential risk in the form of AI that can change everything, and private credit's just at the center of that now. Late last year, concerned retail investors started trying to pull their money out. Blue Owl allowed investors to take 15 percent of shares out of one of its private credit funds. It also sold assets to return capital to investors and reduce debt. Others got more creative. We did see some Blackstone employees basically pitch in some of their own money to help meet redemption requests. But this wasn't enough to calm investors. Blue Owl’s shares have fallen sharply since the start of the year. Industry-wide concern, though, meant it was far from a unique case. People have been talking for years about how private credit has been untested. This is the first time that they’ve really been under this amount of pressure. Some observers have drawn parallels with the lead-up to the 2008 financial crisis. It has the same trappings as subprime mortgage repackaging had. While others say that fear is overblown, especially since private credit firms don’t use your bank deposits to make loans. It’s just really hard to assess because these loans are private. Private credit lenders will tell you that they made really smart investments. But the concern is that, what if they didn't? Good news, everyone. Today you’re going to learn the connection between one of these, lots of these, and even this: Private credit. You can think of private credit as lending that’s not arranged by banks. It could be an insurance company or, in many cases these days, it could be a private credit fund. These deals are managed by some of the biggest names in private markets, like Blackstone, BlackRock, Blue Owl, and even some not named after colors—Greek gods, for instance. But what is it? It’s really a very broad umbrella term that refers to multiple forms of lending. The type that’s in the news most these days is what’s called direct lending. And that’s when basically these institutions lend money directly to companies. If you’re a company and you need to borrow money, you might go to a bank. But if you’re really, really big, a bank can’t provide that much of a loan to a single company. So instead, you go to the debt capital markets, where a bank arranges a bond or loan and sells it on to multiple investors. For a fee, of course. Private credit is a way to accomplish the same type of transaction, but just directly between the borrower and the ultimate buyer of that debt, cutting out all those middle stages. And it became central to the global financial system. Back in 2015, I think this market was around 500 billion dollars. The overall assets under management for private credit is roughly 1.8 trillion as of today. The 1.8 trillion market that most people are talking about is just a subset of what some managers see as a total opportunity of as big as 40 trillion dollars. In other words, the current market’s just the tip of the iceberg for private credit. Far bigger opportunities lurk underneath. There’s supply chain finance, there is consumer, auto loans, mortgages, there is railcar financing, there is different kinds of trade financing, and music royalties. We did a story last year about them getting into things that would have normally been financed by municipal bonds. A lot of lenders, especially places like Apollo, feel like the sky’s the limit. About two thirds of the private debt market is in North America. Europe takes the next biggest slice, followed by Asia-Pacific, and the rest of the world. And you could say private credit’s been around for decades really. I think the 1980s is probably when a lot of this started. But really back then it looked nothing like it does today. One event in particular didn’t so much grease the wheels as crash the car and set fire to it. The 2008 financial crisis led to numerous regulations to try to keep that from happening again. And one of the key things regulators essentially said was they wanted banks to do less risky lending. Regulators didn't want banks to make a lot of loans with their own money. And that's what allowed private credit to sort of step in and start doing a lot of this stuff. It can also help out when things get choppy in other debt markets. For example, in 2022, the Federal Reserve had to start raising interest rates. It really caused some problems in the high-yield bond and leveraged loan markets and made those much harder to borrow from. And private credit lenders really stepped up. And they saw it as a massive way to grow their market share. Now their loans can be more expensive than traditional financing, but borrowers often accept that for speed, certainty, and longer-term relationships with lenders. And software has been one of the biggest targets for direct lenders in recent years. A lot of these companies have very strong revenue growth, but they're not making money. That’s something that banks have a hard time financing, but private credit can do quite easily. This is the S&P 500. And since 2022, firms like Ares, Apollo, Blackstone, and Blue Owl have seen their share prices generally outperform it as they expanded their credit strategies and grew their assets under management. Over that period, Blue Owl’s AUM flew up from around 100 billion dollars to more than 300 billion. But something else happened in those four years. OpenAI is now valued at 852 billion dollars. Anthropic’s been making headlines almost on a weekly basis. The rise of AI has completely upended the private credit market. So now the spotlight is on how much exposure private credit firms have to software. And so retail investors in particular have been pulling their money. Shares of KKR and Blue Owl were down as much as 10 percent. Blue Owl is one of the most exposed. And it’s why investors are trying to figure out if those loans were underwritten with sound principles or based on overly optimistic growth trajectories that the AI revolution could now put in jeopardy. Towards the end of 2025, Blue Owl co-CEO Marc Lipschultz was pretty confident. "Credit quality is excellent in our book and that doesn't mean no defaults. Everyone's gonna have defaults now and then. You just can't have many and you have to get good recoveries and that’s exactly what we do at Blue Owl, and by the way so do our peers and so do the banks." In November though, it called off a merger of two of its funds amid scrutiny over potential investor losses. It coincided with redemption requests—that is people asking for their money back—climbing sharply across the industry, including at Blackstone, Blue Owl, and Apollo. There’s really not a fund that’s been spared over the last few months here. We’ve seen huge funds receive massive redemption requests that are unprecedented in the history of private credit markets. A lot of the firms that had made a big push into the retail market have had to deal with these redemption requests in an amount that was unprecedented. And to preserve the value of the funds, they’ve had to limit in many cases the amount of money that investors could take out. In the first quarter, funds were enforcing caps and denying billions of dollars of withdrawal requests. Predominantly from wealthy retail investors who’d sunk capital into special products tailored for them by private credit firms. In some cases, their unmet requests were tens of percentage points higher than the money actually allowed to be taken out. This all comes years after heavy inflows from institutional investors and retail focused funds. Much of that money going into software businesses that were seen as durable. Now AI is starting to challenge that assumption. And all of this is harder to judge because the market is so opaque. Some of the main drawbacks of private credit is there’s much less transparency. Private credit are bespoke loans between sophisticated parties and it’s all private. Loans are not securities. And crucially, that privacy means they don’t really trade, so there isn’t a market deciding what they’re worth. Skeptics have begun questioning the accuracy of private loan valuations, particularly after a string of investments recently had their values slashed to zero with little to no warning. It follows the collapse of subprime auto lender Tricolor and auto parts supplier First Brands last year. While only tangentially related to private credit, their demise raised broader questions about underwriting and hidden leverage across all credit markets. Jamie Dimon has been sort of a vocal critic of the private credit market, as how I would say it. The JPMorgan CEO was among the bankers facing losses tied to Tricolor and he went full insect in his assessment. "My antenna goes up when things like that happen. And I probably shouldn't say this but when you see one cockroach there are probably more." It’s a very visual image of hidden problems coming to the surface as cockroaches tend to do when lights go off. But the bigger point that he was making is there could be more problems and this could be just the beginning. And no one is really safe. This is what led some observers to draw comparisons to what happened in 2008. I think the counterargument to that is that the regulation following the 2008 financial crisis intentionally pushed risky lending off of bank balance sheets and instead ended up with investors. And so they can afford to lose that; they may not like to lose that, but they can afford to lose that. We're not really seeing that sort of widespread distress just yet. But definitely, I think we can say that the best days for private credit are probably behind us and the next few quarters, years, are going to be more challenging.

Watch CNBC's full interview with Chicago Fed President Austan Goolsbee

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·10:12 ·600 views · 2026-05-08
Austan Goolsbee (President, Chicago Fed)
Chicago Fed President Austan Goolsbee warns that while the labor market remains stable, persistent and rising inflation—particularly in services—is a major concern that may require the Federal Reserve to maintain a restrictive stance and re-evaluate forward guidance.
TL;DR Chicago Fed President Austan Goolsbee warns that while the labor market remains stable, persistent and rising inflation—particularly in services—is a major concern that may require the Federal Reserve to maintain a restrictive stance and re-evaluate forward guidance. ...

TL;DR

Chicago Fed President Austan Goolsbee warns that while the labor market remains stable, persistent and rising inflation—particularly in services—is a major concern that may require the Federal Reserve to maintain a restrictive stance and re-evaluate forward guidance.

Labor Market: "Stable Without Being Good"

  • Low-Hire, Low-Fire Environment: Goolsbee describes the current job market as stagnant rather than deteriorating.
      • Hiring Rates: Current hiring levels are comparable to the depths of a recession.
      • Layoff Rates: Conversely, layoff rates remain at historic lows typical of an economic boom.
  • Overall Stability: Despite the "cross-current" in data, Goolsbee notes that unemployment (4.3%) and vacancy rates have remained steady for over a year, suggesting the market is not "falling apart."

Inflation Concerns and the Dual Mandate

  • The "Wrong Way" Trend: Goolsbee expressed significant concern that inflation has moved away from the Fed's 2% target over the last three months after five years of being elevated.
  • Service Sector Pressure: While energy and tariffs contribute to price hikes, Goolsbee highlighted that services inflation is uncomfortably high and not driven by temporary external shocks.
  • Addressing Stagflationary Risks:
      • Framework: In the event of threats to both employment and price stability, the Fed must determine which side is deviating more and the projected duration of that deviation.
      • Policy Options: Goolsbee emphasized that "everything should always be on the table," suggesting a stern response might be necessary if inflation expectations become unanchored.

Monetary Policy and Forward Guidance

  • Skepticism of "Jawboning": Goolsbee remains skeptical of using forward guidance (words) to influence markets when interest rates are not at the "zero lower bound."
  • The "Data Dog" Approach: He advocates for a comprehensive review of all economic indicators—including food, energy, and private sector data—rather than focusing strictly on core inflation.
      • Quote Reference: He expressed agreement with Chair-designate Kevin Warsh’s view on utilizing a diverse set of inflation measures.

AI and Productivity Risks

  • Short-Term Overheating: While AI promises long-term productivity gains, Goolsbee warns against "counting chickens before they hatch."
  • The Wealth Effect: High expectations for AI can drive up stock market wealth, leading high-end consumers to increase spending before actual economic potential is realized.
  • Supply Chain Bottlenecks: Massive investment in AI infrastructure is already creating "crowding out" effects, such as:
      • Skyrocketing land prices.
      • Shortages of skilled labor (e.g., electricians).
      • Increased competition for computer chips.

Notable Quotes

"I'd characterize it as we've been stable without being good... The hiring rate is low enough it would correspond to kind of the depths of a recession, but at the same time, the layoff rate is as low as would be at the peak of a boom." — Austan Goolsbee
"I want to be one of the data dogs, and one of the main rules of the data dogs is sniff every piece of data that hits the floor because it might be food." — Austan Goolsbee
"Expectations of future productivity growth, the more hyped they are, the more they can contribute to inflation in the short run and they can force the hand of the central bank." — Austan Goolsbee
We're joined from California by Chicago Fed President Austan Goolsbee. Austan, thanks for joining us this morning. Just so much to talk about. Yeah, great to see you, Steve. Hey, let's start off with the jobs report. It came in quite above expectations, unemployment rate remaining stable at 4.3%. What's your take, overall take on the job market? Yeah, pretty much stable for a year, year and a half. I characterize it that we've been stable without being good. It's if you look at the rates, the unemployment rate's been stable, the hiring rate's been stable, the layoff rate's been stable, the vacancy rate's been stable. So, I still think there's not a lot of evidence that the job market is falling apart. And on the other side of the Fed's mandate, inflation hasn't been great and it's been going the wrong way lately. I want to get to inflation more specifically, but what's not good about the job market? You've been above 100,000 for a couple months in a row. You're at a relatively low unemployment rate. The part that's not good, go ahead, is the hiring rate. I mean, the hiring rate is—we're in a low-hire, low-fire environment, as they say. The hiring rate is low enough, it would correspond to kind of the depths of a recession. But at the same time, the layoff rate is as low as would be at the peak of a boom. So, we're getting a little bit of a cross-current in the data. But it's been stable. That's the thing that I want to emphasize. It's not, to me, there's not a lot of evidence that the job market is deteriorating any time on a rapid basis. All right, let's go to the other side of the mandate now, the inflation side. It has been elevated. Is it all energy and are you willing just to look through it, or is there a more stern response from the central bank required here? Well, that's two questions. The second one let's hold off for a second. I don't think it's just energy. Energy's adding onto it. It's more than just that inflation is elevated. It was already elevated before the war, then the war increased oil prices, now it's even more elevated. I think for me, the most concerning part is that if you look at services inflation, that's not coming from tariffs, that's not coming from oil prices. It's for several months in a row now been higher than is comfortable and going the wrong way. And to pile that on, we've been above the 2% Fed target for five years now. We stopped making progress last year and now in the last three months it's going up instead of down. We gotta just keep an eye on this because if everybody starts presuming that inflation rates are going back to something like what they were a few years ago, we would be in a bit of a pickle as a central bank. Fed Chair nominee says, as repeated several times, inflation is a choice. And I'm assuming by that metric that you guys are making the wrong choice if inflation is continuously above target. Are you thinking about whether or not you need to make a choice to address that inflation? Yeah, look, as I say, and Steve, you've been there to the room where the FOMC meeting takes place. It's literally the biggest table that I've ever seen in my life. So there's plenty of room on the table. Everything should always be on the table. That said, we outlined as a committee—if we faced threats to both sides of the mandate at the same time, we voted unanimously on a monetary policy framework. That said if we start facing that kind of stagflationary shock, we're going to look at which side is deviating more and how long we think the deviations are going to last. And you're seeing problems on the inflation side, but there is an argument that these should be one-time increases in prices and not lasting inflation shocks. That's what we're trying to figure out. That we're piling an oil one on to the tariff one before the tariff one went away, I'm a little concerned about. But for me, that idea that let's figure out not just which one is getting worse but how long do we think it's going to last, that's a core of what I'm trying to figure out. Austan, do you think that the Fed ought to remove that guidance which people say is the guidance that suggests the next move is a rate cut, and will it matter if you move that guidance? Is that essentially—should it be seen in your mind as a tightening of policy? And Steve, as you know, before I ever got to the Fed a little over three years ago, I was a little skeptical about the use of forward guidance in the statement as a way of making policy. I can understand it when you're at the zero lower bound so you really can't make policy. But we're not at the zero lower bound by any means. So I'm not—I've never been that big of a fan of trying to use words to jawbone policy decisions. Hi, President Goolsbee, it's Sarah. How are you guys judging inflation right now? Is—are you looking only at core? Are you stripping out food and energy and can you just talk us through that? We're going to get CPI on Tuesday. I mean, what people pay for is food and energy. So I am curious how you're thinking about it. Yeah, no, look, as I say, I understand why in general the Fed tends to look at core because it gives you a—it's not as variable and it gives you a little bit of a better insight into what's a through line, and that's the most important thing is what's a through line. But the fact that that is mostly what we look at drives my mom crazy, drives a bunch—what do you mean you don't look at energy and food prices? That's all we look at. I think you're never going to hear me say throw out any data. We should look at food and energy and core. We should within core look at services separate from housing separate from goods. And we should be incorporating all the private sector information that we can about prices. I'm just—I want to be one of the data dogs and one of the main rules of the data dogs is sniff every piece of data that hits the floor because it might be food. And I think that's what we should do. And I thought in his hearing, Chair designate Warsh said something that sounded like that—that let's look at a lot of different measures of inflation rather than just key on one and I strongly agree with that. I'm also curious how you think the massive amount of spending into AI and building out the AI infrastructure is influencing inflation. Some people say it's deflationary, it's boosting productivity, but right now, I mean there's a lot of money going into building this all out and it's not just, you know, in high tech, it's in the industrial part of the economy as well. Yeah, you're not wrong about that. And just look, on your previous segment, there was that lovely phrase, "Is the AI trade superseding the economy trade?" I want us to just be a little careful at moments like that. If what's happening with AI is there's going to be a huge increase in productivity, but it's not here yet, the more people expect it and hype it, the bigger the threat that we could overheat the economy in the short run. As everybody's trying to pull forward where we get a wealth effect—that consumers are going to spend out of this, high-end consumers who have a big increase in stock market wealth are going to increase their consumption and their consumer spending before the economic potential and the bounty of this thing has arrived in the economy. We gotta be—we have to be careful about that. Yeah, so Austan, you just wrote a paper on this and I was trying to spare viewers from having to talk about it but—thanks a lot Steve. Yeah, yeah, well, it's an interesting academic paper if you're into that sort of thing. But if I could if I could sum it up, it seems like when it—what you're saying is when it comes to monetary policy, the thing you're saying is don't count your chickens before they hatch. And so you sound like you are—would be reluctant to incorporate potential productivity gains on the front end of monetary policy now, but not necessarily on the back end if they show up. And it's also important how they show up. Yes, I think all of those statements are—I agree with. If what happens is like the mid-1990s where we get productivity growth and it wasn't expected and people didn't change their behavior in the expectation that they were going to get these productivity gains, I think inflation goes down and the natural thing is for rates to go down. What matters is how much does the knowledge of what's coming affect our behavior. We consumers, we businesses making these investments. And the more you see this kind of crowding out—like AI data center investment driving all the non-AI industries crazy—we can't hire an electrician, we can't buy computer chips, the price of land is going through the roof—those are kind of the embodiment of the people getting excited, equity values getting ahead of getting into the counting of the chickens, and we just gotta be mindful whether that is going to lead to overheating in the short run. Expectations of future productivity growth, the more hyped they are, the more they can contribute to inflation in the short run and they can force the hand of the central bank.

Autonomous Driving Showdown: Who Will Win the Self-Driving Race? | Bloomberg Tech: Europe 5/08/2026

Bloomberg Technology(723.0K) ·24:03 ·2.3K views · 2026-05-08
Tom Mackenzie (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
The autonomous driving industry is witnessing a strategic split between Waymo’s sensor-heavy, map-dependent "full stack" approach and Wayve’s "mapless" end-to-end AI model.
TL;DR The autonomous driving industry is witnessing a strategic split between Waymo’s sensor-heavy, map-dependent "full stack" approach and Wayve’s "mapless" end-to-end AI model. While Waymo leads in current deployment, Wayve and specialized firms like Einride and Vay are...

TL;DR

The autonomous driving industry is witnessing a strategic split between Waymo’s sensor-heavy, map-dependent "full stack" approach and Wayve’s "mapless" end-to-end AI model. While Waymo leads in current deployment, Wayve and specialized firms like Einride and Vay are betting on lower hardware costs, global licensing models, and remote human-in-the-loop systems to scale faster.

The Technical Divide: Waymo vs. Wayve

  • Waymo’s "Full Stack" Strategy: Led by Srikanth Thirumalai, Waymo utilizes a rules-based system combined with Lidar, Radar, cameras, and highly detailed 3D mapping.
      • The Waymo Flywheel: A continuous learning loop involving the "triad" of the Driver (onboard software), the Simulator (virtual testing), and the Critic (software that hones in on suboptimal performance).
      • Safety Redundancy: Waymo argues that multiple sensor types provide essential safety layers that camera-only systems lack.
  • Wayve’s "Embodied AI" Approach: CEO Alex Kendall advocates for an end-to-end AI system that learns to drive from data rather than pre-programmed rules or maps.
      • Cost Efficiency: Operates on a sensor and compute stack costing only "hundreds of dollars," utilizing six cameras and a single radar unit.
      • Mapless Scalability: Because it does not rely on HD maps, Wayve aims to deploy in complex environments like London, which features 20x more roadworks and 10x more cyclists than San Francisco.
      • Licensing Model: Unlike Tesla (brand-locked) or Waymo (capital-intensive fleet ownership), Wayve intends to license its platform to various manufacturers and fleets.

Diversified Autonomy: Trucks and Teledriving

  • Einride (Autonomous Freight): CEO Roozbeh Charli is deploying "cab-less" electric trucks that have no seats for safety drivers, designed for autonomy from the ground up.
      • Human-in-the-Loop: While the trucks drive autonomously, they are monitored by remote operators in office settings who handle "edge cases" and tactical decisions.
      • Market Expansion: Currently deploying 75 electric trucks with Amazon across five US states; pursuing a US listing in 2026.
  • Vay (Remote Mobility): CEO Thomas von der Ohe is utilizing "teledriving," where remote drivers deliver EVs to customers.
      • Targeting Ownership: Vay aims to replace private car ownership rather than just ride-hailing, targeting a market 10x the size of the current taxi industry.
  • Verne (European Robo-taxis): Launched Europe’s first commercial robo-taxi service in Zagreb, Croatia, using software from China’s Pony.ai.

Global Competition and Vertical Integration

  • BYD’s Integration Power: Executive VP Stella Li highlights BYD’s unique position as a vertically integrated manufacturer that controls both the "heart" (EV hardware) and the "brain" (software).
      • Internal R&D: The company has invested in 5,000 software engineers to keep autonomous development in-house while partnering with NVIDIA for advanced chipsets.
  • Regional Regulatory Shifts:
      • United States: Currently holds the lead in regulatory frameworks and large-scale robo-taxi rollouts.
      • China: Recently paused new autonomous licenses following safety concerns in the robo-taxi sector (specifically regarding Baidu’s Apollo Go fleet).
      • Europe: Catching up in the freight sector, with regulators increasingly "leaning in" to autonomous trucking frameworks.

Notable Quotes

"Instead [of a detector for every object], we don't tell the car how to behave. We simply say, 'Hey, here's the end outcome you need, let the data speak for itself, learn how to drive in this way.'" — Alex Kendall, CEO of Wayve
"It's the driver, the simulator and the critic: onboard components that prioritize safety... that’s what it takes to drive." — Srikanth Thirumalai, Waymo
"What we are doing different is... the brain is autonomous driving software, but then you have the heart which is hardware. BYD is the only company where we integrate software and heart and the hardware together." — Stella Li, EVP of BYD
"We've taken the cab out from day one and built the vehicle to be autonomous from the get-go... there's no safety driver in the vehicle." — Roozbeh Charli, CEO of Einride
You are watching Bloomberg Tech Europe. Coming up, the self-driving race is back on, but the industry is split on the road ahead. There are three competing approaches: systems that use mapping and lidar, those that rely on end-to-end AI, and hybrid setups that combine both. Will it be winner take all, or is there space for multiple players? Wayve versus Waymo: a David and Goliath battle of two visions fighting for the future of autonomous driving. Our tech stack focuses not just on the driver, but also ensuring that all of the plans the driver generates—it's Gen AI of course, so it generates plans—but you have to make sure that those plans that it generates are safe. Building an end-to-end AI system for driving requires a completely different approach, and this is something that we've spent the last decade building from the ground up. We speak to Srikanth Thirumalai at Waymo and Alex Kendall at Wayve and Stella Li of BYD, plus two more companies rewriting the rules of self-driving. Welcome to Bloomberg Tech Europe, I'm Tom Mackenzie. Two companies then, two very different bets on autonomous driving. That's where we start today. Waymo is leading today; Wayve AI is banking on what comes next. Waymo is combining multiple sensors then—lidar, radar, cameras, and detailed maps—and a step-by-step driving system. Wayve AI, by the way, uses just cameras and a single AI model trained to drive from data. That means that Waymo is already on the road today but needs maps and higher-cost hardware and rolls out city by city. Wayve is still testing but doesn't need maps and uses cheaper hardware and could therefore scale much faster. Both will launch in London later this year going head-to-head. Waymo works now, Wayve could work everywhere. The future may lie somewhere in between. So we begin with Wayve, the UK-based company set to launch public road trials of fully autonomous vehicles in the UK this year in partnership with Uber. I spoke to founder and CEO Alex Kendall about why he believes Wayve's approach will define the next era of autonomy. Building an end-to-end AI system for driving requires a completely different approach to thinking about safety, to thinking about infrastructure, simulation, data, even the embedded architecture you put on the car. And this is something that we've spent the last decade building from the ground up. And what we're really excited about now is that we've got, it feels like, the entire weight of the industry behind this. And it's really interesting because there are those different approaches to autonomous driving and partially autonomous driving. There's Waymo which is more reliant, as you said, on mapping and on lidar and radar as well as the sensors. And then there's Tesla with its own approach which seems at least from the outside slightly closer to the approach that Wayve AI is taking. And then there are the Chinese competitors as well, like Baidu and Pony AI, which may be arguably slightly closer to Wayve. Do you think ultimately this is a winner takes all market, or is there room and space for different approaches? You can sell your own cars, that's what Tesla's doing, but that limits you to only your brand and your fleet. And of course, there's so much of a variety in consumer choice. The second approach which Waymo's taking is to build your own fleets. I mean, that requires a very capital-intensive operation to go and grow out city by city. We've chosen a third business model, and that's to license this technology to fleets and manufacturers out there. Now sure, these three business models will coexist, but we've chosen a business model that we believe is going to be the largest because the majority of fleets or manufacturers of robots, whether that's vehicles or in other physical industries beyond cars, are going to find a more efficient and a more effective way to partner with an embodied AI platform. The AI we build can scale and have the economic and safety leverage of the entire industry that we work with. The data that we're able to absorb and provide, I think, provide a safer and more cost-effective solution, a more performance solution than what any manufacturer can build on their own. When I spoke to Waymo about their approach, they said that what lidar and what radar gives them along with the other capabilities they have are redundancies and that that makes their system safer. Are they right? I think that's a complex and nuanced question because it really depends on the product you're building. Some products will be better maybe camera-only, some radar, some lidar. It really depends. We believe that different sensor configurations are going to be beneficial for different products. Because we want to deploy autonomy in any vehicle anywhere, therefore we need to support them all. And that's the foundation model we've built, a physical AI model that can drive with camera-only systems like some of these cars, some of them have radar, or some of the products that we're building have lidar. And it really depends. But the key thing in all of these products is that it's built for scale. And your business approach is quite different to Tesla's business approach, but in terms of the technology it seems at least from the outside that they're quite similar. What are the key technological differences between what you're offering and what Tesla's offering? Today ourselves and Tesla have a similar benchmark in performance of safety, but we've built that with a fraction of the data and compute that we see in that solution. And now as we scale up data and compute, as we start receiving data from large-scale consumer fleets from our partners around the world, we are going to scale up. Alex, you're a Kiwi running a global company out of London. Do you feel the weight of pressure of building a globally scalable successful tech company out of the UK, out of Europe? You know, actually sometimes I feel like there's a bit of a "hold it close" mentality and what I want to be able to do is actually break out and build a global success story. I don't just want to be a success in the local market. I really think of Wayve as a global company and I want to compete and lead the frontier on a global setting. And going back to what motivates me, this is an adventure. This is the chance to push the frontier both technically and now commercially. So I think it's a huge privilege to be where we are today and to have the opportunity to go do this. Speaking of opportunities, we got to experience a Wayve-powered car first-hand. Right, welcome to our robo-taxi. There is something unique to London's pretty ancient road system. To what extent does that challenge the way that your models are trained or give you an advantage? Starting in London, we were out of the thought bubble of Silicon Valley where you saw dozens of autonomy companies all spurning from the DARPA Grand Challenge and the early Google project, that were all building with the same technology strategy. And to be out of that ecosystem gave us the space to go build something contrarian and build a different approach that wouldn't have been rejected by the industry there. And then secondly, London is a driving environment that is so complex that almost forced us to take this more scalable approach because mapping and driving with remote systems where you've got challenges on your connectivity infrastructure, or London is a 2000-year-old city, so you've got way more roadworks than you have in San Francisco. I think about 20 times more roadworks. You've got about 10 times more cyclists and pedestrians than San Francisco, and then of course a lot rather than protected traffic lights, a lot more merging roundabout interaction scenes. And so all of this adds up to a much more complex autonomy challenge that requires a rethink and a scalable approach. Am I right in thinking our safety driver hasn't yet touched the wheel? Oh, they won't on this drive. So you've got the model—the model is actually on the chip stack in the car. The computer is actually on the device. Yeah, that's right. So we have an AI onboard the vehicle that's currently driving this car with six cameras and one radar. It is a very low cost, hundreds of dollars compute and sensor stack. This is the kind of stack that can go on mass-market vehicles. Hundreds of dollars? Yeah, and we're driving around the complex streets of King's Cross in North London. We've got some cyclists on our left, we've got traffic in front of us, we've got a diversion in front of us, and it's driving with all the everything decisions made onboard using onboard intelligence. Is it—the cameras are picking up on indications like whether a car is indicating or not, how does a scenario like that work? This is the transformative approach that we've taken. Traditional autonomy companies would have built an indicator detector, a car detector, a traffic light detector, and then had some logic or learned systems that say how to reason about all of these things. In scenario A do scenario B. Exactly. And you can deploy billions of dollars and create such a system but it's very complex and unwieldy. Instead, we don't tell the car how to behave. We simply say, "Hey, here's the end outcome you need, let the data speak for itself, learn how to drive in this way and figure out what's the right way to represent the world." And we do this by learning a world model and that world model is able to reason and predict how things are going to unfold, but we don't tell it what features to look out for, it just learns. Turning in, no mishaps, very smooth. Fantastic, yeah, very smooth. I felt there was—this was an even more confident version to the version that I joined you in maybe two years ago, a year and a half ago. Good stuff. I mean it just keeps getting better by orders of magnitude every year. Wayve CEO Alex Kendall. Now the counterpoint then: Waymo and its full stack approach. A rules-based system using mapping, lidar, radar and cameras. A platform they claim is safer. I spoke to Srikanth Thirumalai about how that approach is already delivering on the road. There is the obvious which is the driver, the software that runs the car. But there are two other very important pieces of the tech stack. One of course is the simulator, which is this virtual environment where you can test the car before you deploy it on the roads. And then there is a critic, which is a piece of software that's able to detect suboptimal performance in the simulator or in the real world. Because then you can hone in on those suboptimalities and then improve the driver and then verify that those fixes have indeed worked in the simulator. So it's really the driver, the simulator and the critic: that triad in our tech stack that allows us to scale. You have the full stack when it comes to autonomous vehicles at Waymo. That's right. But some of your competitors are going for a very different approach, the kind of "brain only" approach, the end-to-end machine learning approach without as much hardware, without necessarily those three different components. What gives you conviction that your approach, that the Waymo approach, ends up winning out? Our tech stack focuses not just on the driver, but also ensuring that all of the plans the driver generates—it's Gen AI of course so it generates plans—but you have to make sure that those plans that it generates are safe. Right, so you need to build in safety checks in real time so you can make sure the driver is prioritizing safety. And so it's the driver, the simulator and the critic: onboard components that prioritize safety, which is an onboard validation layer, an independent validation layer to ensure that the driver does the right thing. And when you look at all of that, that's what it takes to drive. Because there will be some who say that Waymo is winning the present, but Wayve AI, Tesla, maybe others, end up winning the future. I think for us, what we have seen is that the system allows us to learn fast and learn the right solutions to some of these problems. We call—we talk about this Waymo flywheel inside the company, which is this continuous learning loop where we take all of the experience that we've gained and every additional mile of experience, whether it's on the real road or in the simulator. And we take all of those learnings: we discover, we learn, we train our models, we simulate them, we validate it, we deploy it, and then we collect more miles which is more experience. It's that continuous learning loop, that flywheel, that we build around these three crucial components that allows us to scale. Do you think ultimately the two approaches converge? Those pursuing the end-to-end machine learning approach end up putting some lidar, some radar onto their systems to have that backup when it comes to safety as you start to pair back some of the hardware to meet those cost differentials. Do the two systems start to converge at some point? I can't predict the future, but I think so. Right, because in the end we're all solving the same problem. We want to drive safely and we want to have good solutions to the challenges that we face along the way. If Tesla cracks L4, fully autonomous, and then they ship across that fleet that they have, how much of a competitive challenge would that be? We're always looking at competitors and Tesla is a formidable competitor. And so, you know, I think competition is good. But if there is that breakthrough moment, we'll be right there with it. The Waymo approach of course being explained, and it is something of a David and Goliath. Head to head, this is going to be taking place here in London between Waymo and Wayve AI. Coming up, from autonomous trucks to remotely driven cars, we're going to spotlight more of the novel tech being harnessed to improve mobility. Where does Europe stand when it comes to autonomy in the freight sector? Lyft Earnings Miss Estimates After Global Expansion Push | Bloomberg Intelligence

Bloomberg Podcasts(478.0K) ·25:57 ·36 views · 2026-05-08
Paul Sweeney (Host, Bloomberg Intelligence) Scarlet Fu (Host, Bloomberg Intelligence)

TL;DR Lyft and travel platforms are navigating a shift toward autonomous vehicle integration and exclusive inventory to combat market saturation, while legacy giants like Intel and Texas Roadhouse leverage strategic alliances and "value-first" branding to drive massive stock...

TL;DR

Lyft and travel platforms are navigating a shift toward autonomous vehicle integration and exclusive inventory to combat market saturation, while legacy giants like Intel and Texas Roadhouse leverage strategic alliances and "value-first" branding to drive massive stock recoveries and consumer loyalty.

Mobility and Travel: The Shift to Autonomous and Exclusive Supply

  • The Waymo Threat: Autonomous vehicle (AV) expansion is pressuring the traditional ride-sharing model. Waymo is currently conducting 500,000 rides across more than 10 U.S. cities, nearly doubling its volume over the last 12 months.
      • Both Uber and Lyft are under pressure to move beyond "asset-light" models and add AV capacity to their platforms through partnerships.
  • Lyft’s M&A Potential: Analysts suggest Lyft could eventually be an acquisition target for platforms like DoorDash.
      • A merger would allow for "agentic AI" use cases and cross-selling opportunities between food delivery and mobility, similar to DoorDash’s expansion in the U.K.
  • Airbnb vs. Expedia: Airbnb continues to outperform competitors like Expedia due to its "exclusive supply."
      • Direct Traffic: Airbnb benefits from being a "verb" in the industry, attracting direct traffic that bypasses expensive marketing channels.
      • Event Catalysts: Large-scale events like the FIFA World Cup are expected to be massive demand drivers for the second half of the year across all travel platforms.

Restaurant Sector: Value Leaders vs. Performance "Disasters"

  • Texas Roadhouse Winning on Volume: Despite rising beef prices, Texas Roadhouse is seeing strong traffic because of its perceived value.
      • Trade-Down Trends: Customers are trading down to cheaper cuts of meat or chicken but are *not* decreasing the frequency of their visits.
  • Wendy’s Momentum Loss: Described by analysts as a "disaster," Wendy's is struggling to find a turnaround as McDonald’s enters a period of aggressive discounting and the "chicken game" becomes oversaturated.
  • US Foods as a Defensive Play: The food service distributor is taking market share through a 100% commission-based sales plan and a focus on branded products.
      • Institutional Stability: A large portion of their business is tied to "recession-proof" sectors like hospitals, government, and education, where contracts are often indexed to inflation.

The Cultural Rebirth of Golf and Luxury Branding

  • Audience Expansion: The U.S. golf audience has surged from 24 million pre-pandemic to 40 million today.
      • The demographic has shifted, becoming younger, more diverse, and highly influenced by social media and "non-traditional" stars from the NBA and NFL.
  • Dewar’s "19th Hole" Strategy: Luxury brands are moving away from traditional sports sponsorship toward "lifestyle" integration.
      • Ready-to-Drink (RTD) Innovation: To attract a younger cohort, Dewar's is focusing on canned cocktails (like the "Lemon Wedge") and partnerships with tech-focused venues like Five Iron Golf and Topgolf.

Intel’s Strategic Transformation and Political Backing

  • Unprecedented Alliances: CEO Pat Gelsinger has repositioned Intel as the centerpiece of American manufacturing, securing a 5.5% stake from the U.S. government.
      • Gelsinger has successfully courted industry rivals and partners, including Elon Musk, NVIDIA’s Jensen Huang, and Michael Dell, to support Intel’s CPU role in the AI boom.
  • The "Detail" Challenge: Despite the stock's massive recent tear (up 460% on a trailing 12-month basis according to the transcript), significant internal work remains.
      • Manufacturing Quality: The company must still prove it can fix the quality of its products and factories to move away from outsourcing and regain dominance.
      • Leadership Style: Gelsinger’s "venture capital" style of high-level management is a departure from Intel’s historically detail-oriented culture, representing a potential risk.

Raising Cane's CEO Todd Graves: Building a billion-dollar brand on one menu item | Masters of Scale

Masters of Scale(155.0K) ·35:45 ·36 views · 2026-05-08
Todd Graves (CEO, Raising Cane's)
Todd Graves built Raising Cane’s into a multi-billion-dollar empire by defying industry norms, maintaining a strict focus on a limited menu, and prioritizing long-term cultural integrity over short-term private equity gains.
TL;DR Todd Graves built Raising Cane’s into a multi-billion-dollar empire by defying industry norms, maintaining a strict focus on a limited menu, and prioritizing long-term cultural integrity over short-term private equity gains. His journey from working as an Alaskan fisherman...

TL;DR

Todd Graves built Raising Cane’s into a multi-billion-dollar empire by defying industry norms, maintaining a strict focus on a limited menu, and prioritizing long-term cultural integrity over short-term private equity gains. His journey from working as an Alaskan fisherman to fund his first location highlights a "never quit" mentality that remains the brand’s core driver.

The Founding Philosophy: Simplicity as a Competitive Edge

  • The "B-Minus" Business Plan: While in college ('90–'94), Graves wrote a business plan for a chicken finger restaurant that received the lowest grade in the class.
      • Industry Pushback: The professor argued the plan ignored the industry trend toward variety (e.g., McDonald’s adding salads and wraps) to avoid the "veto vote."
      • Graves’ Counter-Intuition: He believed that executing one thing better than anyone else would create a "craveable" destination. He cited In-N-Out Burger (founded in 1948) as an eventual model for this focused approach.
  • Operational Efficiency: The limited menu allows for "scratch cooking" and high-speed service.
      • Speed Factors: Customers don’t need to study the menu; they often order by name (e.g., "Box Combo, no slaw, extra toast").
      • Consistency: By refusing to add items like spicy chicken or barbecue sauce, the kitchens maintain specialized, high-speed cooking procedures that "seconds matter" in the drive-thru.

Funding Through Grit and Manual Labor

  • Bootstrapping the Vision: After being rejected by every bank in town, Graves committed to funding the restaurant himself through grueling manual labor.
      • Boilermaker Work: He worked 90-hour weeks in California oil refineries (El Segundo and Torrance) to accumulate capital.
      • Alaskan Fishing: On the advice of a coworker, "Wild Bill" Tolar, Graves spent a summer in Naknek, Alaska, gillnetting sockeye salmon. He worked 20-hour days with only four hours of fragmented sleep to save the final $40,000–$60,000 needed.
  • The First Location: Graves opened the first Raising Cane’s at the north gates of LSU in a dilapidated building he renovated himself to save costs, learning plumbing and contracting on the job.

Ownership Strategy: Eschewing Franchises and Private Equity

  • Shift to Company-Owned: Graves originally planned for a 60/40 split between company-owned and franchised locations but eventually bought back his franchisees.
      • The Excellence Gap: He noted that while his company-owned stores operated at a 95/100 level, even good franchisees hovered around 85/100. This 10% difference in quality and culture drove him to move to a 100% company-operated model.
  • The "Death by a Thousand Cuts" Warning: Graves strongly advises founders against selling to private equity firms that prioritize three-to-five-year exit strategies.
      • Boardroom Erosion: He argues that PE-backed companies often make small, incremental cuts—like cheaper music systems or commissary-made sauce—that destroy crew morale and product quality over time.
      • Purpose-Driven Growth: Graves views his success as a platform to help his crew and community, a mission he believes is often lost when shareholders prioritize quarterly financial returns.

Marketing through "Caneiac" Culture

  • Top-of-Mind Awareness: Graves emphasizes the need to be visible the moment a customer asks, "What do we want for lunch?" This is achieved through heavy investment in billboards, radio, and timely TV placements.
  • Authentic Celebrity Partnerships: The brand utilizes "celebrity Caneiacs" who genuinely love the food, such as Snoop Dogg, Saquon Barkley, and Cynthia Erivo.
      • Humanizing the Brand: Marketing stunts, such as Snoop Dogg working a drive-thru window or hosting a fashion show during Fashion Week, keep the brand relevant and "cool" without taking itself too seriously.
  • Personal Leadership: Graves models himself after founders like Dave Thomas (Wendy’s), believing that a visible founder conveys a commitment to food safety and quality that a faceless corporation cannot.

Future Outlook: AI and Human Connection

  • AI for Prediction, Not Replacement: Raising Cane’s uses AI for data compilation and drive-thru speed optimization but Graves is hesitant to replace staff with robotics.
  • The Human Advantage: Graves believes that as competitors move toward fully robotic restaurants, Raising Cane’s "human touch"—friendly crew and handmade food—will become an even stronger competitive differentiator.

***

"The subconscious believes what you tell it... You can get up in the morning and say, 'Hey, I'm going to have a great day,' or you can get up and go, 'Oh my God, it's just going to be a tough day.' And whatever you say is pretty much right." — Todd Graves
"If you're sitting in a boardroom and the CFO says, 'Here's 15 things I can cut to make us more profitable'... it’s death by a thousand cuts. You implement it from a boardroom without being in the kitchens, and you lose the crew's attitude and the customer service." — Todd Graves
"If you try to be all things to all people, you're not serving any of them well." — Todd Graves

You get up in the morning and say, "Hey, I'm going to have a great day," or you can get up and go, "Oh my God, it's just going to be a tough day, right?" And whatever you say is pretty much right. This is Masters of Scale. I'm Jeff Berman, your host. This week on the show, Todd Graves. Todd is the founder and co-CEO of Raising Cane's Chicken Fingers. He blitzscaled his fast food concept into a brand worth billions, and is well-known both for its deliciously simple menu and its refreshingly friendly staff. We talk about how he perfected the recipe for success, why he thinks restaurants should be wary of private equity, and much, much more. It is a tasty conversation. Stay tuned. Todd, welcome to Masters of Scale. "Thank you for having me here." I'm thrilled to have you. I discovered Raising Cane's in, I want to say, 2022 after you opened in El Segundo in LA. And for my now 16-year-old and I, it had absolutely changed our chicken eating habits. So thank you. Fired up to talk to you and get into the story. Let's start at the start. Where did the inspiration for Raising Cane's come from? "Well, it really started as a kid. You know, I was always entrepreneurial. I was the kid that did the lemonade stands in the neighborhood, and then cut people's grass for five dollars, and just always thought around business. And when I started working, you know, high school years, I worked in the restaurant business. And I did that because I liked to cook with my mother. Like, food was, you know, for me, an expression of love. You know, spending time with my mother, cooking Cajun food, like let's say a gumbo, right? Or my grandmother made me a pie. And so I'm like, you know, it was my first job, go work in the restaurants. And I liked the pace. I liked the immediate gratification of cooking something and then serving it to somebody and them being happy, right? Working with teams, things like that. So for me, during college, you know, I was trying to consider what I was going to do. And that entrepreneurial streak just went back in me and I'm like, you know what? I want to open a restaurant. And I want to open it when I get out of college. I had no idea how hard that would be at that point, but that was the dream. It was just a natural thing for me to go into to do the food business. And when I was in college, and if you remember back in these days, this is '90, '94. Back then it was like ground meat was bad for you. Beef was bad and chicken and fish was good for you, but chicken became the number one protein. And I could see, and I grew up in Louisiana on fried chicken, right? But it was all bone-in fried chicken. During that time, while I was in college, people started eating the boneless chicken, whether it was chains like Chili's having that sauce, or it was going to mom and pops. And I was like, 'Man, this is gonna be the next thing.'" So, okay. So you're a college student in the '90s. You have this idea. Start working on a business plan back then? "I had a partner when I started the business and he had one more year of college and he enrolled in business classes, and we took a business planning class. I couldn't actually go to the class, but I actually wrote that business plan with him for that class. And man, it was good because I knew the business, right? I mean, it was a well-done business plan that he presented, but it ended up getting the worst grade in the class." Wait, why? What was the response? "And actually I was really glad it was the worst grade, because it motivated me. But the professor said, 'The plan's exceptional.' I mean, I knew details. I knew exactly, I knew what our aprons would cost to get washed, right? And so because I worked in the business. But he said, 'You know, you didn't follow the concept and understand the industry.' At that point, quick-service restaurants like McDonald's were adding menu items. They were worried about a veto vote. One person in the car doesn't want something from that restaurant, then they're going to go to another restaurant that has more variety. So they were adding variety and they were also adding healthy menu items. They were adding salads and wraps and things like that. So he said, 'Overall the plan was good, but you didn't hit with what the industry's doing.' So that's why I did it. And, you know, I was like, 'You know what? I'm gonna use that as motivation.' Because I knew that point, you go to a place because it's craveable food. And you're going to go there, and even though it has a hundred different menu items, you're going to get what you really like. And I'm like, being able to execute on one thing and do it better than anybody else is going to be a recipe for success. And it ended up being there. Look, In-N-Out Burger's done it since 1948. And I didn't have that as a model then, but I just had that intuitive sense that serving something craveable, people will come back for." We've had so many entrepreneurs on Masters of Scale who hear a hundred no's before they get their first yes. The first no sounds like it was a professor saying like, "This is the worst grade in the class for this reason." I appreciate the instinct was there, but to go launch a business takes... takes more than instinct. I mean, there's... there's a lot that goes into that. Where do you think that conviction came from, not just to believe it and feel it, but to follow through on it? "Yeah, so I was like one of these nerds in college. I was part of the entrepreneurial club and would... would read business magazines, you know, reading the paper. And what I saw was a lot of people that had really good ideas that they're passionate about never go and get it done because it's so hard to start a business, right? And so I was like, 'If I want to do this and this is my dream to go own my own restaurant, I gotta be committed, like to success or eventually failure, whatever it is, but never stop till I get it done.' Took that business plan to every bank in town. You know, bought a cheap suit and cheap briefcase and went in and presented the plan. And, you know, no after no after no. Nice enough, right? But then they also give me some advice like, 'Hey, why don't you... you don't have any money. Why don't you... you got a college degree, why don't you work in the industry for 10 years and then you can do that.' And with every 'no', you know, it's the best thing for an entrepreneur to be told, 'Hey, I don't think this is going to work,' because you just use that as fuel. You want to get a chip on your shoulder. You're like, 'I believe in this and I'm going to do this.' And, you know, to back that up, you know, when I couldn't get any money, I'm like, 'I'm going to go work and work as long as it takes for me to fund the restaurant myself.' And so I got a job through a friend of a friend working in... as a boilermaker, which is turnaround shift working in refineries. One in El Segundo, LA, and the other one in Torrance in LA. And that's when I came out to LA and worked hard. 90-hour weeks. California you get some double time in there. It's a great way to make money if you want to work hard. And met a guy there, interesting name, Wild Bill Tolar. Okay, Wild Bill. Everybody had nicknames there, and mine was Hollywood, which is a whole other story. But Wild Bill's like, 'Man, you're... you're not afraid of hard work and I know you have this chicken finger dream.' He's like, 'You should come with me, come up to Alaska and I'll introduce you, show you around, and you can work the Alaskan fishing trade catching sockeye salmon with gillnetting.' And he says, 'You'll get a job on the boat. You're determined.' He says, 'Actually you're going to make some real money.' He said, 'Now it's dangerous. The hours are incredibly hard, but you can make some good money.' So sure enough, caught a plane to Anchorage, float plane to King Salmon, hitchhiked to Naknek, and spent that summer commercial fishing in Alaska." So, a guy named Hollywood, nicknamed Hollywood, goes up to Alaska to start doing commercial fishing, which, to say it's dangerous and to say that the hours are tough and the working conditions are rough is an understatement. And that's 30 minutes here, an hour here. 20-hour days you're just like working hard, hard manual labor. The boats are filling up with fish and captains don't want to go to the tender boat and you might sink and boats are ramming each other, National Geographic helicopters going over, medical evac helicopters going. It was just insane deal. And I was just like, 'I'm doing this. I'm fueling my chicken finger dream. Nothing's going to stop me.' But that resolve, having that resolve is what kept me going and then eventually opening up." And you were telling everyone your why. You were telling, "I'm here because I'm going to make enough money to go open my restaurant." "That's right, that's right." Wow. And do you think that there's something psychological when you say it out loud that commits yourself to it? Is there a psychological insight there? "It absolutely is for me. And so being able to say, 'I'm going to open my restaurant. I'm going to do it. I'm going to... I'm not going to stop until I do. Nothing's going to ever stop me for this. I'll never quit. I'll never give up.' And when I tell myself that, and it's a human condition, right? The subconscious believes what you tell it, right? And the brain believes it. And that's where I could get that fuel to keep working hard and do it. And it still does today too, right? So, you know, you can choose your... you can choose your mood during the day. You get up in the morning and say, 'Hey, I'm going to have a great day,' or you can get up and go, 'Oh my God, it's just going to be a tough day, right?' And whatever you say is pretty much right." By the end of that Alaskan summer, how much had you saved up? "That point about maybe like 40 to 60 grand at that point, which was significant money and enough for me to get the project rolling. And also this point too is I was able to get a small group of preferred investors because they had seen, you know, it had been roughly two years to where when I opened it up, they're like, 'You know what? You are committed to this.' You know, little five thousand dollars, ten thousand dollars. Some of them boilermakers, some of them Alaskan fisher people, you know, my bookie who invested in cash. But it's just little five, ten thousand dollar increments. And then got a small SBA loan. And since I had that equity side of it, and I had to live off it too—I mean, because the money I'd made, I had to live off that. We were paying ourselves like five bucks an hour, whatever we were doing. And was able to open up. Secured a place in the north gates of LSU, right? This old dilapidated building. The broker was a great guy, Red Reynolds, and he said, 'I believe...' He actually like told the landlord, Tallulah Harper, she was like 96 years old, and said, 'Todd will... it was all kind of college concept.' It just was in a year, out a year. He's like, 'He will make a long-term thing and we can just wait for him.' So let's just wait for it. But going in, I wanted to be prudent, right? And I didn't have a ton of money going in and renovate this place. So I just learned what to do, right? Learned how to do plumbing. I learned how to do contracting work, things like that, and then bring in the experts when we really needed—you know, electrician, things I couldn't do—so figured that out. Same with going to the restaurant. I knew the restaurant business, but I'd never gone out to hire people, never done these things before so I'd learn it." Was there a piece of advice you got from someone who'd been there, done that before that was particularly instrumental in this phase of the process? "You know, not at that point because, to be honest with you, I had a lot of bad examples. So when, you know, working in restaurants in high school and college, the restaurant business was... it was more militant. It was 'do this, do that,' no music going in the kitchens. There wasn't a semblance of having fun or teamwork. It was, you know, it's the 'yes chef' type of a deal. And so I knew what I didn't want, you know what I mean? I knew I wanted people to wear casual uniforms, just Raising Cane's shirt and jeans, shorts, whatever. I wanted people to listen to music. I wanted them to have fun, but taking pride while they worked and did those things. So those types of things helped me on doing that. So like a lot of these restaurateurs were just... just not good people people. They weren't good culture people. So, but look, National Restaurant Association gave us a lot of good things on like actually the formats on how to do things, how to do templates for scheduling and things like that. And then I reached out to the restaurateurs I did respect in Baton Rouge and be like, 'Hey man, how are you getting good people? And what are some incentives you're doing?' My wife, who I started dating back then, was a McDonald's franchisee. So we spent a lot of our time just talking about, you know, 'How do you motivate the crew? How do you salt your fries?' things like that." Was there a point where there was an inflection where you said, "Oh, we've got this. We really have this right now"? "Luckily we made money from the first month, right? I saved thirty bucks, then we started doing well. And actually we had so much success that I opened my second location 18 months later. Got a full SBA loan, 18 months on that, and construction from the ground up on this building. And so I was always fortunate that we were making money and doing it. Iran-US War: Major Update | Hormuz War | Trump | Mojtaba Khamenei|N18G
