TL;DR

The Middle East is on the brink of total war as Israel eliminates top Hezbollah and Hamas leadership, prompting Iran to mobilize hypersonic missiles and reject diplomatic deals with the U.S. until blockades are lifted. Simultaneously, Russia’s "Victory Day" ceasefire has collapsed, with Ukraine deploying new long-range cruise missiles against Russian industrial targets.

Iran-US Tensions and Diplomatic Deadlock

  • Khamenei’s Hardline Stance: Supreme Leader Mojtaba Khamenei held a high-level meeting with President Masoud Pezeshkian, establishing that no deal will be reached with Donald Trump unless the naval blockade of Hormuz is lifted and attacks on Lebanon cease.
  • Retaliatory Threats: The IRGC (Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps) has warned that any "forceful" tactics by the U.S. will result in the direct targeting and destruction of American warships.
  • Drone Warfare: Iran has reportedly shot down a U.S. fighter drone in the Strait of Hormuz, signaling a shift in focus toward disrupting global maritime trade routes.
  • European Intervention: France and Britain have launched a joint mission, deploying the aircraft carrier *Charles de Gaulle* toward the Strait of Hormuz to protect oil interests and pressure Iran.

Israel’s Multi-Front Offensive

  • High-Value Targets Neutralized: Israeli Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu confirmed the elimination of Ahmed Ghalib Balut, Chief of Hezbollah's elite Radwan Force, along with senior commanders Mohammad Ali Bazi and Husain Hassan Romani.
  • Hamas Leadership Hit: In Gaza, Israeli strikes killed Azzam Khalil al-Hayya, the son of senior Hamas leader Khalil al-Hayya.
  • Netanyahu’s Strategic Autonomy: Despite diplomatic pressure from the U.S., Netanyahu stated that Israel will act independently to ensure Iran never achieves nuclear capability, specifically targeting Iranian uranium stockpiles.

Advanced Missile Technology and Defense

  • Iran’s Hypersonic Arsenal: Iran has placed Fattah-1 and Kheibar Shekan missiles on launch pads.
      • Fattah-1: A hypersonic missile with a 1,400km range capable of changing trajectory mid-flight to bypass radar.
      • Long-Range Threats: Kadr (2,000km) and Emad (1,700km) missiles are positioned to strike major Israeli cities like Tel Aviv and Haifa.
  • U.S. Defense Systems: The U.S. has deployed THAAD (Terminal High Altitude Area Defense) systems in Israel.
      • Capabilities: Each battery can intercept targets at an altitude of 150km, though concerns remain regarding their ability to stop 100% of a coordinated "saturation attack" by hundreds of Iranian missiles.

Russia-Ukraine: Failed Ceasefire and New Weapons

  • Victory Day Escalation: A proposed ceasefire for Russia’s May 9 "Victory Day" failed immediately. President Zelenskyy accused Russia of launching over 30 air strikes during the supposed truce, killing 27 people.
  • Ukraine’s "Flamingo" Missile: Ukraine has debuted the FP-5 Flamingo cruise missile, which reportedly has a 3,000km range—double that of the U.S. Tomahawk—targeting Russian navigation and aviation industries.
  • Moscow on High Alert: Russia claims to have intercepted 347 Ukrainian drones in a single night. Security in Moscow has been elevated to "war levels," including potential internet shutdowns, ahead of the annual military parade.
  • Force Numbers: Experts estimate Russia has 500,000 troops currently deployed in Ukraine, with a recruitment rate of 30,000 new soldiers per month to offset heavy casualties.

Notable Quotes

"Our target is to completely destroy all uranium held by Iran. President Trump may have a different view, but I have given clear instructions to our security agencies: Iran must not become a nuclear power at any cost."

— Benjamin Netanyahu, Prime Minister of Israel

"Talking about stopping the war for one day while killing our people before and after is not serious. We saw attacks in Kharkiv and Dnipro just as the ceasefire was announced. If we give them a 24-hour break, they will just start killing us again the next morning."

— Volodymyr Zelenskyy, President of Ukraine

"Trump has completely failed. His plans for a coup in Iran, or the seizure of Hormuz and our uranium, will never succeed. If he tries to use force, no one will be able to save his warships this time."

— General Jawani, IRGC Commander

ईरान और अमेरिका की डील फाइनल होने से पहले इज़रायल ने भयानक हमले शुरू कर दिए हैं। गाज़ा से लेबनान तक इज़रायल ने बमबारी की है। लेबनान में हिज़बुल्ला की रिज़वान फोर्स चीफ के साथ कई कमांडर इज़रायल ने ढेर कर दिए हैं। आपको बता दें कि इज़रायल के हमले से भड़के ईरान ने अमेरिकी ड्रोन को भी मार गिराया है। ईरान के सुप्रीम लीडर मुजतबा खामेनेई ने राष्ट्रपति के साथ तीन घंटे की मीटिंग की है और मीटिंग के बाद ट्रंप के होश उड़ाने वाला फैसला भी लिया है। रिपोर्ट आपको दिखाते हैं। खाड़ी युद्ध का कंट्रोल अब सिर्फ नेतन्याहू के हाथ में है। इज़रायल फैसला कर रहा है कि कहां पर बम फटेगा, कहां तबाही मचेगी, कहां लाशें बिछेंगी। सारे फ्रंट पर बारूदी तांडव शुरू हो चुका है, जिसके साथ अमेरिका की युद्ध से भागने की कोशिश कामयाब होती दिखाई नहीं दे रही। ईरान ने होर्मुज में उसके फाइटर ड्रोन का शिकार शुरू कर दिया है। फ्रांस भी ईरान युद्ध में अपना बैटल कैरियर ग्रुप लेकर आ धमका है। इज़रायल ने तो ट्रंप के रोकने के बाद भी गाज़ा और लेबनान को दहला दिया है। इज़रायल की बारूदी प्लानिंग ने ईरान को भड़का दिया है। ईरान ने होर्मुज से इज़रायल पर फोकस शिफ्ट करने का इशारा कर दिया है। अमेरिका और इज़रायल से नए राउंड के युद्ध से पहले ही ईरान के सुप्रीम लीडर मुस्तबा खामेनेई भी अब सीन में आ गए हैं। ईरान के राष्ट्रपति मसूद पजेश्कियान ने सुप्रीम लीडर मुस्तबा खामेनेई से ढाई घंटे की लंबी मुलाकात की। मीटिंग में मुस्तबा ने साफ कर दिया कि होर्मुज में ट्रंप की नाकेबंदी हटने तक कोई समझौता नहीं होगा। सुप्रीम लीडर ने ईरान को समझौते की टेबल पर अपनी सभी शर्तें मजबूती से रखने के निर्देश दिए हैं। जूनियर खामेनेई ने यह बात दोहराई है कि समझौते में लेबनान पर हमले बंद करने की शर्त भी शामिल है। मसूद पजेश्कियान, राष्ट्रपति, ईरान: नज़दीक दो घंटे, दो-सवा दो घंटे भी हम ख़िदमत में गुफ़्तगू में रहे। अव्वल तो मुझे ख़िदमत में कहना पड़ेगा, उनका नज़रिया, उनके गुफ़्तगू का अंदाज़ वाक़ई बड़ा हक़ीक़ी था, सादगी भरा। एक रहबर से आप ऐसे अंदाज़ की ही उम्मीद करते हैं, उनका बर्ताव वाक़ई शानदार रहा। यानी ईरान उन्हीं शर्तों पर डील करेगा जो मुस्तबा ने तय की हैं। ईरान रिवोल्यूशनरी लड़ाकों ने धमकी दी है कि ट्रंप ने अगर ज़ोर-ज़बरदस्ती की कोशिश की, तो अबकी बार उसके जंगी जहाज़ों को कोई बचा नहीं पाएगा। आईआरजीसी कमांडर जनरल जवानी ने कहा है कि ट्रंप पूरी तरह से फेल हो चुके हैं। जवानी ने धमकी दी है कि ईरान में तख्तापलट, ईरान का यूरेनियम और होर्मुज पर कब्ज़े के मंसूबे कभी कामयाब नहीं हो सकते। अमेरिका की तरफ से फिलहाल समझौते के सिवाय कोई बात नहीं हो रही है। लेकिन ट्रंप फोर्स वॉर जोन में रेडी है। ईरान पर प्रेशर बनाने के लिए फ्रांस का एयरक्राफ्ट कैरियर भी वॉर जोन में एंट्री ले रहा है। फ्रांस ने अपने कैरियर चार्ल्स डी गॉल को होर्मुज की तरफ भेज दिया है। कैरियर चार्ल्स डी गॉल मिस्र की स्वेज नहर से तेज़ी से होर्मुज की तरफ बढ़ता जा रहा है। फ्रांस के राष्ट्रपति इमैनुएल मैक्रों ने कहा कि एयरक्राफ्ट कैरियर लाल सागर की तरफ बढ़ता जा रहा है। इसके बाद उसे होर्मुज के करीब तैनात किया जाएगा। यह फ्रांस और ब्रिटेन का जॉइंट मिशन है, जिसमें फ्रांस और ब्रिटेन के जहाज़ शामिल हैं। ईरान ने फ्रांस के तेल के जहाज़ को निशाना बनाया, जिसके बाद से फ्रांस बेहद गुस्से में है। यानी ईरान अब यूरोपीय महाशक्तियों के टारगेट पर आ चुका है। लेकिन फिलहाल ईरान को इज़रायल की कोई काट नहीं मिल रही क्योंकि नेतन्याहू ने अमेरिका से अलग ट्रैक पर चलकर दुश्मनों को बेरहमी से कुचलना शुरू कर दिया है। बेरुत, नबातियेह, गाज़ा... लोकेशन अलग-अलग है लेकिन बारूदी तस्वीरें एक जैसी सामने आने लगी हैं... धमाके की, तबाही की, आग की, धुएं की। क्योंकि इज़रायल भयानक वॉर मोड में आ गया है। नेतन्याहू ने ऐलान कर दिया है कि ईरान और लेबनान में जब तक इज़रायल के दुश्मन हैं, तब तक युद्ध चलेगा। बेंजामिन नेतन्याहू, प्रधानमंत्री, इज़रायल: ईरान के पास जो भी यूरेनियम है, उसे पूरी तरह से खत्म करना हमारा टारगेट है। इसके लिए राष्ट्रपति ट्रंप का अलग नज़रिया हो सकता है, लेकिन हमने अपनी सेना और सुरक्षा एजेंसियों को साफ़ निर्देश दे दिए हैं कि किसी भी कीमत पर ईरान को परमाणु ताकत नहीं बनने देना है। गाज़ा और लेबनान को दहलाकर इज़रायल ने ईरान को फिर से उलझा दिया है। मुस्तबा की उलझन यह है कि वह ट्रंप से समझौता करते हैं, तो भी युद्ध रुकने की कोई गारंटी नहीं। अगर ईरान ट्रंप से समझौता नहीं करता, तो अमेरिका उसे धरती से मिटाने वाला हमला शुरू कर सकता है। ईरान ट्रंप से डील पर सीरियस है, बातचीत चल रही है। लेकिन जिस तरह इज़रायल ने युद्ध भड़का दिया है, उससे ईरान समझ नहीं पा रहा कि वह ट्रंप पर भरोसा करे या नहीं क्योंकि इज़रायल ने ट्रंप की सभी अपील धुआं-धुआं कर दी है। इज़रायल डिफेंस फोर्स ने बेरुत पर भयानक हमला करके हिज़बुल्ला के रिज़वान स्पेशल फोर्स के कमांडर अहमद गालिब बलूत का सफाया कर दिया है। बलूत के खात्मे का ऐलान खुद बेंजामिन नेतन्याहू ने किया है। बेंजामिन नेतन्याहू, प्रधानमंत्री, इज़रायल: रिज़वान फोर्स का कमांडर मारा गया है। वह उत्तर युद्ध जीतने का प्लान बना रहा था। उसे लगा था कि बेरुत के आतंकी मुख्यालय से वह हम पर हमले करता रहेगा। उसे लगा था कि बेरुत में उसे सुरक्षा मिली है, लेकिन कोई आतंकी सुरक्षित नहीं है। मैं अपनी सेना के वीर जवानों और खुफिया एजेंसियों को उनकी कामयाबी पर सलाम करता हूं। इज़रायल ने जानकारी दी है कि नासिर यूनिट के खुफिया विभाग के सरगना मोहम्मद अली बज़ी और हिज़बुल्ला के हवाई निगरानी कमांडर हुसैन हसन रोमानी को भी मार गिराया गया है। गाज़ा पर भीषण हमला करके इज़रायल ने हमास चीफ खलील अल-हय्या के बेटे अज़्ज़ाम खलील अल-हय्या को मार डाला है। मतलब यह कि इज़रायल सभी फ्रंट एक्टिव कर चुका है। सीज़फायर के बाद पहली बार बेरुत पर हमला करके नेतन्याहू ने सिग्नल दिए हैं कि अगला नंबर तेहरान पर धमाके का है। ईरान ने भी पूरी तैयारी के साथ इज़रायल से हिसाब बराबर करने की धमकी दी है। ईरान ने इज़रायल के लिए सबसे खतरनाक मिसाइलों को लॉन्च पैड पर तान दिया है। इसमें सबसे खतरनाक फत्ताह-1 और खैबर शिकन मिसाइल शामिल हैं। इसमें कद्र और इमाद मिसाइल भी शामिल हैं। फत्ताह हाइपरसोनिक मिसाइल की रेंज 1400 किलोमीटर है, खैबर शिकन मिसाइल की रेंज साढे 1400 किलोमीटर है। कद्र मिसाइल की रेंज 2000 किलोमीटर तक है और इमाद की रेंज 1700 किलोमीटर है। यह सभी मिसाइल इज़रायल के खिलाफ ऑपरेशन में दागी जा सकती हैं। तेल अवीव से लेकर हाइफा और अश्दोद से लेकर बीरशेबा और शेफाल जैसे छोटे शहरों को इन्हीं मिसाइलों से ईरान पहले दहला चुका है। इसीलिए ईरान के कमांडर जो वार्निंग दे रहे हैं, उसे इज़रायल बिल्कुल भी हल्के में नहीं ले सकता। ईरान के मिसाइल गोदाम लगातार एक्टिव हैं, उनके लॉन्च पैड लगातार रिलोड किए जा रहे हैं। इसमें सबसे ज्यादा खतरनाक है ईरान की फत्ताह मिसाइल। फत्ताह की खासियत है कि यह तेज़ रफ़्तार के साथ अपनी दिशा बदलती है, यानी एक ट्रेजेक्टरी में हमला नहीं करती। यह पृथ्वी के वायुमंडल के अंदर और बाहर सभी दिशाओं से गुज़र सकती है। और जब टारगेट पर हमला करती है, तो इसकी रफ़्तार इतनी ज्यादा होती है कि दुश्मन का इसे देखना भी मुश्किल है। यह बात नेतन्याहू बहुत अच्छी तरह से जानते हैं। ऐसे में इज़रायल में अगला धमाका कहां होगा, कौन सी मिसाइल कहां गिरेगी, इसे लेकर इज़रायल के मिसाइल ट्रैकिंग रडार सिस्टम काफी प्रेशर में हैं। इन सबसे खास है अमेरिका का थाड मिसाइल डिफेंस सिस्टम, जो पूरे इज़रायल को बचाने के लिए तैनात किया गया है। थाड 200 किलोमीटर की दूरी तक दुश्मन की किसी भी मिसाइल को मार सकता है। यह 150 किलोमीटर की ऊंचाई तक किसी भी मिसाइल को इंटरसेप्ट कर सकता है। अमेरिका के पास थाड की सात बैटरियां हैं। एक बैटरी में छह ट्रक माउंटेड लांचर होते हैं। दुश्मन की मिसाइल को रोकने के लिए थाड सिस्टम के एक लांचर से एक बार में आठ मिसाइल दागी जा सकती हैं। यानी पूरी बैटरी में कुल 48 इंटरसेप्टर मिसाइल होती है। इसे ऑपरेट करने के लिए 95 सैनिकों की ज़रूरत होती है। इज़रायल में ऐसे दो-दो सिस्टम तैनात हैं। लेकिन क्या यह काफी साबित हुए? बिल्कुल नहीं। हाइफा और तेल अवीव में फटी मिसाइलों ने बताया कि ईरान एक बार में सैकड़ों मिसाइल फायर करने की ताक़त रखता है और उनको रोकना न इज़रायल के बस की बात है, न ही अमेरिका इसमें 100% कामयाब रहा है। अगले 24 घंटे इज़रायल के लिए बहुत खतरनाक होने वाले हैं। अगर ट्रंप से ईरान की डील फाइनल नहीं हुई, तो ईरान लेबनान और गाज़ा का बदला इज़रायल की तबाही से ले सकता है क्योंकि रिज़वान फोर्स चीफ और हमास चीफ के बेटे की मौत ने अब सुप्रीम लीडर मुस्तबा को बहुत ज्यादा भड़का दिया है। रूस ने एक दिन के लिए 9 मई को सीज़फायर का एलान किया। लेकिन इस एलान के बाद रूस और यूक्रेन में भयानक युद्ध भड़क गया है। ज़ेलेंस्की ने दावा किया है कि एकतरफा सीज़फायर एलान के बावजूद रूस ने कीव समेत दूसरे इलाके में कई बड़े हमले किए हैं। अब रूस का यह भी दावा है कि उसने 300 से ज्यादा यूक्रेनी ड्रोन को इंटरसेप्ट किया है। रूस ने चेतावनी दी है कि 9 मई को विक्ट्री डे परेड के दिन अगर मॉस्को पर हमला हुआ तो कीव पर मिसाइलें गिरेंगी। पुतिन को कीव हर हाल में जीतना है। विक्ट्री डे से पहले कीव में मॉस्को का तिरंगा फहराना है। ईरान और अमेरिका के बीच युद्ध एक पल के लिए थम गया है, लेकिन रूस और यूक्रेन के बीच जंग डेढ़ हज़ार से ज्यादा दिनों से ऑन है। यह युद्ध अब सबसे खतरनाक मोड़ पर पहुंच गया है। रूस ने विक्ट्री डे के लिए सीज़फायर का एलान किया, लेकिन उसी बीच कीव में धमाके हुए, ड्रोन गिरे, मिसाइलें चलीं। रूस ने दावा किया कि उसने एक रात में 347 यूक्रेनी ड्रोन मार गिराए। यूक्रेन का दावा है कि उसने रूस के 90 से ज्यादा ड्रोन को इंटरसेप्ट किया। सवाल यह है कि रूस और यूक्रेन के बीच सीज़फायर का एलान क्या सिर्फ दिखावा था? क्या पुतिन 9 मई को विक्ट्री परेड से पहले यूक्रेन पर जीत को पक्का करने वाला हमला चाहते हैं? टूटा सीज़फायर, अब कीव पर फाइनल अटैक। पुतिन का ऑर्डर, कीव को कुचलो। वोलोदिमीर ज़ेलेंस्की, राष्ट्रपति, यूक्रेन: एक दिन के लिए युद्ध रोकने की बात करना लेकिन उससे पहले हमारे लोगों को मारना यह बिल्कुल ग़लत है। कल खारकीव के मरेफा में हमला हुआ, कल नीप्रो में। लोग मारे गए, कई घायल हुए, बच्चे और बड़े सब। इतना सब होने के बाद अगर कोई कहे कि परेड से एक दिन पहले मिसाइल और ड्रोन हमले रोक देते हैं और फिर 24 घंटे का युद्धविराम कर लेते हैं तो यह कोई गंभीर बात नहीं लगती। सीज़फायर या छलावा? क्या मॉस्को ने शुरू कर दिया है निर्णायक युद्ध? मॉस्को में सुरक्षा बढ़ा दी गई है, एयर डिफेंस एक्टिव है क्योंकि यूक्रेनी ड्रोन रूस के अंदर तक पहुंच चुके हैं। यूक्रेन रूस से युद्ध करने के लिए अब प्राइवेट मिलिट्री कंपनी बनाने की तैयारी कर रहा है। यह कंपनी सर्विस के बदले पैसे वाले मॉडल पर काम करेगी। ज़ेलेंस्की का इरादा ऐसे सैनिक तैयार करने का है जो पेशेवर, हाई-टेक और ताक़तवर हों। रूस के वैगनर ग्रुप की तर्ज़ पर वह ऐसे लड़ाकों की फौज तैयार करना चाहते हैं जो कॉन्ट्रैक्ट किलिंग मशीन की तरह काम करें। दूसरी तरफ रूस का दावा है कि उसने सैकड़ों ड्रोन मार गिराए हैं, यानी अब युद्ध सिर्फ बॉर्डर पर नहीं बल्कि कीव बनाम मॉस्को तक पहुंच चुका है। तो क्या विक्ट्री डे से पहले ही पुतिन दुनिया को एक बड़ी सैन्य जीत दिखाना चाहते हैं? या फिर रूस-यूक्रेन युद्ध पहले से और ज़्यादा ख़तरनाक होने वाला है? वोलोदिमीर ज़ेलेंस्की, राष्ट्रपति, यूक्रेन: मैं उन सभी देशों और नेताओं का धन्यवाद करता हूं जिन्होंने यूक्रेन के पूरे युद्धविराम के प्रस्ताव का समर्थन किया। रूस ने इसका जवाब सिर्फ नए हमले तेज़ करके दिया। पूरे दिन, लगभग हर घंटे यूक्रेन के अलग-अलग इलाकों से हमलों की ख़बरें आती रहीं। मॉस्को की सड़कों पर रूस के विक्ट्री डे परेड की तैयारी चल रही है। लेकिन आर्म्ड व्हीकल, मिसाइल लॉन्चर और आर्टिलरी सिस्टम वाले ट्रक क्या रूस की सीमा को पार करके कीव तक पहुंच जाएंगे? क्या विक्ट्री डे से पहले मॉस्को ब्रिगेड कीव पर सबसे बड़ा हमला करेगी? 9 मई रूस का विक्ट्री डे, जब मॉस्को जर्मनी पर जीत का जश्न मनाता है। रेड स्क्वायर पर टैंक निकलते हैं, मिसाइलें दिखाई जाती हैं। पूरी दुनिया को रूसी ताक़त का संदेश दिया जाता है। लेकिन इस बार विक्ट्री डे से पहले मॉस्को खुद डरा हुआ दिखाई दे रहा है। यह मॉस्को के डर के पीछे पुतिन का वार प्लान है। मॉस्को ने दावा किया कि यूक्रेन के ड्रोन रूस के अंदर तक पहुंच रहे हैं, जिसके बाद मॉस्को के आसपास एयर डिफेंस बढ़ा दिया गया है। रिपोर्ट में दावा किया गया है कि इंटरनेट तक बंद करने की तैयारी की गई है और परेड की सुरक्षा युद्ध स्तर तक पहुंचा दी गई है। सवाल यह है कि क्या पुतिन विक्ट्री डे से पहले एक बड़ी सैन्य जीत दिखाना चाहते हैं? हालाँकि रूस ने कहा है कि वे सीज़फायर चाहते हैं, लेकिन यूक्रेन ने आरोप लगाया कि सीज़फायर एलान के बावजूद रूस ने ड्रोन और मिसाइल हमले जारी रखे हैं। हमले की आवाज़ कीव, सूमी, नीप्रो और ज़ापोरिझिया हर तरफ सुनाई दे रही है। तो सवाल उठता है कि क्या यह सीज़फायर था या सिर्फ दुनिया को दिखाने के लिए एक राजनीतिक ड्रामा। रिपोर्ट में दावा किया गया कि 24 घंटे में रूस की सेना ने 30 से ज्यादा घातक हमलों को अंजाम दिया। रात से सुबह तक 20 से ज्यादा एयर स्ट्राइक की गई। रूस की सेना ने यूक्रेन पर मिसाइल हमले, ड्रोन अटैक किए। यूक्रेन ने दावा किया कि उसने 90 से ज्यादा ड्रोन को हवा में ही मार गिराया। जबकि इन हमलों के 24 घंटे पहले ही 9 मई को रूस ने विक्ट्री डे के लिए सीज़फायर का एलान किया था। रूस के हमलों से यूक्रेन के शहर फिर खंडहर की तरह दिखने लगे हैं। रिपोर्ट में दावा किया गया कि रूस के सिर्फ ड्रोन अटैक में 27 से ज्यादा लोग मारे गए, जबकि 100 से ज्यादा लोग घायल हुए हैं। यूक्रेन ने इसे इस साल का सबसे घातक हमला बताया। रूस के हमलों के बाद ज़ेलेंस्की ने सोशल मीडिया पर FP-5 फ्लेमिंगो क्रूज़ मिसाइल लॉन्च का वीडियो शेयर किया, जिसमें दावा किया गया कि 1500 किलोमीटर दूर रूस के शहर चेबोक्सारी को भी निशाना बनाया गया। ज़ेलेंस्की ने पलटवार करते हुए कहा कि यह रूस के हमले का जवाब है। यूक्रेन का दावा है कि उसने इस मिसाइल से रूस के उस ठिकाने को तबाह किया जहाँ से रूस की नेवी और उसके मिसाइल और एविएशन इंडस्ट्री को नेविगेशन कंपोनेंट सप्लाई होती है। हालाँकि इसमें मिसाइल लॉन्च की जगह का ज़िक्र नहीं किया गया। FP-5 फ्लेमिंगो यूक्रेन की खुद की बनाई मिसाइल है जिसे कीव की फायर पॉइंट कंपनी ने बनाया है। इसे अगस्त 2025 में सार्वजनिक रूप से पेश किया गया था। यह टॉमहॉक से दोगुनी रेंज वाली मिसाइल है। इसकी रेंज करीब 3000 किलोमीटर है। इसमें 1150 किलोग्राम वॉरहेड फिट हो सकता है। रफ़्तार 900 किलोमीटर प्रति घंटा है। इसका GPS गाइडेड सिस्टम इसे 14 मीटर के अंदर सटीक मार करने की क्षमता देता है। यूक्रेन की फ्लेमिंगो मिसाइल रूस के यूरोपीय हिस्से के लगभग पूरे इलाके को कवर करता है। रूस के पास फिलहाल मैक्सिमम 2500 किलोमीटर रेंज की ही मिसाइल है, लेकिन मॉस्को की मिसाइलों का वज़न फ्लेमिंगो की तुलना में ढाई गुना भारी है। मतलब फ्लेमिंगो से ज्यादा तबाही मचा सकती है। रूस के पास दुनिया में सबसे ज्यादा परमाणु बम हैं। वोलोदिमीर ज़ेलेंस्की, राष्ट्रपति, यूक्रेन: उन्हें किसी तरह की छुट्टी मनाने का मौका क्यों दें? हमारे लिए तो कोई छुट्टी नहीं है। हमें तो रूस के हमलों की वजह से हर समय दुख और परेशानी झेलनी पड़ रही है। अगर हम उन्हें एक दिन की राहत दे भी दें, तो 9 तारीख के बाद अगली सुबह वो फिर हमें मारना शुरू कर देंगे। इसलिए अब देखना होगा आगे क्या होता है। अब सबसे बड़ा सवाल यह है, क्या पुतिन ने कीव पर दबाव बढ़ाने का ऑर्डर दिया है? पिछले कुछ दिनों में रूस के हमले अचानक तेज़ हुए हैं। पूर्वी यूक्रेन में रूसी सेना लगातार आगे बढ़ने की कोशिश कर रही है। डोनेत्स्क और खारकीव इलाकों पर अब रूस का फोकस बढ़ता जा रहा है। एक्सपर्ट्स का मानना है कि विक्ट्री डे से पहले रूस किसी बड़े शहर पर कब्ज़ा कर लेता है तो पुतिन उसे राजनीतिक जीत की तरह पेश करेंगे। दावा है कि रूस के पास अभी भी 10 लाख से ज्यादा एक्टिव सैनिक हैं, जिसमें से करीब 5 लाख सैनिकों की पूरी तैनाती यूक्रेन में है। जंग में उसके करीब 12 लाख सैनिक या तो घायल हुए या मारे गए, लेकिन इसकी भरपाई के लिए वह हर महीने 30 हज़ार से ज्यादा सैनिकों की भर्ती कर रहा है। 2026 में सेना में आने वालों की कमी हुई है इसीलिए रूस पर आरोप लग रहा है कि वह जबरन सैनिकों की भर्ती बढ़ा रहा है। लेकिन इस जंग में यूक्रेन भी पूरी तरह टूट चुका है। उसके बिजली के ढांचे को बर्बाद कर दिया गया है, शहर के शहर खंडहर हो चुके हैं, लाखों की संख्या में लोग माइग्रेट हो गए हैं। बावजूद इसके ज़ेलेंस्की झुकने को तैयार नहीं हैं। ज़ेलेंस्की की सिर्फ एक ही डिमांड है, यूक्रेन का सिर्फ एक ही संदेश है... सीज़फायर नहीं, रक्षा चाहिए। सिर्फ 24 घंटे के युद्ध में यूक्रेन के शहरों का हाल कुछ इस तरह से है, हर तरफ हाहाकार मचा हुआ है, इमारतें तबाह हैं, गाड़ियाँ जल रही हैं। रेस्क्यू टीम हर मिनट किसी न किसी की जान बचाने में लगी है। इस पूरे युद्ध में अब सबसे खतरनाक हथियार बन चुके हैं ड्रोन। सस्ते, तेज़ और घातक ड्रोन। रूस ईरानी डिज़ाइन वाले ड्रोन इस्तेमाल कर रहा है, यूक्रेन वेस्टर्न टेक्नोलॉजी वाले ड्रोन से तबाही मचा रहा है। यानी यह सिर्फ रूस-यूक्रेन युद्ध नहीं बल्कि हथियारों की नई लैब बन चुका है जहाँ दुनिया की सबसे बड़ी ताकतें अपनी टेक्नोलॉजी टेस्ट कर रही हैं। सीज़फायर टूटा या तोड़ा गया, यह बहस चलती रहेगी, लेकिन इतना तय है रूस-यूक्रेन युद्ध अब सिर्फ ज़मीन की लड़ाई नहीं रहा, यह सम्मान और ईगो की लड़ाई बनता जा रहा है। उस संदेश की लड़ाई है जो मॉस्को दुनिया को दिखाना चाहता है। सवाल बस इतना है विक्ट्री डे पर दुनिया जीत का जश्न देखेगी या एक और भीषण युद्ध की शुरुआत।

Marc Lore's Blueprint For Scaling Billion-Dollar Startups

Forbes(2.1M) ·27:12 ·127 views · 2026-05-08
Marc Lore (Founder, Chairman, and CEO, Wonder Group) Moira Forbes (Host, Forbes)
Marc Lore is leveraging vertical integration and "physical AI" at Wonder to disrupt the trillion-dollar food industry, utilizing a math-driven scaling framework (VCP) to transition from software-only models to capital-intensive physical infrastructure that creates a defensible market moat.
TL;DR Marc Lore is leveraging vertical integration and "physical AI" at Wonder to disrupt the trillion-dollar food industry, utilizing a math-driven scaling framework (VCP) to transition from software-only models to capital-intensive physical infrastructure that creates a...

TL;DR

Marc Lore is leveraging vertical integration and "physical AI" at Wonder to disrupt the trillion-dollar food industry, utilizing a math-driven scaling framework (VCP) to transition from software-only models to capital-intensive physical infrastructure that creates a defensible market moat.

The Wonder Thesis: "Cloud Kitchens" for the Physical World

  • Vertical Integration: Lore argues that food delivery aggregators (e.g., DoorDash, Uber Eats) hit a ceiling because they don't own the production. Wonder owns the restaurants, the cooking process, and the delivery to ensure quality and timing.
  • Kitchen as Software: Wonder is "decoupling the kitchen from the restaurant," similar to how the cloud revolutionized software.
      • Wonder Create: A platform launching late 2024 allowing individuals to launch and scale a digital restaurant across Wonder’s infrastructure for $10/month.
  • Operational Density: A single 2,500-square-foot Wonder kitchen can house 25 different restaurant brands across 20 cuisines (from Bobby Flay steaks to Jose Andres tapas) using electric-only equipment and no open flames.
  • Advanced Logistics: By owning the tech stack, Wonder sequences cooking so that a multi-restaurant family order finishes simultaneously and is timed perfectly with the courier, resulting in 5-minute average delivery times in urban areas.

The High-Stakes Pivot: From Vans to Brick-and-Mortar

  • The "Cook-en-Route" Experiment: Wonder initially utilized 450 Mercedes Sprinter vans to cook food outside customer homes. While customers loved the quality, the model was too capital-intensive and difficult to scale.
  • The Hard Reset: In early 2023, Lore pivoted the entire company to a brick-and-mortar "hub" model after seeing just three weeks of superior data from a test location.
      • Financial Impact: He took a $100M loss and drove revenue to zero to restart with the more scalable model.
      • Current Trajectory: Wonder has opened 120 locations in three years; Lore aims to reach 1,000 locations within the next 3.5 years.

The VCP Framework: Vision, Capital, People

Lore manages his ventures through a proprietary "VCP" system to ensure foundational stability during hyper-growth:

  • Vision: Writing down a 10–15 year vision and building an organizational structure with zero dependencies. Lore hires top-tier leaders and empowers them to run their departments without needing cross-functional approval, which he defines as "true focus."
  • Capital Strategy: Lore uses a "magic number" (66.66) to determine funding needs based on target valuation.
      • The Formula: Divide the target exit valuation by 66.66 to find the necessary seed round. (e.g., To build a $10B company, the seed should be $150M).
      • The 18-Month Rule: The goal is to double the company's valuation and the amount of capital raised every 18 months.
  • People & Performance: Lore emphasizes that investors often over-focus on numbers while ignoring the "foundational systems" of culture and compensation that allow an organization to pivot when things go wrong.

Automation and "Physical AI" as the New Moat

  • Disrupting Labor Costs: While e-commerce fights for basis points of margin, restaurants have 30% labor costs ripe for automation.
  • Robotics Integration: Wonder acquired Spice Robotics (an automated bowl-making machine) from Sweetgreen to automate high-volume concepts.
  • Infrastructure as a Moat: Lore believes capital-intensive businesses are now more attractive than pure software companies because physical infrastructure and proprietary robotics are harder for AI to disintermediate.
  • IPO Goals: Lore plans to take Wonder public in approximately 11 months to gain access to public currency for further brand acquisitions.

Notable Quotes

"We’re doing what the cloud did to software. We’re decoupling the kitchen from the restaurant so that we could scale restaurants like software." — Marc Lore
"I think now with AI... software's become a bad word in the venture community. And here we are... being respected for building a real moat with physical infrastructure and physical AI." — Marc Lore
"Focus is a little bit misunderstood... A lot of it comes down to organizational structure—having the right level person on whatever it is that they're focused on [with no dependencies]." — Marc Lore
"If you have the best people, real clarity around vision, and the right organizational structure that allows people to run and be empowered... magical things will happen." — Marc Lore
I want to know what is the ability of that company to hire the very best people in the world. So you have to be able to identify great talent, you need to convince them to come to the organization, and then you have to get the very best that they have to give. And getting all three of those right is hard. And organizations that have figured that out are the ones that are going to ultimately win. Mark, thanks so much for sitting down with us today. You've built companies across a number of different areas or eras in business: e-commerce in the early 2000s when nobody believed things like diapers could sell online; the marketplace wars of the 2010s with Jet; and now Wonder in this really AI-saturated and capital-tight 2026. From the inside, what feels the same across these eras and what's changed as you're now leaning into Wonder? Yeah, I mean in all three startups I've always, you know, picked capital-intensive businesses that are, I think, able to give us a real moat around the business. I think in the earlier days, the first two startups, that that was a bad thing to actually have a lot of capital required to get to scale. But I think Amazon sort of laid the path for others to follow. And I think now with AI disintermediated software companies, software's become a bad word in the venture community. And here we are sitting here finally where we're being respected for building a real moat with physical infrastructure and physical AI. So it's kind of an exciting time because after 25 years of capital being a headwind, now it seems to be a tailwind. You've called food the last trillion-dollar category without an Amazon. And people have been trying to dominate this category—food delivery—for 15 or so years: DoorDash, Uber Eats, Grubhub and the like. And most often we've seen them scale either into mediocrity or oftentimes hitting a ceiling. Walk us through the actual thesis of Wonder. What have the companies that have tried to tackle the food and restaurant space missed that you're now trying to fulfill on? Yeah, I mean I'm just looking at the customer value proposition as a user of food delivery aggregators. And you know, the customer service, the long delivery times, the high fees and things, and just felt like it was ripe for vertical integration—like not just doing the delivery but also owning the restaurants, doing the cooking, and being able to stitch the cooking and the delivery together in a sequenced way that elevates the customer experience. That was the original thinking. I think over the last couple of years with advancements in AI and robotics, I think we're thinking much bigger now. We're thinking about completely reimagining what it even means to be a kitchen. And at Wonder, we're doing what the cloud did to software. We're decoupling the kitchen from the restaurant so that we could scale restaurants like software. And in fact, we're at the end of the year we're launching something called Wonder Create, which will allow any individual in the world to build a restaurant on the Wonder platform for ten bucks a month and instantaneously scale it across all locations utilizing Wonder's back-end supply chain management, ingredient sourcing, cooking of the food, delivering of the food. So all you need to do is market your restaurant. So we see a future where there are literally going to be millions, if not tens of millions, of restaurant owners that own restaurants only in the digital world, and they're there to market it, and it would all exist on the Wonder infrastructure. How complicated has it been for you creating that vertical infrastructure in this category? Because it seems like Wonder is your biggest bet to date in a lot of different dimensions. Walk us through the complexities and all the different pieces you've had to put together to be able to fulfill this value proposition. Yeah, it has taken seven years and over two billion dollars of capital, so it was much harder I would say than we probably originally thought. It's that entrepreneur naivety that I love—makes you dump into something that people say is impossible. I think the hardest part was, we have now in a single 2500 square foot kitchen we've got 25 different restaurants across 20 different cuisines. So everything from a high-end steakhouse Bobby Flay steak to Jose Andres' Spanish tapas to burgers, barbecue, Chinese, pizza, Italian, Thai, Middle Eastern—just about every cuisine you could imagine all being cooked in a systematic way in 2500 square feet with no gas, no open flames, only electric cooking equipment with very lightly trained labor. And then increasingly more we're adding robotics into the back end. We've completely automated the expo area, which is the toughest part in a restaurant. We just bought Spice Robotics, which is the infinite bowl-making machine; we bought that from Sweetgreen. So in the very near future all the bowl concepts will be completely automated without labor. So yeah, I think that has been the toughest part. I think, you know, acquiring Grubhub Seamless for the delivery piece of it was also critical. And we built all our own tech that stitches it together in between. So we can sequence the cooking. If you order—if you're a family of four and order from four different restaurants—all the food finishes cooking at the same time. But more importantly, it's timed with the courier, so the food doesn't sit in the kitchen and the courier doesn't wait. And it's all integrated and we set a really tight delivery radius so we're able to get it to you in the city—our average delivery time is five minutes—and in the suburbs it's just over 10 minutes. So it's just a faster, more on-time, hotter multi-restaurant order experience that we've been able to create. Many many different pieces that go into it but seamless on the other side as the consumer when they get to experience these. You talked about Wonder being much harder in a lot of different ways than you expected. Can you walk us through a couple of those elements that proved to be more complicated or really also maybe even helped you see the opportunity of this business given that complexity? Yeah, I think the first thing that was super interesting to me was just how much higher the margins are in restaurants than they are in e-commerce. And e-commerce we've got a ton of automation because you're fighting for basis points. And I think in the restaurant world there's so much margin that there really hasn't been this focus on needing to automate. But that really, when you think about it, is ripe for automation because there's 30 points of labor, you know, in every kitchen plus or minus. And so it's really that's where the automation should be focused and it hasn't to date. So I think that that is a really really big opportunity. You have mentioned that early on with Wonder, for example, you had a different business model. A couple of times in your businesses you've had to ultimately pivot. Can you walk us through this evolution in terms of the early concept and where you really saw the opportunity moving forward today? Yeah, so we started with this idea—vertical integration, same vision—but instead of the traditional cook and then deliver the food, we thought what if we deliver then cook? And so we basically built these kitchens in the back of Mercedes sprinter vans and it would drive to your house and then cook the food in six to ten minutes outside your door and deliver it. And we had it down where the driver could cook so there was no extra labor. So you really weren't wasting any time because you have to drive anyway and you have to cook anyway, and the same person was doing it. So the customers loved it. It worked really well. We had 450 trucks on the road. We had an insane Net Promoter Score. But it was very capital intensive. It only worked in the suburban areas and it was definitely harder to scale the labor model. And so some complexities there. At the same time, we had tested a brick-and-mortar and we said what if we—each truck had a restaurant on it and we had now, you know, 25 different restaurants—and we thought what if we take all 25 restaurants and put it in a brick-and-mortar and set a tight delivery radius? Could we sort of replicate the quality? If so, this would work in urban, suburban, exurban, rural. The TAM was much bigger, much less capital, more efficient labor model. So we opened a brick-and-mortar location at the end of 2022. After three weeks of data, I said, "This is it. This is it. That's the future." And you know, we didn't really have the resources to run both models at the same time so we had to make a decision. And so we pulled 450 trucks off the road, sold it, took an 80 million, 100 million dollar loss, took revenue to zero and started over in early '23. And then in the last three years we opened, you know, 120 locations, which is fast to our knowledge ever to do that. And in the next three and a half years we'll have a thousand. So we're just running really fast trying to make up lost ground. It's—I don't know if you've ever missed a turn when you're driving and you miss an exit—you wind up driving faster to get back because you feel like you gotta make up lost time. That's how I've felt the last three years. Like we lost a couple of years and so like we gotta go, you know. We don't have time to do it the way we would have done it if we had unlimited time. When you were first looking at this opportunity and this idea, did you feel at that moment in time just how ambitious it was? I mean with every startup I'm always thinking bigger. You know with Jet, thinking bigger than diapers. I think with diapers.com, you know, I was thinking "Wow, this could be a billion dollar business," right? This was the first real one. And we got off the train at 550 million. And then with Jet, I was like "Okay, I think this actually could be a 10 billion dollar business." And 26 months after we started we got off at 3 billion, right? So in the same order of magnitude but just earlier than the goal. And then I think with Wonder, you know, started and said "I think this could be a 100 billion dollar business." And so but we're not going to get off the train. We're going to take this thing public. And what's interesting—and this hasn't happened before—I think now with the new model and the advancements in AI and robotics, I do think this has the potential now to be a trillion dollar market cap company, which is super exciting as a founder. It's a founder's dream to have that size TAM and market opportunity and be a disruptor. So I'm all in on this. I've dedicated my life to making this happen at this point. You've talked about this IPO for Wonder as would be the biggest entrepreneurial achievement of your career today. What does an IPO represent to you at this moment in time that your other exits didn't? Yeah, I think one is raising access to the capital markets. So this does require billions of dollars of more capital. So access to the public market—we'll be ready to go public in like 11 months, so that's exciting. And also having a public currency to be able to make acquisitions. So a big critical part of our strategy is to acquire the best restaurant brands in the country and buy them and then be able to arb that acquisition by blowing it up on the platform without having to add any new capital or open up any new locations. So very excited about going public for those two reasons. I'd be curious, how has this venture stretched you as an entrepreneur or built different muscles that you may not have expected having already built such strong entrepreneurial muscles in your previous ventures? Yeah, I mean I think the rate at which we're growing is unprecedented in my career—like I haven't done that before. I think the pace of acquisitions as well and some big ones, you know buying Grubhub for example, so that's all new. I think the level of executives that we brought in is a whole other level than anything I've dealt before. So that's also new, you know. Yeah, I think I feel like I'm just learning every day and becoming a better entrepreneur. A lot of the VCP—the Vision Capital People systems that we built at diapers, I've watched it evolve into Jet and then into Wonder. And those systems continue to evolve, you know, the performance management system, compensation systems, and how we think about org structure. And it's just fun to keep learning and evolving every day. When you look at scaling these businesses so quickly and you look at the different phases and what you love, what phase of Wonder are you in right now in a way that sort of really brings out that same entrepreneurial grit that you've had for your entire life? Yeah, I think we're at a really good spot now because I think the last three years like I said, once we went brick-and-mortar, we're trying to put all the pieces together very quickly—buying a delivery network, you know, building the units, buying the restaurants, building restaurants—like we're doing a lot of foundational work. And I feel like we're at a stage now where, and then with the acquisition of the robotics company, I think we're really crystal clear on what the sort of 2040 vision is. And we have all the pieces in place to go execute against that vision. And it's really a matter of like continuing to scale, continuing to keep the culture strong because it's all about execution at this point. And making sure that the food quality continues to improve as we scale, which it has been, which is probably the most exciting and most focused part of where my brain is, which is food quality getting better as we scale. You know, when you talk about this business, particularly with the vertical integration and just the complexity of it, it's really significant and meaningful. When you work with your teams and you're building out your strategy, vision, capital allocation and the like, how do you really exercise the art of focus? How do you ensure that you're continuing to align all the different efforts around the ultimate goals of the business when you do have different complexities, even acquisitions and the like, that may not have existed in your previous businesses? Yeah, I think focus is a little bit misunderstood, you know. I think people look at the number of things you're doing and the more things you're doing, the it's—you know—a lay person would say that's lacking focus. But I think when you look under the hood you have to: one, see how the pieces fit together, you know, in a really compelling strategy and all tied to the vision. I think that's most important. But equally probably as important is building the organizational structure so that you have no dependencies from one piece to another. So you hire a great person that focuses on a particular area and there's no dependencies on any other area, then that person is as focused as they could possibly be. You have other organizations that maybe only doing a few things and they have a upside-down org structure where there's dependencies left, right, and center and there's lacking focus because no one's accountable and no one has the ability to execute without having to rely on somebody else. And so I think a lot of it comes down to organizational structure—having the right level person on whatever it is that they're focused on. You talk about the organizational structure, but what are the things that you think are most underestimated when it comes to what it's taken to build Wonder, what it's taken to get to where you are today to really see the big bold vision that you've set out? What do people not understand has been as complicated that really also is the real value add and the real advantage that you're creating in this category? Yeah, I mean just I think with any business and Wonder's no different, it will always come down to the quality of the people in the organization. And so when I look at a company and judge whether a company's going to be successful, I want to know what is the ability of that company to hire the very best people in the world to do what they need to do. And so it starts with, you know, building a culture that is very attractive, having the vision to sell the individuals into the organization, and then having the right performance management system, compensation systems—the foundational systems—to get the best that they have to give. So you have to be able to identify great talent, you need to convince them to come to the organization and then you have to get the very best that they have to give. And getting all three of those right is hard. And organizations that have figured that out are the ones that are going to ultimately win. And I think people miss that. Like investors certainly miss that. I don't get questions at all about the actual infrastructure required and our ability to attract, retain, get the best out of the best people. Because if you have the best people and you have real clarity around the vision and strategy and you have the right organizational structure that allows people to run and be empowered without having dependencies, then magical things will happen. Why do you think investors don't spend time on that? And obviously you want to hear business and strategy but at the end of the day it's all about people to lead the execution and to build those things. Yeah, I mean I think some of it is just I've just learned human nature. I mean some investors do get it and they do focus on it; most don't. I think it's just human nature to want to look at the numbers. Like "Show me the numbers. I want to—I'm a smart person—I want to independently look at those numbers and I want to make a decision." But if you looked back when we had trucks on the road and you looked at the numbers, well—I don't know—a year later, three years later, it doesn't look anything like it did back then. And so you were looking at the numbers—fine—but you weren't looking at the infrastructure and the organization's ability to recognize that something's not working and to be able to pivot. And in order to be able to pivot and find the opportunity, you need the right people and you need the right structure. So more important than anything I think is people and structure and then your ability to raise capital. That's the other thing. So you could have great people, great vision, clarity, structure and everything but if you can't raise capital then that's also an issue. And so I would also look at, you know, the company's ability to raise capital, their history of raising capital. So... When you talk about capital obviously that's sort of the fuel to be able to grow and scale a business. You have a lot of hard-won wisdom and insights around what founders need to do to raise capital today, to really be able to look at what amount is needed and the dollar figures they actually should be going to raise toward. Can you walk us through that strategy and where you see some lack of alignment today in terms of where and how people are going out to raise money in this current market? Yeah, I mean first I always in every startup had a what I call capital plan. So from the very first day when you start the company, try to understand how much money you want to raise with each round, what you want the valuation to be, how much dilution, you know, ultimately is from each round that you're taking and kind of map that out day one. It all starts from the level of ambition. Because your very first seed round needs to be tied to the level of ambition. And so I have a very simple thing: you know, figure out what size company you ultimately want to create, divide by 66.66—that's the magic number. That tells you basically what the seed round should be. So if you want to create a hundred million dollar company and you divide by 66.66, the seed round's 1.5 million. Billion, it's 15 million. Hundred billion—oh sorry, 10 billion—then it's 150 million, right? And so it's actually and then you start over with that seed round. Let's say 20% dilution on the first round. The goal and the rule of thumb is to try and double the valuation and double the amount raised every 18 months. So that's kind of the way I plan it out. So if you start—you want to create a hundred million dollar business—you raise one and a half million at a four and a half pre, six post, you know that's 25% dilution. And then you say "Okay, now in the next 18 months I want to raise 3 million at a 12 pre" because that was a six post. And you make it in bite-sized chunks, right? And then after you raise that round, then you want to double again and you say "Okay now I want to raise, you know, 6 million at double the valuation and then 12 at double the valuation and 25 at double the valuation." And after six rounds of financing you basically have yourself your hundred million dollar business. And so you just break it down into these chunks. And what it does is when you're building your budget, you sort of know how much capital you have in between raises and you know that you have to double the valuation. And so you just work backwards. And that's the way I've always done it. And at Diapers and Jet it wasn't perfect but every round, you know, every investor made 100% compounded annual return because that's the way it was structured. It was to double the valuation each round. And so if you want—there we did it in sort of 12 months instead of 18—but if you do it in 18 it's a little less than 100% IRR but it's still an amazing IRR. The founder's still left with, you know, probably 15%—I don't remember the exact math, you know, at the end—and so everything works. What I find is sometimes people they undercapitalize it or they raise too much money too soon and it's hard then to make the returns that investors require because every investor—the seed round is different from the A, different from the B, different from the C—they're different investors and they have a different profile that they invest in. And if you stick to this trajectory, you're kind of at the right place at the right valuation for the right investor each round. Mark, I—you know—we've talked about this, how much I think the process and the intention and the foundation has been so instrumental and so unique in terms of how you've been able to do what you do over and over again. When people look at your success, what do you think they underestimate in terms of what's been a driving force? Are there things that people don't understand in terms of what it's really taken or in terms of what your superpowers are as it relates to building again and again at this level? Yeah, I mean there's a lot of pieces to that. I've built this framework called VCP: Vision Capital People. Each has a few different components to it. I spend 80 to 90% of my time thinking about VCP. When you think about Vision it is: what is your 10 to 15 year vision and getting that written down on a piece of paper? What are the core strategies, the 'how' of achieving that vision? What is org—what's the organizational structure that backs the strategy? So again, you have the right people in the right spots driving the most important strategies and metrics without any key dependencies. And how you build that is really important. Having a capital plan under C and having the right pitch deck and the right story and just spending time on that. And then on under the P is, you know, having a clear mission, a clear set of behaviors, a clear compensation system, performance management system. We actually have a room in the office called VCP and we have all these things I just mentioned on whiteboards around the room. And every week our team meets to talk about VCP. It's sort of—the analogy is, you know, a basketball player doing foundational work. So they could be an NBA player but still sitting under the basket and just without even a ball just working, working the form. And that's what I think is missing. A lot of people just running around, you know, putting out fires but not always taking the time to do the foundational work. Because when everyone in the organization understands the nuances of the vision and strategy, in every word—what does 'very' mean? what does 'a lot' mean?—really the nuances of it and that permeates throughout the organization, then everyone is thinking like an owner, everyone's thinking like the founder. And that's leverage. Very much so, if you can create that at scale in a company and really unique to do. Mark, as we wrap up, I'm really struck because you're not someone who's had one success and then is happy just to take a step back and be on vacation for 365 years. You are incredibly driven, you know, all the things I read, incredibly competitive as well. You have to be to do what you've done. When you look at the companies you're building, obviously Wonder super ambitious, have a strategy, a plan in front of you. When you take a step back, what are you building toward? At what point do you feel like you will have achieved success as you define it? Yeah, well first I, you know, I think you have to have a mission that you're really passionate about. It's tough to just say "I'm just building this to make money"—it doesn't, that's not that motivational. But our mission at Wonder is to make great food more accessible. And so we're bringing great food to suburban and exurban areas that don't have access to great food. We're open now till a.m. in the morning in the suburbs and even in exurban areas. Again, where there's only fast food available, so people that are working night shifts at the hospital, they now could have access to better quality food. We're bringing down the price point of great food. We've got a couple of great concepts, salad concept and a fast-casual Mexican that's 30% cheaper than what you'll find in the market for the same quality and portion size. That really fuels me. I love the idea of making food, great food, more accessible to people. And it's such a big mission and it's sort of our north star. We're never going to get there. We're never going to say we checked the box, great food is now accessible. Like you could always do more. And so we're very driven to do that. And so that that's fun. And then as an entrepreneur, you know, you do think about the brands that people talk about from centuries ago. And what was it about those businesses that allowed it to last centuries? I think that's the dream of any entrepreneur, right? To build a business that has that kind of legacy. And so there's some fun in that as well. So I think I would say the combination of that plus the mission is really what fuels me and why I love to get up every morning and do what I do. I love that, yes, to build businesses that outlast you. That's one of the greatest testaments to a successful founder. Well Mark, it's really exciting what you're building with Wonder. I can't wait to see what's ahead, particularly as you look down the IPO route. A really interesting moment for the company but I would imagine for you and your entrepreneurial journey as well. So thanks so much for the insights that you shared and we'll all be watching. Thank you. It was great to be here. Thank you.

President Trump Delivers Remarks, May 8, 2026

The White House(3.1M) ·20:03 ·96.0K views · 2026-05-08
Donald Trump (President, United States)
President Trump hosted a Rose Garden event to honor Mother’s Day, specifically recognizing "Angel Moms" and Gold Star families while highlighting administration successes in border security, drug price reduction, and family-focused economic incentives.
TL;DR President Trump hosted a Rose Garden event to honor Mother’s Day, specifically recognizing "Angel Moms" and Gold Star families while highlighting administration successes in border security, drug price reduction, and family-focused economic incentives. The President...

TL;DR

President Trump hosted a Rose Garden event to honor Mother’s Day, specifically recognizing "Angel Moms" and Gold Star families while highlighting administration successes in border security, drug price reduction, and family-focused economic incentives.

Border Security and "Angel Moms"

The President dedicated a significant portion of his remarks to mothers whose children were victims of crimes committed by illegal immigrants, linking their loss to national security policy.

  • Zero-Tolerance Results: Trump claimed that the southern border has achieved "zero" illegal crossings over the last 11 months, attributing this to a shift from "open border" policies to the "strongest border in American history."
  • Angel Mom Recognition: He specifically recognized Mary Ann Mendoza, Tammy Nobles, and Jacqueline Medina, praising their "unyielding resolve" in advocating for stricter immigration enforcement to prevent further tragedies.
  • Criminal Statistics: The President stated that 11,888 murderers had previously been allowed into the country under prior administrations and claimed that his administration is actively removing these individuals.

Fentanyl and the Drug Epidemic

The briefing detailed progress in the "war on drugs," focusing on the reduction of synthetic opioids entering the United States.

  • Supply Chain Disruptions:
      • Maritime Interdiction: Reported a 97% decrease in illegal drugs entering the country by sea.
      • Fentanyl Reduction: Claimed a 59% drop in the flow of fentanyl across land borders.
  • Legislative Action: Trump highlighted the signing of the HALT Fentanyl Act as a cornerstone of his mission to end the drug epidemic.
  • Cartel Influence: Acknowledged the difficulty of land enforcement due to the power of Mexican cartels, noting that "poison" still claims roughly 200,000 American lives annually.

Military Sacrifice and Gold Star Honors

The President paid tribute to mothers of fallen service members, focusing on the human cost of military engagement and recent tactical failures.

  • The Story of Jesse Melton: Trump honored Gold Star Mom Janice Chance, recounting the story of Marine Captain Jesse Melton, who volunteered to take a comrade's place so the fellow Marine could witness his daughter's birth, ultimately losing his life.
  • Abbey Gate Criticism: He specifically addressed mothers of those killed during the Afghanistan withdrawal at Abbey Gate, calling the event a result of "gross incompetence" by the previous administration.
  • Gold Star Mothers Present: Recognized Alice Lopez, Alicia Lopez, Paula Knauss-Self, and Coral Darsino for their sacrifice and resilience.

Family Economic Policy and Healthcare

The President outlined a series of financial incentives designed to support American families and lower the cost of living.

  • Direct Financial Support:
      • Newborn Grants: Announced a program giving every American newborn $1,000 automatically for long-term investment.
      • Trump Accounts: Tax-advantaged savings accounts designed to help families save for their children's futures.
      • Child Tax Credit: Highlighted the expansion of the credit to $2,200 per child.
  • Healthcare Costs:
      • Prescription Drugs: Claimed a 70–90% reduction in drug prices through a "most favored nations" policy, ensuring the U.S. pays the lowest price globally for prescriptions.
      • Family Planning: Pledged to lower the costs of IVF and cut regulatory barriers for families.
  • Education: Ttouted the "largest expansion of school choice in American history."

Rose Garden Renovations

Trump opened the event by discussing the physical transformation of the Rose Garden and the White House grounds.

  • Structural Upgrades: Replaced the "wetland" grass with white stone to prevent mud and allow for year-round events, such as lunches and news conferences.
  • Presidential Walk of Fame: Introduced a new installation featuring black granite and images of U.S. presidents from George Washington to the present.
"In their honor, we’ve rapidly turned the worst and most dangerous border in the history of our country into the strongest border in American history... during the last 11 months, we have zero, zero [crossings]." — President Donald J. Trump

Global Markets, World Uncertainties and the $2 Trillion Fund | Jens Stoltenberg & David Solomon

Norges Bank Investment Management(107.0K) ·27:49 ·4.0K views · 2026-05-08
Nicolai Tangen (CEO, NBIM) Jens Stoltenberg (Former Secretary General, NATO) David Solomon (CEO, Goldman Sachs)
While geopolitical instability in the Middle East and structural stagnation in Europe present significant short-term risks, long-term economic optimism remains high due to the transformative productivity potential of AI and the continued compounding power of global equity markets.
TL;DR While geopolitical instability in the Middle East and structural stagnation in Europe present significant short-term risks, long-term economic optimism remains high due to the transformative productivity potential of AI and the continued compounding power of global equity...

TL;DR

While geopolitical instability in the Middle East and structural stagnation in Europe present significant short-term risks, long-term economic optimism remains high due to the transformative productivity potential of AI and the continued compounding power of global equity markets.

Geopolitics and Market Volatility

  • Middle East Conflict: Jens Stoltenberg characterized the current situation as "serious and dangerous," noting that while the primary tragedy is human suffering, the economic fallout—reduced energy supplies, higher prices, and inflationary pressure—is inevitable.
  • Market Disconnection: David Solomon expressed surprise that equity markets have not "derisked" more in response to geopolitical tension.
      • Underpriced Risks: While rate and commodity markets have reacted, equity risk premia remain low. Solomon suggests the market is discounting the high probability of a "longer conflict" that could leave "sand in the gears" of global growth.
  • Private Credit Resilience: Despite concerns about higher rates, Solomon argues that the private credit asset class (approx. $1.7 trillion) is manageable.
      • Historical Context: During the Financial Crisis, leveraged lending saw a 10% default rate but a 50% recovery rate; with current coupons at 9-10%, the net loss remains sustainable for diversified portfolios.

The Growth Gap: Europe vs. the United States

  • Shifting Allocations: The Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund has halved its European exposure (from 42% to 21%) over the last decade while increasing US exposure to 55%, driven largely by the divergence in earnings growth.
  • Structural Impediments: Solomon identified a widening gap between US trend growth (~2%) and European trend growth (~0.7%).
      • Fragmentation: Europe functions as 27 independent nations rather than a single economic bloc, inhibiting the creation of "European champions" in sectors like banking.
      • Bureaucracy: Excessive regulation and a failure to implement reforms (such as those suggested in the Draghi report) act as headwinds.
  • Risk Culture: Solomon argued that a "risk-taking culture" is notably absent in Europe compared to the US. He noted that the "risk-taking genes" largely migrated to America, leaving Europe with a more cautious, status-quo-oriented population.
  • Investment Strategy: Stoltenberg defended the fund’s "conservative" index-based approach, owning roughly 1.5% of 7,200 companies globally as the most effective risk management tool for a democratic nation.

AI and the Productivity Revolution

  • Enterprise Transformation: Solomon views AI as a "powerful technology acceleration" that allows firms to move beyond simple automation to "remaking operating processes" from scratch.
      • Capacity for Growth: Productivity gains from AI allow enterprises to reinvest saved capital into growth areas where they were previously resource-constrained.
  • The Ford Analogy: Solomon compared the AI revolution to Henry Ford’s implementation of the assembly line, which allowed for a shift from a six-day to a five-day work week without losing productivity.
  • Norway’s Digital Exception: Stoltenberg noted that while Europe lags generally, Norway maintains high productivity per hour worked due to early and aggressive adoption of digital solutions in both the public and private sectors.

The China Outlook

  • Shifting Predictions: Solomon noted that in 2014, economists predicted China would be the world's largest economy by now. While China hit its GDP targets, the US outperformed expectations so significantly that China’s path to the top spot is no longer "cast in stone."
  • Capital Allocation: Solomon expressed skepticism that a centrally controlled economy can compete indefinitely with a market-driven one.
      • Interdependence: Despite friction, Solomon emphasized that "we need China, and China needs us," suggesting a dynamic of "gives and gets" rather than total decoupling.

Notable Quotes

"In 1996, [the Sovereign Wealth Fund] was zero. Now it is two trillion... 27% of every krone we spend on the Norwegian budget is financed by the return from the fund." — Jens Stoltenberg
"A lot of the risk-taking genes left with the Mayflower. The risk-takers per definition said, 'Let’s take some risks, let’s go to America.' The people who are left in Europe are the people who don’t want to change." — David Solomon
"Find me a 25-year period of time where the world was not better 25 years in the future from where it was 25 years in the past. You can’t find one for the last thousands of years." — David Solomon

Well, I read it as a very serious and dangerous situation. As a politician but also as an economist by... I worked for many years ago as an economist. I'm a bit surprised that the markets have not reacted more. And David, why is that? You know, obviously commodity markets have reacted. But equity risk premia has not really reacted. And I don't have a good answer. And so I don't have a crystal ball. The one thing I know is I don't know.
