It's actually a reasonable possibility. And if it continues, you will see equity prices repricing over time. Do you think it could amplify the certain signs we are seeing in private credit through higher rates, more difficult environment? I think when you're talking about private credit, you really have to recognize a handful of things. But one of the things I'd just say is credit broadly is an asset class and private credit is a small part of the overall asset class of credit broadly. It's direct lending, which is what's getting all the attention, is 1.6, 1.7 trillion dollars of assets, about 230 billion of which is kind of retail-focused. If we have an economic slowdown, losses will increase. But I think, and I said this on our earnings call this morning, I think the thing that people aren't putting in perspective, what does a cycle look like? Well, if you take a very difficult cycle, the financial crisis, all leveraged lending through the financial crisis, the loss rate was, the default rate was about 10% and the recovery rate was 50%, which means the net loss rate was about 5 to 6%. And the coupons are 9 to 10%. So you have to keep that in perspective, both on the size of the asset class and the cycle. It doesn't have to be a systemic thing, probably won't for that asset class. And even though losses go up because we have an economic slowdown, it doesn't mean through the life of the portfolio that the performance hasn't been reasonable. If you're collecting 10%, we've had a long cycle, if you collect 10% for 15 years and then you go through a cycle where you have a 5 or 6% loss, your returns are actually going to be okay. Over the last 10 years in this fund, the investments in Europe have gone from 42% to 21%. So it's halved. Those in the US have gone from 37 to 55. And it's to a very large extent driven by difference in earnings growth. Jens, any reflection on just the relative power of Europe and the US? Well, it reflects a reality that the economy in the US has grown much more than the economy and markets in Europe. And since we are indexed, or at least mainly an index fund, of course that is reflected in the way the Norwegian Pension Fund is invested. I think for a politician who is responsible for setting those indexes, the big issue is should we change? Should we do something different? And we actually asked some experts to evaluate that. And the main conclusion from that group is actually that we should not change. And that the main idea we developed and agreed back in 1997, that we should follow the markets, we should spread across the world in almost all listed companies. As you know, we own roughly 1.5% of almost all listed companies, of more than 7,200 companies in total. That is the best way for a country, a democratic country to manage risk. I say this also partly because I remember again when we made the decision that I had meetings with the Qatari investment fund and with Kuwait investment office and so on, and they had a totally different approach. We wanted to learn from them but we decided to do the opposite, to be very conservative, to spread the risks. And also tell the Norwegian voters and the media and the journalists when they ask about obvious risks, for instance to be so big in the United States, is that there's no way to hide 20,000 billion Norwegian kroner or two trillion. The alternative was to keep the value, the national wealth in oil and gas in the ground. That would have been even more risky. So this long answer has one conclusion and that is that we strongly believe from the Norwegian government that we should continue to be diversified, small in many companies, and then we are extremely grateful for what you do also with the active management, but that's within limits that doesn't really change that main approach. And David, any reflection on the differential between Europe and, not necessarily share price performance but more the environment, the operating environment for companies? What are companies telling you about investing in Europe? Sure. And the thing I'd highlight because you're talking about it as an indexation against earnings growth, 10 years from now it's going to continue because Europe is 0.7 trend growth and the US is 2% trend growth. And so what you're seeing and what you've seen, what's created this widening differential is one's growing meaningfully faster than the other and that compounds. It's going to keep compounding unless Europe changes the way it operates, which it doesn't seem that Europe has the collective ability to materially change the way it operates to get that growth level up to a different place. I mean, you just have to step back and look at the size and the scale of Europe and its growth rate against the size and the scale of the US and its growth rate and it looks like that's going to continue to compound unless something were to change that doesn't seem likely at the moment. What could they change? Well, I think the thing that I've talked publicly about is that the theory of the European Union was to harness the collective economic power of all the European nations. But that's not happened. You know, we can talk about the great experiment of the European Union and there have been benefits, obviously, but the real benefit was to harness the economic might of 450 million people and get people to work collectively. But what we really have is 27 nations operating relatively independently, a big bureaucracy that sits in the middle that actually inhibits growth and investment. And look, I think Mario Draghi's paper have a lot of good ideas but only about 11% of them have been implemented. So one thing you can do is implement 50% of them. But given the way Europe operates, I think the chance of that is low. And that will continue to be a headwind for European growth and will continue to shift the weighting of where a fund like yours, if you're indexing against earnings around the world, when you look forward 10 years, unless there's a shift in that, that will widen. I totally agree. I think it's in one way quite obvious what Europe should do and that is to actually create a real single market. In the 90s, they were quite successful to establish the internal market, to establish a common currency. I think many people believed that was not possible, they did it, and it triggered some growth. Then it has slowed down and just to be able to create a capital union, savings union, and to really have common regulation would be a huge step forward, would help them a lot. And when you meet political leaders in Europe, they all see it. But so far they've not been able to really implement that. But at some stage, I really hope and believe they will do it. Then remember, the European Union, it's 450 million people. But if you look at Europe, for instance, defined by NATO allies, which are European countries but not members of the EU, like Norway, and add them to the 450, then we have 600 million people. So it's a huge economy and a lot of people and a great potential. But then we need to overcome the fragmentation and all the national regulations which still exist in Europe. Two things that I think need to happen to do that. One, you've got to create European champions. So for example, you need a couple of European champion financial institutions that can compete with the leading financial institutions in the US. But to do that, you can't be worried about whether they're located in France or Germany or somewhere else. They just have to be located in Europe. It has to be in the collective interest of Europe to create banking consolidation across borders so that you have a scaled institution that has the ability to compete globally. You have to have centralized capital markets, not fragmented capital markets, so you can have capital formation. The other thing that's really lacking in Europe broadly speaking, present company excluded, is there is not a risk-taking culture in Europe. One of the great things about the United States is people want to take risk, and taking risk spurs investment, which drives innovation and drives economic growth. A lot of the risk-taking genes left with the Mayflower, you know. And so, because the risk-takers per definition is just 'Hey, let's take some risks, let's go to America.' And so the people who are left in Europe are the people who don't want to change, we want to be at home, you know, with mummy. So that's a bit different. Like you and me. Well, at least I studied here. Okay, neither. Okay. David, one of the reasons for the trend growth, the difference in trend growth is technology. And of course, you're seeing it in, you know, the large concentration in stock markets and so on. How does AI change the way you work at Goldman's? How we're working at Goldman, how we're working... I mean, I'm super excited about AI technology and what it's going to do over the next decade or two to accelerate growth and productivity in the world. This is a powerful technology acceleration that the best enterprises will figure out how to use it to really remake operating processes in their business so that they have more capacity to invest in growth in their business. And so at Goldman Sachs, there are two ways that we think about this. The first is our business because it's a professional services business, has a lot of smart people. And so one of the things we obviously do is get these tools, these technology tools into the hands of smart people so the smart people can find ways to do more, do things more productively. That's easy, we've done that for ages with technology. But the more powerful thing here is that this technology is allowing big enterprises to remake operating processes, really fundamentally take out a white sheet of paper and say the way we've always done this, this particular process, we can do it differently. The result of that is enormous efficiency and productivity gains. And you know, the benefit of that is not just that you can bring your cost down, the benefit for most enterprises is it gives you more capacity to invest in places where you want to invest to grow that you've been constrained in terms of how much you can invest. And so we're extremely focused on that combination of re-underwriting certain operating processes to create efficiency and create more capacity to invest in growth in places where we feel constrained. And most CEOs that I talk to are really focused around that thought process. A paradox I know about Norway on this is that when you look at Europe, they have not invested as much in digital solutions and AI as the United States. But Norway is an exception for some reason. It's quite high. And I think that demonstrates that we don't have the big tech companies but we are able to use, to implement the solutions they are providing at a higher scale than most other European countries. And for again for a politician, the issue is not only to understand the potential of AI, which is obviously great, but how can we as politicians create a framework so actually the society is able to take benefit and to use that technology. And I think that's at least a lesson from Norway is that high level education helps and also high level digital solutions, for instance in the public sector. I lived for some 10 years in Belgium and it's a nightmare when it comes to, you know, basic issues like tax forms or all the financial transactions compared to in Norway where you do it on your cell phone, most of those transactions. And the fact that we like to spend some quality time at our cabins in the mountains. Yeah, that's not wrong in itself. Should be a driver for using AI. That's correct. But I just saw an overview in the Economist, they compared productivity and Norway again, they're quite high when it comes to overall productivity, but in particular when it comes to productivity per hour worked. So when we don't work so much, but we are very productive when we work. The joking aside on hours worked, just a lens for people to think about when people talk about this. You know, when you think through history, you have technology change and it destroys jobs and it changes jobs and things shift. If you go back 115 years when Henry Ford was starting to mass produce cars, people think about Henry Ford as being famous for creating the assembly line to build cars. But another thing that Henry Ford was famous for is he was the first CEO of a big US company to say the company was going to go to a five-day work week from a six-day work week. And he thought because the assembly line was increasing productivity, he could in a five-day work week with that assembly line get as much productivity or more than he was getting in a six-day work week and it would be better for his employees. It's a lens to look at in the world we live in today. We don't have to be locked in a five-day work week. It doesn't mean that everybody works hard, but productivity can go up in many ways and things that look so certain today might not look so certain 10 years from now with the productivity leverage that we have. And it's just interesting to go back in history and think about the shift from 95% of jobs coming from farming to an industrial revolution to now a technological revolution, and there'll be things that mirror in different ways. But we are increasing productivity massively, and that doesn't mean that it doesn't come from the same metrics that we've always looked at. But we are now up against China where they don't really talk about four-day week, it's more like six-day week, right? And they are applying AI even more than we do, even though on the models they may be slightly behind, the application is very strong. Earlier today I did a podcast with the CEO of Pfizer. He thinks that within one to two years they'll be ahead of us also in cancer medicine. So just how do you see the Chinese competition when you talk to your clients? China's a very, very significant economy and they might be ahead of us in certain things. But I think anyone that's so certain as to how this plays out should just look at history as a lens because it's not so simple. Jim O'Neill was a very, very famous Goldman Sachs economist. He's the Goldman Sachs economist who coined the phrase BRICs. In 2014, Jim O'Neill predicted that China would be the largest economy in the world and in 2027 it would be a 21 trillion dollar economy. Well, he got the 21 trillion, right? It looks like China's going to be a 22 trillion dollar economy in 2027. But what he got massively wrong was how the US would grow competitively and how the US's position would change. And so if we were sitting here in 2018, all three of us would agree China would be the largest economy in the world sometime in this decade. Now we all sit here and everyone would agree there's no way China will be the largest economy anytime that we can see in the future. So the world is competitive. They're going to be a very large economy. We have to figure out how to work with and integrate with China. There's a lot of friction there. But it's not cast in stone. And I would just say that in the history of the world, it is very, very hard to compete in a globally competitive world when all the capital allocation is centrally controlled. That doesn't mean that this time it won't work. But I think it'll be very dynamic. I think we need China, I think China needs us. There'll be places where they run ahead, there'll be places where we run ahead. But you know, we have to, if we stick to the knitting of really driving growth and freedom and combining those two things, we'll do just fine. But there'll be gives and gets. Absolutely. Before we open up for questions, last thing, David, what advice would you give to the Norwegian Minister of Finance when it comes to winning elections? No, when it comes to, when it comes to the Sovereign Wealth Fund. Well, one thing for sure, I am not a politician. I mean, I've got such enormous admiration for what you've done. Because I think that you intuitively or academically or otherwise really understood the power of believing in growth in the world and of compounding. And I'm still a big believer. You know, the world's not perfect, things are going to be wrong. But I'd bet you a lot that if we were sitting here 25 years from now, the world will continue to be better. And if the world continues to be better, that means it's growing. And if it's growing and you're investing and you're participating, you're compounding. Okay. We all know that 7% for 10 years, you double your resources. And so I don't think I need to give you advice. I think you get it. The world's going to grow, you're going to participate in a very powerful way, and you've got to stick to keeping that participation where the growth is in the world. And if you're aligned with the growth in earnings in the world, you're going to do just fine. I really believe that. And I'm a huge optimist. You've got to step out of the moment, you know, find me a 25-year period of time where the world was not better 25 years in the future from where it was 25 years in the past. You can't find one for the last thousands of years.

A conversation with Manus AI's cofounder and CPO Tao Zhang

Stripe(104.0K) ·38:18 ·218 views · 2026-05-08
Tao Zhang (Cofounder and CPO, Manus AI)
Manus AI transitioned from a viral product demo to $100M in ARR within eight months by shifting the AI paradigm from "answer machines" to "autonomous agents" that execute tasks in a cloud-based virtual sandbox.
TL;DR Manus AI transitioned from a viral product demo to $100M in ARR within eight months by shifting the AI paradigm from "answer machines" to "autonomous agents" that execute tasks in a cloud-based virtual sandbox. The "Mens et Manus" Concept: Tao Zhang explains the...

TL;DR

Manus AI transitioned from a viral product demo to $100M in ARR within eight months by shifting the AI paradigm from "answer machines" to "autonomous agents" that execute tasks in a cloud-based virtual sandbox.

The Product Philosophy: "Mind and Hand"

  • The "Mens et Manus" Concept: Tao Zhang explains the company’s name is derived from the MIT motto (Mind and Hand). While LLMs serve as the "mind," Manus provides the "hands" to operate tools and impact the physical world.
  • Virtual Sandbox (VM): Unlike standard chatbots, Manus provides the AI with its own computer/virtual machine.
      • Execution Capabilities: The agent can browse the web, write and run Python code, store files, and retrieve them later without human intervention.
  • Transparency as a Feature: A critical product decision was allowing users to see the agent’s "thinking" process (opening browsers, writing code).
      • Trust Building: Showing the work process helps users understand and trust the autonomous nature of the agent.
  • Asynchronous Workflow: Because the agent operates in a cloud-based VM rather than locally, users can assign a task and close their laptops. Manus notifies them once the outcome is ready, mirroring a human delegating to a colleague.

Rapid Growth and Monetization Strategy

  • Viral Velocity: The product launch went viral through a raw, unedited screen recording of the agent in action.
      • User Acquisition: 2 million signups in the first week; 3.5 million in the first month.
      • High Demand: Invite codes were reportedly reselling for up to $14,000 online.
  • Zero-Dollar Marketing: Growth was entirely organic, driven by influencers in unexpected markets like Egypt and Brazil.
  • Monetization Metrics: The company tracks success through ARR and 1-5 star user ratings.
      • Signal Detection: While the standard top-tier plan is $200/month, some users pay over $5,000/month via usage-based billing, signaling high-value use cases for small-to-medium businesses.
  • PMF Validation: Zhang believes charging for AI products early is essential because AI is expensive; payment is the ultimate proof of Product-Market Fit (PMF).

A New Playbook for Product Development

  • The Flipped Workflow: Manus AI has reversed the traditional software development sequence.

1. Prototype First: Product managers write prompts (the "new PRD") and use Manus to build a functional prototype with front-end, back-end, and AI logic.

2. Engineering: Engineers determine how to make the prototype production-ready.

3. Design: Designers are the final step in the process, refining the interface after the function is proven.

  • Engineering Pool Structure: The team does not use fixed "feature teams."
      • Flexibility: Engineers belong to a general pool and are assigned to projects as needed.
      • AI for Context: To solve the "reading someone else's code" problem, engineers use AI to ingest and explain entire codebases in minutes, allowing for rapid rotation across projects.

Global Operations and Future Vision

  • Global Footprint: Headquartered in Singapore with offices in Tokyo and San Francisco.
  • Cross-Functional Culture: Despite having multiple sites, Zhang insists that researchers, engineers, and product managers sit together to foster "small talk" innovations.
  • The Problem-Definition Bottleneck: Zhang argues that "solving a problem" is no longer the hurdle; the new bottleneck is the human ability to "define a problem" and identify user pain points.
  • The Era of the Manager: Zhang envisions a future where every individual can be a manager, overseeing 10 to 100 autonomous agents to perform tedious work, allowing humans to focus on higher-level creative and strategic tasks.
"Mind we think they are LLMs, they are the models, they are super smart brains. But only with brain, us humans, we can't make real impact... we have to be the one who build hands for these very smart brains then they can operate tools." — Tao Zhang
"Right now, I think the bottleneck is still the capability of defining a problem. Solving a problem is not a problem anymore. But to define a problem is somehow I think most people still lack this capability." — Tao Zhang
"The prompt itself is the interface... the way you write your prompt actually is just like the way you design your interface 10 years ago. It’s a new PRD." — Tao Zhang
AI isn't just moving fast. It's moving at a pace we've never seen before. At Stripe, we're seeing the data firsthand. AI startups are rewriting the playbook and scaling to revenue milestones years faster than the giants that came before them. But how are they doing it? And how do you sustain that kind of momentum? In this series, we sit down with the founders behind this historic rise to find out. This is the AI Boom on Stripe. Episode 3. Tao, thank you so much for being here with us today. Yeah, me too. So, I'd love to take us back to the first time that I learned about Manus, and I think probably the whole world. It's March 2025. We've had AI chatbots for two, three years. Everyone's kind of been interacting with chat, but that's really it. And then a video starts circulating the internet. No flashy stage, no Silicon Valley keynote, just a raw screen recording of your product, Manus. Tell us what this video shows the world. Yeah, I think it's just the seven days before the launch date. When looking at our website, I think it's kind of like too simple. So I was thinking about maybe we should make a video for our launch. I called some video agencies and they all told me that it took them maybe two weeks or three weeks to build a launch video. I told them we only have like six days and they said oh, this is impossible. So we decided to shoot the video by ourselves. I used my own camera. I shot the video and Peak, our chief scientist, is the actor there talking about the product. I shot it and I edited it. Because we are not a very professional launch studio team, we only put Peak on camera for maybe one minute talking about our product. The rest of the time is Peak demoing our product for three use cases. I think that can be the main reason why we went super viral in the first place. We just showed the world what an AI product could be, other than a chatbot. We actually have that video right here, so let's take a look. Hi, I'm Peak from Manus AI. For the past year, we've been quietly building what we believe is the next evolution in AI. Today we're launching an early preview of Manus, the first general AI agent. This isn't just another chatbot or workflow. It's a truly autonomous agent that bridges the gap between conception and execution. While other AI stops at generating ideas, Manus delivers results. We see it as the next paradigm of human-machine collaboration and potentially a glimpse into AGI. Now let me show you Manus in action across three completely different tasks. Demo 1. Resume Screening. Let's start with an easy one. In this example, we'll ask Manus to help screen resumes. I've just sent Manus a zip file containing 10 resume documents. Since each Manus session has its own computer, it can work like a human—first unzipping the file, then browsing through each resume page by page, and recording important information in documents. Manus works asynchronously in the cloud, which means you can close your laptop anytime and Manus will notify you when everything is complete. So, the video shows basically that with Manus you can not just sort of chat, you can actually execute on tasks. It writes Python code, it opens browsers, and— Yeah, do everything. And it goes entirely viral. I think you had 2 million signups for the private preview after that. Oh, yeah, yeah. We had like 2 million in the first week and we got 3.5 million in the first month. Yeah, a lot of people on the waitlist. I think I read somewhere that people were reselling the invite codes for up to $14,000 online. Yeah, at that time, if an influencer got an invitation code to Manus, he would attract much more traffic compared to the price he paid for the invitation code. Unluckily, we didn't get any money from that. No, of course not. No, that's a whole new—we could do a whole different podcast on fraud on the internet now with AI, but we'll leave that for a different time. So what do you think it was in the video or about the product that struck such a nerve with people? Why was—what was the aha moment for people in that time? Yeah, because I think before Manus, people treated AI just as an answer machine. I give you a question and you give me an answer. It's still like telling you how to do things, but in the end, you have to do things by yourself. But in our launch video, we always focus on the outcome, on the deliveries. Maybe it delivers you a site, it delivers you a website, or it delivers the actual code that works. So I think people just get amazed. Another thing is that in the whole process when the AI is doing things, it's totally without human intervention. You just tell Manus what you want and Manus will figure out what the plan is and how to execute the plan step by step just by itself. It's all automatic. So I think that's really like something new and amazing for the whole world to see. Yeah, I remember thinking, oh, this is—it was the first time where I felt oh, this can actually save me time and help me do my job versus it just being a little bit of a thought partner. It's really magical. Did you know it was going to be so successful? Like the night before launch, were you like, yes, we've hit on something really special? Yeah, we were a very small startup at that time. We only had like 40 people there and we thought it was a very good product. When we developed the product, we were amazed by our product ourselves every day. Every day in our office was filled with, "Oh, it's awesome," or "Damn, how can Manus solve this?" So we thought it was going to be successful. But I think nobody expected that we could be at this level of success. Yeah, that's a high bar to clear. This is a good time to talk about the product. From the very beginning of Manus, I feel like you really reinvented what agentic AI products should look like. I wanted to go over some of these product areas with you and talk through some of the thinking behind them. Virtual sandbox—tell us what that is. The virtual sandbox—internally we call it like a virtual machine. It's super important as it's the fundamental part of the whole Manus system. For us, we think in the past why most AI wouldn't work is because they only use the brain. Maybe here is the time I can share why we chose the name Manus for this product. We take this word Manus from MIT's motto, which is Mens et Manus. It's an old Latin word, which means mind and hand. Mind, we think, they are LLMs; they are the models, they are super smart brains. But only with a brain, us humans, we can't make real impact in the physical world. That's why we think we have to be the one who builds hands for these very smart brains, then they can operate tools and they can make impact in the physical world, right? So I think when I say what wouldn't work for other AI tools is because they only have the brain. So what they do is they keep thinking, keep thinking inside the head. But what we do is that we provide a computer to the AI. So anything the AI thinks, okay, maybe I should do something or maybe I should run some code to test my thesis, it can use its own computer to browse the internet, to run the code, to store a file on the file system, or maybe retrieve the document again later. The virtual machine really changes everything because it provides maybe the most powerful tool, the computer, to the AI. It's now you're building tools for the AI to do its job and that's just really incredible. One of the interesting product decisions you made was for the user to be able to see what was happening, like opening the browser and writing the code. What was behind that decision? Oh, yeah, actually, that's kind of like the first important decision we made after we kicked off the project. Because we thought that agent technology is so new and people are not very familiar with this kind of product. So we think it's very important at this stage to show every step, the whole process to the users, to make users understand what is happening in the background so they can trust the product. This is very important. So we build transparency as a feature in our product. That's right. It's an incredible feature. Tell me a little bit about the async workflow. Because you create that trust and now the user can step away and not really worry. Where did that come from? In our founding team, we are all engineers. Even though I run the product team, I have written code for more than 30 years. So we all know coding. We are all Cursor fans; we use Cursor a lot of times. But there's a problem that when you are using Cursor, you have to keep your laptop open because it runs locally on your computer. So you have to keep it open and watch it. In the first maybe four or five months, it needed us humans to click the accept button. Sometimes it'll ask you accept, accept, accept. When we used the product, we thought, why does Cursor need me to click the accept button? It's because it runs locally on my computer. So it needs my permission to run these commands or it has a chance to break the whole computer. But in Manus, the computer is in the cloud. It's not your computer. So it's super safe to perform each step just by the agent itself. So we don't ask the users to keep pressing the accept button. Instead of that, we just let the agent run everything in the cloud. Then it gives us another advantage, which is the async advantage. Everything is running in the cloud, which means after you assign your task to Manus, you can just leave it. You can just close your laptop, you can put your phone back in your pocket and do your own things. After maybe like 10 or 20 minutes when the work is done, we will give you a notification and you will see the outcome. What do you use Manus for? Oh, a lot. Nearly everything. For me, I'm doing prototyping every day with Manus. Whenever I have a new idea, I will not just ask my designers to put the idea alive to me. I will use Manus instead of that. I will just tell Manus everything that is in my head and Manus will build the first prototype for me. And this is not just something we prototype in Figma. This is a fully functional prototype, which means it has the front-end, back-end, and it has the AI capability inside that. In this prototype, actually you can just try and play with it and tweak it. After the prototype is done, I will give the prototype to my designers and engineers and they will instantly understand what I want because this is the real thing. That's putting the fun back in software development, I think. Yeah. Who is your ideal Manus user? Right now, I think we describe them as non-technical users. In the first months when we kicked off the project, at that time we already had Cursor, Windsurf, Devin—all these fancy tools for engineers. We decided that we would not build another fancy tool for engineers because they have enough. But for normal people, for average users, they are underserved. They don't have these fancy tools. They don't have cloud code; they don't have Codex. So the way we built Manus is to build Manus for everyone. So the ideal user is someone who wants to achieve much more with AI but with less technical background. I love that. And there's so many more of them. Yeah, so many more of them. That's a bigger market. You've been coding for 30 years. Do you think it's different building for AI products or do you think really fundamentally software is software? Oh, I think building AI software is totally different. For me, I'm already in this industry for more than 15 years. Before, the way we built a product was somehow like I have an idea. I just build the prototype on Figma. Then I talk to my designer, talk to my engineer, and they will do the design, the engineering, and then write code. We test it, we launch it, and we hear the feedback. And then we just iterate. That's the way we did things before. But now, I think it's totally changed. Previously, I think product managers and designers' outcome was somehow like a PRD or a design. It was about an interface. But in the AI world, the interface doesn't exist anymore. For most AI products, it's just about conversations. So I think the conversation is the new interface, which means the prompt itself is the interface. I know there are many companies today where prompts are still written by engineers or researchers. But I think a better way to do that is the product manager writes the prompt themselves. Because the way you write your prompt actually is just like the way you designed your interface like 10 years ago. It's the new PRD essentially. Yeah, it's a new PRD; it's a new interface. The way we do product here is that whenever we have a new idea, we will just use Manus itself to prototype with our idea. After we feel the function works really well and this is the feel we want, then we will go to our engineer and ask them how to put this prototype in our production environment. Once the engineer tells us this thing can be delivered in our production environment, the last step is to go to the designer. So the whole process is really in the opposite. We have the final experience first. Then we have to make it engineering ready. And the last step is to ask the designer. It's like everything is ready, just do the design. You've completely changed the sequence of that process. Amazing. So at Stripe, we like to paraphrase this quote from Pablo Picasso, which is essentially: when art critics come together, they like to talk about form and function and meaning, and then when artists come together, they like to talk about where to buy the cheapest turpentine. Tell us a little bit more about that. In our company, we think it's very important to put the product guy, the engineering, and also the researchers sitting together. For example, back in our previous office, I and Peak, our chief scientist, and Bean, who is our full-stack guy—he's a real full-stack guy because he's not a full-stack engineer, he does everything. Our logo is also designed by him. So he's a full-stack guy. So we sit together and every day we have many small talks. It's like, "Okay, I saw something very interesting. Can we try that?" When we sit together, it's very easy to try out some new ideas. I know there are some teams that will separate different teams—the product part and the engineering part. We insist that we should sit together. Right now it's still me, Bean, and Peak sitting together and every day we have many small talks. We believe that those small talks lead to all the innovations we did in the past year. Yeah, especially for a product like Manus, which is so far-reaching and so cross-functional. You said something interesting about how you structure your engineering team. Most engineering teams are structured by product areas or even the customer journey. How do you guys decide on who works on what? Oh, our CTO Panpan has his own philosophy about managing an engineering team. Right now, we have near 40 or 50 engineers and they are in one very big team. Whenever we have a new project, Panpan will just assign someone to working on this project. After that, they will come back to the big pool. No one is specifically appointed to a feature or a project. We think it's very important because it makes us much more flexible. So, a project comes up and you're like "Who here is available?" and people raise their hand and you're like "Great, you two go work on this particular project." They are done with the project, they come back into the general pool and then it's the next project. Who maintains the code and the product after they're done with the project? Oh, yeah, that's a good question. I think the way we run our engineering team also somehow benefits from the AI's capability. Before, we would let the same engineer keep working on the same project because we needed the context. It's really hard to read others' code, right? But right now with the help of AI, it's really easy. If we give an engineer a project and there is some existing code, he can just use the AI to read all the code and explain how the whole project works. So it only takes maybe 10 minutes to understand a very complex project. Before that, it would take days. It's an incredible unlock because now you have everyone, and also people by definition will have more context anyway because they're working on so many different pieces. So I think that's amazing. I hope that's kind of the future of product development and building products. At Stripe, we like to paraphrase this quote from Pablo Picasso, which is essentially: when art critics come together, they like to talk about form and function and meaning, and then when artists come together, they like to talk about where to buy the cheapest turpentine. Yeah, definitely. Actually, I put that sentence in my personal profile. Really? Yeah, I really love that sentence. That's amazing. Well, we love it here too. One of the reasons I love doing these types of interviews is that the builders and the co-founders of these companies like you guys are the artists. So, I want to talk real tactics now about how you've built Manus and really built the company and gone globally. You went from zero to 100 million in ARR in just eight months. How? Oh, okay. Yeah, I know a lot of people are very curious about that. But we are also curious about that too. Before last November, which was in the first eight months, we nearly put zero marketing budget into the whole product, which means most of the traffic was organic traffic from everywhere. For example, back in last June, we were at the top place in the App Store of Egypt. We don't know why. We did some research and found out there was some random guy on Facebook who wrote a post in Arabic talking about Manus, which made us go viral in Egypt. We didn't know that before the post. In Brazil, we have a huge user base there because two influencers on YouTube made videos about us. We didn't know these two influencers until then. For us, a lot of viral waves just came from word of mouth. As you just mentioned, the first time you saw our video, you thought "Oh, this is something new," and you got amazed and told your friends. In the whole first eight months, it was all about the product itself and word of mouth. So, your growth tactic is just build an insanely good product and listen to your feedback. Yeah, that's it. I know in many teams they will treat growth as a separate sector in their company, but for us, we think the best growth is the product itself. After last year, the management team had some review about the past year and we saw there were some important decisions in our product that made us more viral. The first thing is about 20 days before the launch—we decided to build a feature we call session replay. Whenever a task is done and you share the task to your friends, your friends will see the whole session in replay mode. We think that's very important. If your friend only sees the final step, they will not get amazed. They'll just think it's another AI product. But when they see the session in replay mode, they're like "Oh, what is going on?" The AI is controlling the browser, the AI is writing the code, and so on. I think that feature is really important for our growth. You also have the build-first, sign-up-later feature that's obviously using Stripe sandboxes. Has that helped at all with your growth? Oh, yeah, I think that part helped the growth of our Manus 1.5 feature. In the version of Manus 1.5, we introduced a new way to build websites. Before that, Manus could only build static web pages—just static HTML. But in Manus 1.5, we can build fully functional websites with the front-end, back-end databases, and native AI capabilities. Many users were asking us if they could integrate Stripe into the system so they could run their real business on a Manus website. Stripe has the best developer experiences. But because our ideal customer is the non-technical person, it's really hard for them to understand what a token is or many technical things. We think the Stripe sandbox really helped us to make the whole process much simpler. Even for non-technical users, they can build a functional business like an e-commerce website without any knowledge about the technical details. Once he is done with the website, he can just transfer the Stripe sandbox to the real environment. It's an incredible feature. I'm so excited to see all the businesses that are going to happen because of tools like Manus. One of the really cool things about Stripe is that we get some insight into how fast AI cohorts grow, and they have fundamentally different growth curves. In this new world, how do you measure your success when you don't have many benchmarks? I think for us, we care about two metrics. The first is about ARR. It's a traditional metric, but we think it's much more important in this AI era. Right now, AI is still very expensive. You can't just make a free product. In my early years in this industry, we were always talking about not caring about revenue and just bringing the users in, and after you have millions of daily active users, you can find a way to make money. But today, I think things have changed. You must ask the users to pay for your product in the first version. AI is expensive right now, so it's really important to earn money not just for revenue, but to prove your PMF. Someone will pay only if they feel the value is real. Focus on ARR because that means a lot for your PMF. The second metric we are tracking is the user's rating. After every task is done, we will ask the user to rate the task on a scale of one to five stars. That tells us a lot about our quality and performance. You can see these ratings through different segments—by different features, countries, or pricing tiers. That tells us a lot. We are following these two metrics and we are analyzing them every week. Speaking of ARR, how did you think about pricing? Pricing is super important, but we don't know what's the best approach. We learn from each other. If you are looking at today's cutting-edge AI applications from a yearly scope, you will see everyone changes. Sometimes we will learn from others and six months later we find out they learned from us too. Everyone changes their pricing plans. We don't know the best practice here, but we think it's very important to keep in mind that there are some very heavy users and powerful users. They will feel like they get much more value than other users, so they're willing to pay more. Always leave a space there for users to be charged by usage. Usage-based billing is very important. Even if you have a monthly subscription plan, always leave a space there for users to pay more. It seems like it's kind of unbound in some ways; there are users who are willing to pay a whole lot more. And also, I think this is not just about the revenue, it's also about signals. For us, our top plan when we launched was $200. We thought it was a lot. After we launched the plan, we left an option for our users to recharge their account after they hit the limit. Luckily, we found out there are some users who would pay us $5,000 a month. That's a super positive signal to catch. If someone pays $5,000 a month, it means the output of the Manus may be valued 10 times more. So we will do a user interview and find out what they are using us for. Why do they think it's so valuable? That provides us with many insights for the future product roadmap. Any insights you can share from those conversations? Definitely. Why we made Manus 1.5 is because we were looking at users who build web pages in Manus and they spend a lot. We asked them why they want to build these web pages there. They told us that in their country, it's very hard to find engineers to build a business website for small-medium businesses. So for them, spending $1,000 on Manus is super cheap. That gave us some inspiration. We live in a very big country and every day we talk to people from the US which is full of tech people, so we don't feel that pain. But the pain exists in the real world. There are people we never met but they have this pain. After we understood that, we thought we should make our website builder much more powerful. That's why in Manus 1.5 we introduced fully functional website building with the front-end, back-end, and databases. What a fantastic insight. You're bringing access to parts of the world that just never had it. Folks can just start their own business entirely on top of Manus. Just five years ago, the bottleneck was coding. That's no longer the case. What do you think is the bottleneck now? Is it people's imagination? Is it being able to prompt? Yeah, I think right now the bottleneck is still the capability of defining a problem. Solving a problem is not a problem anymore. But to define a problem is somehow something most people are still lacking. In today's world, the AI still needs you to ask the question to assign the task to them. You have to be the one to watch around. What is the pain? What is my customer's pain? What does our organization need right now? You can generate questions from these real user pains and give the task to the AI. Ask it to solve it for you. But if you can't see these problems in your life, you don't have any task for the AI. So I think that's still being the bottleneck. Do you think eventually AI will also help us ask the questions themselves and define the problems? I think maybe, yeah. But I think it's very important for us to learn. It fundamentally changes the way we learn and we execute on our daily jobs. Before, only a few people could be the manager. Not everyone could be the manager because you need people to do the work. But right now, I think everyone can be the manager. If you know how to manage, you can manage 10 or 100 agents working for you. So I think this is a new era for everyone to learn how to be a good manager. Fast forward 20 years in the future—AI is doing a lot of the tasks and the execution. What do you hope the world will look like? I know a lot of people are afraid of that future because they think AI is taking their jobs. But if you are looking at maybe 100 years ago when we just invented cars, a lot of people thought that too. When we invented machines to lift heavy things, people thought "Oh, I don't have my job," because before that maybe we needed 20 people to lift something. But look at history—after we had cars and machines and we released labor from this tedious work, we found so many things to do. We found arts, new things, and new business models. So I think AI will be like that. 20 years later, there are so many tedious works we don't have to pay attention to, but there will be more and more important things for us to do. Yeah, that's a beautiful thought. Maybe it'll let us get a little closer to what it means to be really human. Well, I'm so excited for you and Manus to help us get to that future vision. Thank you so much, Tao. This was fantastic. I really enjoyed chatting. Yeah, thank you, Eeke. Me too.

Secretary of State Rubio Speaks to Reporters in Rome

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·23:10 ·10.5K views · 2026-05-08
Marco Rubio (Secretary of State, United States) Giorgia Meloni (Prime Minister, Italy) Pope Leo (Pope)
Secretary of State Marco Rubio’s visit to Rome focused on aligning with the Vatican and the Italian government on global security threats, specifically Iran’s maritime aggression and the need for humanitarian corridors in Cuba and Sudan.
TL;DR Secretary of State Marco Rubio’s visit to Rome focused on aligning with the Vatican and the Italian government on global security threats, specifically Iran’s maritime aggression and the need for humanitarian corridors in Cuba and Sudan. Rubio reaffirmed that while the...

TL;DR

Secretary of State Marco Rubio’s visit to Rome focused on aligning with the Vatican and the Italian government on global security threats, specifically Iran’s maritime aggression and the need for humanitarian corridors in Cuba and Sudan. Rubio reaffirmed that while the U.S. remains committed to NATO, future troop deployments will be contingent on members' willingness to grant the U.S. logistical power projection during global contingencies.

Diplomatic Engagement with the Vatican

  • The Papal Meeting: Rubio characterized his meeting with Pope Leo as "cordial and important," focusing on the Church’s role as a global spiritual leader and humanitarian facilitator.
      • Strategic Alignment: Discussions covered Christian populations in Africa, the situation in Lebanon, and the Church’s insights into Latin American political dynamics.
      • Addressing Tension: Rubio dismissed media narratives regarding President Trump’s past criticisms of the Pope, stating the President will act in the U.S. interest while maintaining a "productive and fruitful" relationship with the Church.
  • Humanitarian Aid to Cuba: Rubio highlighted that the U.S. has offered $100 million in humanitarian aid to the Cuban people, but the "incompetent regime" is blocking distribution.
      • Role of the Church: The Catholic agency Caritas remains a primary partner for distributing aid, such as recent hurricane relief.

National Security and NATO Strategy

  • NATO and Troop Presence: Rubio addressed potential U.S. troop withdrawals from Europe, noting that while no final decisions have been made by the President, the U.S. is reassessing its footprint.
      • Base Access Challenges: Rubio expressed frustration with NATO allies—specifically citing Spain—who denied the U.S. use of bases during recent "contingencies."
      • Policy Shift: He warned that if NATO membership no longer guarantees the ability to project power from European bases, the U.S. must examine the cost and utility of those deployments.
  • Ukraine and Russia: Rubio described the ongoing conflict as a tragedy and noted that U.S. mediation efforts have "stagnated." He emphasized that the U.S. stands ready to mediate only if there is a realistic opportunity for a diplomatic resolution.

Iranian Aggression and International Waterways

  • Maritime "Red Lines": Rubio warned that Iran is attempting to "normalize" illegal control over international waterways, specifically the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Defensive Posture: Following reports of U.S. destroyers being fired upon, Rubio stated that any threat to American assets would be met with immediate force.
      • Operation Epic Fury: He distinguished current defensive responses from "Operation Epic Fury," which he defined as a completed offensive mission that successfully destroyed Iranian missile launchers and air capabilities.
  • Nuclear Ambitions: Rubio asserted there is global consensus that a nuclear-armed Iran is "unacceptable," but emphasized that the current administration is the first to take "concrete" action to prevent it.

Regional Conflicts and Foreign Policy

  • Lebanon and Hezbollah: The U.S. goal is a strong Lebanese government capable of confronting Hezbollah. Rubio called on Italy to play a role in equipping the Lebanese military and cutting off illicit financing for the Iranian-backed group.
  • Sudan Crisis: Rubio identified Sudan as one of the world's worst humanitarian crises. The U.S. is pushing for a "humanitarian ceasefire" and safe havens for aid distribution through the "quad process."
  • Cuba Sanctions: Rubio clarified that recent sanctions target GAESA, a holding company run by Cuban generals that "steals from the Cuban people" to enrich regime insiders, rather than targeting the populace directly.
  • Taiwan: Ahead of a scheduled trip to China, Rubio reaffirmed that U.S. policy remains unchanged: the U.S. opposes any forced or compelled change to the status quo in the Taiwan Strait.

***

Notable Quotes

"The red line is clear: if they threaten Americans they're going to get blown up... If you fire a missile at the United States and we saw you fire it, we're going to hit you. Of course we are. Who doesn't do that?" — Secretary of State Marco Rubio
"One of the advantages of being in NATO is that it allows us to have forces deployed in Europe... some countries in Europe, like Spain as an example, denied us the use of those bases for a very important contingency... if that's no longer the case... that's a problem and it has to be examined." — Secretary of State Marco Rubio
"We have the most resources, but our resources are not unlimited. We have to always assign resources around the world including military resources on the basis of what serves the national interest." — Secretary of State Marco Rubio
Okay, let's just go to questions. Are you ready? Let's go. Hi, Mr. Secretary. Hi, yeah. Thanks for doing this. I wanted to ask you first about your meeting yesterday with the Pope Leo. President Trump has had very harsh language and words for the Pope against the backdrop of the war in Iran. Did you make amends with the Pope yesterday? No, we had a very good meeting. I mean, ultimately it's important, first of all, we—there's a lot that we work together with, with the church, and we talked about those areas that we're working together on in different parts of the world. I know everyone's interested in, you know, the other aspects of it, but I expressed, you know, updated them on the situation with Iran. I expressed our point of view about why this was an important and the danger that Iran poses to the world, which is largely recognized. And obviously the church has always interacted on behalf of a mission for peace and—and a—respect for all of humanity. We're prepared to do more. In fact, we've offered the regime there 100 million dollars of humanitarian aid that unfortunately so far they have not agreed to distribute to help the people of Cuba. Why would I tell you what I'm going to recommend to the President? But beyond that, the President will always speak clearly about how he feels about the US and US policy. No. I—here's—here's the bottom line on that is that the church is an important global institution, it has a presence all over the world. I know you guys are fixated on who said what about who at what time. I understand all that, okay? Mr. Secretary, can you say in your meeting with Prime Minister Meloni whether you talked about the possibility of withdrawing US troops from Italy as well as the possibility of the United States withdrawing from NATO altogether? No, we—I—we didn't discuss any specifics like that and that's a decision for the President to make. It's a decision every President makes. The—fact of the matter though is, and I—I've said, you know, publicly and I've said repeatedly: I've been a strong supporter of NATO throughout my career in the Senate and even now, and one of the advantages of being in NATO is that it allows us to have forces deployed in Europe and bases that allow us a logistical ability to project power in—in case of contingencies. Well, we had a contingency. And some countries in Europe, some countries in Europe, like Spain as an example, denied us the use of those bases for a very important contingency that in some ways the denial of those bases actually impeded the mission, not severely, but had a—had a cost and in fact even created some unnecessary dangers. So if the re—if one of the main reasons why the US is in NATO is the ability to have forces deployed in Europe that we could project to other contingencies, and now that's no longer the case, at least when it comes to some NATO members, that's a problem and it has to be examined. But ultimately that's a decision for the President to make. His team and people like myself and others will provide him what those potential options are, but ultimately he'll have to make that decision. He hasn't made those decisions yet. As far as recent news of deployments, like for example the—ones—those were all already ongoing. For example in Germany, the—the—troops that were withdrawn, which represent less than 14 percent of our total troop presence there, that's already pre-programmed. And in fact, all it did was take us back to where we were in 2022. And so there was—there was always a plan to do some shifting within NATO. As far as broader changes, tha—that's—I don't have an announcement for you on that today, but that's a decision for the President to make. Mr. Secretary, your former colleagues on the Hill, Republican senators, believe that troops withdrawal will embolden Putin and will benefit Russia. Do you agree with that? Sorry, troops withdrawal will embolden Putin and benefit Russia. Do you agree with that assessment? Well, in the end, the United States is always—the President is going to always do what's in the national interest. We have global obligations all over the world. We have troop deployments all over the world. And those are constantly shifting and changing. For example, we increased troop presence in NATO in the aftermath of the invasion of Ukraine. So—we're always making these decisions and there were some already previewed in—before any of this had happened in terms of the—base denials. But in the end, look—here the bottom line is: we always, you know, while we may be the most powerful country in the world and we may have the most resources, our resources are not unlimited. We have to always assign resources around the world, including military resources, on the basis of what serves the national interest. That's a decision the President has to make, especially within the context of recent developments by some countries within NATO and the stance they've taken towards the United States. Again, an Iranian agent, Hezbollah, who is victimizing not just Israelis, but Hezbollah's also victimizing Lebanon. Well, I mean, Hezbollah's an Iranian agent, 100 percent. I mean, Hezbollah wouldn't exist without Iran support. So but I think it's possible. I mean, Hez—Hezbollah's been both weakened but still capable of inflicting damage and doing terrorist activities as we've seen. So that's what we're going to focus on. Mr. Secretary, can we come back to Cuba a little bit? The—United States, including yesterday, are ramping up sanctions against—against Cuba, the regime and all that. With the exchange with the Pope, did you feel any convergence of—of views on that and on the US policy? Well, let me clarify something for you. Our sanctions are against a company named GAESA. Okay? This is a holding company set up by generals in Cuba that has generated billions of dollars of revenue, none of which benefits the Cuban people. Not one cent of it benefits the Cuban people. You understand this, right? I don't know if you know this. There's the Cuban government and they have a budget, and then there's this private company that has more money than the government does. None of the money in that company goes to build a single road, a single bridge, provide a single grain of rice to a single Cuban, other than the people that are part of GAESA. So that's what we're sanctioning. Is a company that basically is taking anything that makes money in Cuba and illegally putting it into the pockets of a few regime insiders. So that's not sanctions on the Cuban people because the Cuban people don't benefit from GAESA. It's a sanction against this company that is stealing from the Cuban people to the benefit of a few. And we didn't discuss those sanctions yesterday, but we imposed them yesterday and we're going to be doing more, by the way. Mr. Secretary, sorry. Many people thought that your—your was a mission impossible after the Trump's attack—heavy attacks—heavy attacks on the Pope. Which mission, I'm sorry? The visit—the mission. Yes. What's your judgment at the—at the end of the visit? Mission accomplished? We work with the Vatican on a lot of things. Our relationship between, you know, for example, our mission here and the Vatican, like I told you. I mean, we're working together on humanitarian issues, we're focused on other issues as well. The President's perspective is clear. He thinks that Iran is a threat and that it needs to be addressed. It's—and that position remains unchanged. And—and I think we're capable of having that position and expressing that position clearly and also working cooperatively and—as we have for century—as we have for decades with the Vatican and with the Catholic Church who has a global presence. Did you specifically ask the Pope to—to stop his criticism of the Iran war? I'm not going to discuss what I talked about with the Pope, but I'm not going to ask him to—that's not the purpose of the—I told you guys before I even came here. This was a trip that had been planned even before all of these things had happened. So why how—just as an example. Why would I not want to hear the perspectives of the most important in many cases far-reaching, you know, religious leader in the world on his perspectives about what he saw on his trip to Africa? Or what his bishops and others and agencies are hearing in the Western Hemisphere? President Trump Delivers Remarks in the Rose Garden

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·20:05 ·1.8K views · 2026-05-08
Donald Trump (President, United States)
President Trump utilized a Rose Garden Mother’s Day event to honor "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms," while detailing administration achievements in border security, drug interdiction, and new economic incentives for American families.
TL;DR President Trump utilized a Rose Garden Mother’s Day event to honor "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms," while detailing administration achievements in border security, drug interdiction, and new economic incentives for American families. Border Enforcement Claims: The...

TL;DR

President Trump utilized a Rose Garden Mother’s Day event to honor "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms," while detailing administration achievements in border security, drug interdiction, and new economic incentives for American families.

Border Security and Recognition of "Angel Moms"

  • Border Enforcement Claims: The President asserted that the administration has transformed the southern border from the most dangerous in history to the strongest, claiming "zero" illegal crossings over the last 11 months and the removal of thousands of criminal aliens.
  • Angel Moms Recognition: Trump specifically honored "Angel Moms"—mothers who lost children to crimes committed by undocumented immigrants—praising their resilience in the face of "open border policies."
      • Named Honorees: Mary Ann Mendoza, Tammy Nobles, and Jacqueline Medina were highlighted for their advocacy and personal sacrifice.
  • Criticism of Previous Policies: The President characterized past border management as "stupid borders" run by "stupid people," specifically citing the influx of criminals and the "emptying of prisons" from foreign countries like the Congo.

Anti-Narcotics and Drug Pricing Initiatives

  • Drug Interdiction Statistics: Significant progress was reported in stopping the flow of illegal substances into the U.S.
      • Fentanyl: The President claimed a 59% decrease in the flow of deadly fentanyl across the border.
      • Maritime Interdiction: Reported a 97% reduction in drugs entering the country by sea.
  • Pharmaceutical Cost Reductions: Trump touted the "most favored nations" policy, which he claimed has reduced prescription drug prices by 50% to 90% by ensuring the U.S. pays the lowest price available globally.
  • Legislative Action: Mentioned the signing of the HALT Fentanyl Act as a cornerstone of the mission to end the drug epidemic.

Family Support and Economic Policy

  • Financial Incentives for Parents: The briefing highlighted several initiatives aimed at lowering the cost of raising children:
      • Child Tax Credit: Expanded to $2,200 per child.
      • Newborn Savings: Announced a program to give every American newborn $1,000 automatically to invest for their future.
      • Trump Accounts: Tax-advantaged savings accounts designed to help families set aside money for children.
  • Education and Healthcare:
      • School Choice: Claimed the delivery of the largest expansion of school choice in American history.
      • IVF and Living Costs: Pledged to lower the price of IVF and cut regulatory barriers to make starting a family more affordable.
  • Private Sector Partnership: Thanked the Dell family for contributing over $6.2 billion toward family-centric initiatives.

National Security and Gold Star Tributes

  • Gold Star Families: The President honored mothers of fallen service members, including Janice Chance, mother of Marine Captain Jesse Melton, who died in Afghanistan in 2008.
  • Abbey Gate Families: Specific recognition was given to mothers who lost children during the 2021 withdrawal from Afghanistan at Abbey Gate, which Trump attributed to the "gross incompetence" of the Biden administration.
  • Key Administration Women: Trump recognized Brooke Rollins (Secretary of Agriculture) and Susie Wiles (White House Chief of Staff) for their leadership roles.

White House Infrastructure: The Rose Garden Renovation

  • Functional Upgrades: Trump detailed the replacement of the Rose Garden’s grass with white stone to prevent drainage issues and "muddy shoes" during events, noting the stone’s ability to reflect sunlight and keep the area cool.
  • The Presidential Walk of Fame: The addition of new black granite features and portraits of U.S. presidents from George Washington to the current administration was highlighted as a well-received improvement to the grounds.

***

Notable Quotes

"In their honor we’ve rapidly turned the worst and most dangerous border in the history of our country into the strongest border in American history. We have no people come in during the last 11 months—we have zero, zero."

— President Donald Trump

"We had the highest drug prices in the world by far. Now we have the lowest drug prices in the world. Can you imagine? So we cut them by 50, 60, 70 percent... the fake news doesn't want to write about it, but that's why I talk about it."

— President Donald Trump

"America’s moms are raising the future of our country. You have the most important job there is in America or anyplace else, and you’re doing an incredible job."

— President Donald Trump

Kara Swisher Calls Out Elon Musk and Big Tech’s “Petty, Angry" People | Pivot

Pivot with Kara Swisher and Scott Galloway(236.0K) ·56:25 ·58.9K views · 2026-05-08

This briefing covers the passing of media visionary Ted Turner, a mixed earnings week for legacy media giants Disney, Paramount, and Warner Bros.
TL;DR This briefing covers the passing of media visionary Ted Turner, a mixed earnings week for legacy media giants Disney, Paramount, and Warner Bros. Discovery, and the escalating "petty" legal and corporate drama between Elon Musk and OpenAI. A Captain of Industry: Scott...

TL;DR

This briefing covers the passing of media visionary Ted Turner, a mixed earnings week for legacy media giants Disney, Paramount, and Warner Bros. Discovery, and the escalating "petty" legal and corporate drama between Elon Musk and OpenAI.

The Legacy of Ted Turner and the Evolution of News

  • A Captain of Industry: Scott Galloway and Kara Swisher reflect on the death of CNN founder Ted Turner at age 87, describing him as a "different kind of billionaire" who balanced aggressive entrepreneurship with extreme philanthropy.
      • Key Achievements: Beyond founding the first 24-hour news cycle, Turner won the America’s Cup and famously donated $1 billion to the United Nations.
      • Masculinity Role Model: Galloway highlights Turner as a model for "aspirational masculinity," noting he saw his wealth as an obligation to serve rather than a tool for seeking attention.
  • The Erosion of News Quality: The speakers trace the decline of news from a money-losing "public service" to a profit-driven "screamfest."
      • The Murdoch Factor: Galloway argues that Rupert Murdoch’s insight—that conflict-driven "point/counterpoint" segments drove ratings—eventually led to the hyper-polarized state of modern cable news.

Media Earnings: Disney Rises While Linear Bleeds

  • Disney’s Momentum: Under new CEO Josh D'Amaro, Disney reported strong revenue of $25 billion (up 7%).
      • Streaming Success: Streaming operating income jumped 88% to $582 million, with margins hitting double digits (11%) for the first time.
      • The "Iger" Halo: Galloway notes that while D’Amaro is at the helm, these results are largely the fruit of Bob Iger’s prior strategic shifts.
  • Warner Bros. Discovery (WBD) Struggles: WBD reported a massive $2.9 billion net loss this quarter.
      • The "Worst Acquisition" Title: Galloway suggests Time Warner is the center of the worst value-destruction deals in history (AOL, AT&T, and now WBD).
      • Linear Decline: Domestic pay-TV subscribers dropped 10%, highlighting the continued struggle of traditional networks.
  • Paramount’s Resilience: Paramount beat expectations with $7.35 billion in revenue and added 700,000 subscribers.
      • Content Strategy: Despite rumors of "gutting" the studio, Paramount has doubled its film slate for 2026 following the Skydance deal.

The Musk vs. OpenAI Soap Opera

  • The SpaceX/Anthropic Deal: Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei reports 80x growth, necessitating a massive compute deal with SpaceX AI (formerly xAI).
      • Musk’s About-Face: After previously calling Anthropic "evil," Musk now claims his "evil detector" wasn't triggered after meeting their executives.
  • The Legal Battle: Musk’s lawsuit against OpenAI continues to reveal "loathsome" interpersonal drama via court-ordered text releases.
      • Corporate Infighting: Testimony from Mira Murati and records involving Shivon Zilis suggest a culture of manipulation and lack of trust, though Swisher and Galloway dismiss most of it as "boring" corporate hijinks rather than illegal activity.
      • Contract Reality: Galloway argues Musk has "no legal leg to stand on," characterizing the lawsuit as "seller’s regret" dressed up as a legal argument.

AI in Finance and Crowdfunding Trends

  • The Limits of AI Investing: A recent *Wall Street Journal* experiment showed ChatGPT making basic math errors and providing "confident but flawed" investment advice.
      • The Institutional Gap: Galloway warns that retail investors using $20/month AI tools are bringing a "squirt gun" to a fight against institutional "howitzers" operated by PhDs at firms like Citadel.
  • Prediction: Crowdfunded Acquisitions: Galloway predicts that in the next 12–36 months, a charismatic social media figure will successfully crowdfund the acquisition of a small-to-mid-cap company.
      • Example: A TikToker recently gathered $132 million in non-binding pledges to try and save Spirit Airlines, indicating a shift in how retail investors might organize via AI and social platforms.

— Kara Swisher

"Ted Turner saw his wealth and his seat as an obligation... He was optimizing for service, not for attention."
