Why Silicon Valley Should Build for National Interest | Ben Horowitz on a16z

a16z(272.0K) ·29:03 ·1.6K views · 2026-05-08
Ben Horowitz (Co-founder, a16z) David Ulevitch (Partner, a16z)
Andreessen Horowitz (a16z) is positioning its record $15B fundraise as a strategic bet on American technological dominance, arguing that venture capital must actively support national interests and the U.S.
TL;DR Andreessen Horowitz (a16z) is positioning its record $15B fundraise as a strategic bet on American technological dominance, arguing that venture capital must actively support national interests and the U.S. government to ensure the next century of global progress. The...

TL;DR

Andreessen Horowitz (a16z) is positioning its record $15B fundraise as a strategic bet on American technological dominance, arguing that venture capital must actively support national interests and the U.S. government to ensure the next century of global progress.

The Strategic Necessity of American Technology

  • The AI Revolution: Ben Horowitz emphasizes that just as the Industrial Revolution established American influence, winning the AI revolution is critical for maintaining economic, military, and cultural leadership.
  • Irreplaceable Systems: The U.S. system is unique because its foundational freedoms (e.g., free speech) are viewed as "self-evident" and not granted by the state, making them harder to revoke than in other nations.
  • The Power of Opportunity: A core tenet of "American Dynamism" is the country's unique ability to give individuals a "chance" to contribute, a trait Horowitz argues is rare globally but essential for human advancement.

National Security and the Moral Role of Founders

  • Technological Integration: While China was initially faster at integrating AI into government and military bureaucracy, the U.S. is rapidly catching up due to a surge in entrepreneurial interest and government flexibility.
  • The Ethics of Defense: Horowitz rejects "dime-store morality" regarding military contracts, arguing that tech leaders have a moral obligation to provide the best tools to those risking their lives for the country.
      • The Anthropic "Kerfuffle": He suggests the recent high-profile deal collapse between Anthropic and the Department of War was likely a tactical business withdrawal rather than a deep ethical conflict.
  • State Department Alignment: Founders are cautioned against playing "vibe geopolitics." Horowitz argues that private companies should not presume to know better than the State Department or intelligence agencies regarding global security interests.

The Evolutionary Shift in Venture Capital

  • The Scale Problem: The historical VC model was built to find ~15 companies per year. Because "software is eating the world," almost every interesting new company is now a tech company, requiring VC firms to scale significantly.
  • Organizational Structure: Scaling a firm to 600+ people requires centralized leadership rather than traditional "shared control" partnerships.
      • Reorganization: Traditional firms struggle to adapt because individual partners with voting rights often block necessary structural changes.
  • The Squeezed Middle: Horowitz predicts the industry will bifurcate into "large-scale firms" (like a16z) that cover all sectors and "hyper-specialized firms" (e.g., AI infra, Bio, Crypto). Generalist firms in the middle are expected to struggle.

Building a Global Allied Ecosystem

  • Exporting Dynamism: While the U.S. remains the primary hub for entrepreneurship, a16z is looking to leverage the specific strengths of allies:
      • Mexico: High-quality manufacturing expertise to counter Chinese supply chains.
      • Japan: Leading the world in robotics and industrial automation.
  • Narrative and Optimism: There is a concerning "optimism gap" where 70% of people in China are positive about AI, compared to only 30% in America.
      • The Goal: The U.S. needs to shift the conversation from "AI overlords" to practical outcomes like ending traffic deaths, curing cancer, and eliminating poverty.
"When you're a leader in an industry, then the whole industry, the size of it, the ethics of it, the morality of it, kind of depends on you." — Ben Horowitz
"You're going to decide that those people who are sacrificing their lives, putting their lives on the line to protect us, don't get the best technology? I'm not doing that." — Ben Horowitz
"In the new world... the key to winning isn't not making a mistake, it's being interesting. If you don't say anything interesting, it's not going to get any pickup because there are too many other interesting things out there." — Ben Horowitz
Over 70% of people in China are optimistic about AI, and less than 30% in America were optimistic about AI. We have placed the largest bet in American history on the proposition that this country will win the next century of technology. We won the Industrial Revolution, and we did that because we had superior technology. And here we are on the dawn of a new technological revolution: the AI revolution. Every interesting company that gets started is a technology company now. America does give everybody a chance, and entrepreneurs can really count on that. If America loses its technology edge and this dominance, the entire world will lose as well. What makes the American system irreplaceable for the world at large? Everybody's in the same game, but the rules of the game have changed. And what I mean by that is... So we've had a lot of excellent discussions. We've talked a lot about policy, but we're actually going to start this more on the venture capital and technology side. You recently shared a blog post that the firm has raised over $15 billion in a new fundraising set of funds for the firm, the largest in a16z history. What do you think are the new obligations that come with that kind of scale? We are the largest venture capital firm in the world, and how does that tie into the mission? Yeah, so it's interesting. My old mentor Andy Grove said to me, "Ben, when you're a leader in an industry, then the whole industry—the size of it, the ethics of it, the morality of it—kind of depends on you." And I remember thinking when he said that to me—he was running Intel at the time—I thought, "Wow, I'm glad I'm never going to be the leader of an industry because that's a lot of responsibility." But here I am; here we are. And I think, you know, for us, you have to take a step back and say, "Okay, for the technology industry and particularly for kind of new technology companies, what does it need to be?" And you have to think about it on a large scale. And the way we've been thinking about it is, well, if you go all the way back to humanity, like, what's important for humanity? What advances humanity? And it really kind of comes down to: do people have a chance to contribute? Because when people have a chance to contribute, then they can have an impact, and then they can kind of advance the state of things for everybody. And there's really been no country in the world that comes close to America at giving people a shot, you know, giving them a chance to succeed. And, you know, it's not a completely equal chance. Some people are born rich, some people are born poor, some people have better genetics, some people live in dysfunctional families, and so forth. But a chance. And in most countries, people don't have a chance, or they have a very small chance, a very slim chance. And so that ends up being really important. And then if you think about why America has the influence it has today from a military standpoint, from an economic standpoint, from a cultural standpoint, it comes back to: well, we won the Industrial Revolution. And we did that because we had superior technology. And here we are on the dawn of a new technological revolution, the AI revolution, and are we going to win? And are we going to have that influence? And are we going to be able to give people a shot and kind of to continue to advance humanity? I think that's an open question right now. And so when I think about our role in the industry, it's: what can we do to help America win technologically? That's everything from what we invest in to how we integrate into the government and make it accessible to all the citizens, and integrate with our allies, and so forth. And so it's a big mission, it's a lot of work, and, you know, it's why we had the American Dynamism conference and everything. Yeah, you have said that the American way, you know, giving people a shot, that if we lose this technology race, if America loses its technology edge and this dominance, that the entire world will lose as well. What makes the American system irreplaceable? Why is our technological dominance not just good for America, but actually good for the world at large? Yes, so I think a lot of things go into it. But to me, it always comes back to: there's a line in the Declaration of Independence that is very well-known but also underrated, which is: "We hold these truths to be self-evident." And what does that mean? Well, it means that these truths didn't come from us. They're truths; they are self-evident. They came from a higher thing. They came from God. We didn't make these rules. We didn't give you these rights. We didn't give you this freedom. It came from something else. And that's really, really important because it means, like, if I'm the President, if I'm Congress, no matter who I am, I can't change it. No matter how much I want to change it. And there are many who would like to change some things, who think they know better. We're going to talk about that in a minute. Yeah, and, you know, like, that is a thing. But because of that, those freedoms that you have in America are really, really protected to a degree that they're not in any other country, and they're very hard to lose. And we've seen this... there was just a great talk about free speech. We've seen this with free speech, where countries are like, "Well, you know, maybe that's not such a good idea anymore." But if it didn't come from people, it can't be taken away from people. And that's been very, very powerful and very persistent, I think, over the entire history of the country. And it's really unique. There are not other countries who have quite as strong a version of that. Absolutely. You've seen since we've launched... it's now been four plus years since we launched the American Dynamism practice. We're on our second fund. You and Marc have been incredible supporters of this effort, of investing in the national interest at the intersection of advanced software and hardware and, you know, rebuilding not just the defense industrial base but manufacturing and all the precursors that go along with it. So what's been most surprising? What do you think the biggest learning has been? Well, you know, it's been interesting because if you go back to kind of pre-ChatGPT, the conventional wisdom was, you know, China had this big AI lead on us. And then ChatGPT comes out and says, "Oh, well, maybe no, we have a lead on them." And then, you know, things have evolved from there. But the thing that was true about the kind of old incorrect idea was that they were way ahead of us in integrating their AI technology with their government, you know, on a kind of military basis, on a bureaucracy basis, you know, on all facets. And so when we started, we were coming from, I would say, very far behind, you know, in that idea. The thing that's been surprising, though, is like how fast we've been catching up. And I think that that's been really kind of heartening from both the number of entrepreneurs that want to help and then the willingness of the U.S. government to say, "Hey, we're interested in these new technologies, we're interested in these new companies. We're not stuck in our ways. We'll change the rules if we have to." And that's been, you know, it's been amazingly fruitful. And I think that, you know, we're seeing it in the kind of current conflicts that we have, and it gives me great hope that if we keep at it, that we'll be very strong going forward. Yeah, I agree. I think this is a moment of optimism to see just how quickly things can shift when there's a sense of urgency and a sense of change-makers. I want to talk about AI and policy. We had Emil Michael earlier, the Under Secretary of War for research and development, CTO of the Department of War here. He was obviously in the news talking about Anthropic over the last week. But this isn't an issue... it may have bubbled up to the mainstream news in the last week, but this has been an ongoing issue. Yeah, so look, I think to me the thing on the whole Anthropic kerfuffle that nobody's really said out loud, but is the obvious thing that happened, is if you understand deals, particularly software deals, you realize that that deal did not fall apart because of philosophical differences. It fell apart because Anthropic wanted out of the deal. And you know that because they had all the leverage. You know, they were already deployed. We were about to go to war. Like, nobody's ever had more leverage than that in a software deal. So anything reasonable that they would have asked for, they would have gotten. Like, even probably beyond what they should have. But the fact that they, you know, found something they could walk away on, and then didn't return Emil's calls for whatever—the three calls and then the call to the admin or all those things that happen—it's just: why would you not return the call if you wanted in the deal? They just wanted out of the deal. Now, I don't know why they wanted out of the deal, whether it was an employee thing or a Dario thing or what have you, but that's what happened. And I think the fact that it's getting reported on like there's some ethical conflict... I don't think there is. I think that—and in fact, if you wanted somebody to use your AI safely, nobody has more rules than the U.S. government, particularly the Department of War. And nobody is more... by the way, and then since I'm speaking to a Washington crowd, you know this: if you were to break those rules, there is a 100% chance it's going to be leaked to the press immediately. Like, there are no secrets in Washington. So that whole thing was a little bit surreal to me to watch, at least at this point. You know, it's not like everybody has agreed on peace worldwide. And it's not as if everybody's agreed not to commit crime ever. So, like, we do have crime, we do have war, we have these things. And then you have to think about, okay, the people that we grew up with who have gone into the military, risked their lives to save our lives: you are making the call because you are better ethically and you know more about geopolitics than the people in the State Department and the people running the Department of War and the people running the intelligence agencies? Like, if you're going to basically bet your whole life on an idea and build something, you kind of have to believe that the government isn't just going to arbitrarily take it away from you at any point. And it's going to be very, very exciting to see that play out and for sure a lot of our companies and a lot of our allies will have a role to play. In a recent deep dive, you know, Packy McCormick did this long deep dive on the firm. Thought it came out pretty well. He described the firm as a power broker using capital and networks to shape markets and influence. How do you view that and balance that power with the need to remain authentic to our founders, to institutions? How do we leverage that power? You know, obviously we're here in DC. How do you think about that responsibility and how do we use it to advance the state of the art? Well, kind of what you lack as an entrepreneur is power. So you have a great invention, you may have built a great technology, but you don't have the power to call anybody, you don't have the power to kind of get the right meeting necessarily with the right people in Congress to kind of help get the regulation correct. You don't have the power to get an audience with the right CEO to buy your product and that kind of thing. So power is sort of a feature of our offering is the way I think about it. And that's very different than the culture that we want to have and want to project internally. And as you know, kind of our very first kind of cultural idea is first-class business and only in a first-class way. And what that means when it comes to entrepreneurs is the ultimate respect for what they're doing. It doesn't mean we're always their best friend or anything like that, but we show up on time, we get back to them in a timely fashion, we're honest, we do all the little things to make sure that our overall behavior isn't "we're the powerful and you're the weak" because, you know, that obviously would be a short ride for our firm at the top. What do you think about, you know, as people watch us raising so much capital? It happened during a period where I think broader the VC industry had slowed down fundraising, but we closed very quickly, we raised a massive amount of capital. Do you think that signals anything about where venture is going as an ecosystem, as an industry? What do you think it looks like in the state of venture capital today? Yeah, so this is something that we've seen coming for a long time and that we kind of prepared for. If you go back to when we started the firm, there was a study that was done that showed that in any given year, there were basically approximately 15 companies that would be started that would ever achieve $100 million a year in revenue. And so the whole idea of venture capital was that you had to get in as many of those 15 as possible, and you really didn't want to be in anything that wasn't one of those. And so the way venture capital firms were constructed was as these partnerships in both economics and control that were small. Because why have, you know, 100 or 500 or 600 people like we do now, if you're only going after 15 companies? It doesn't make sense. But what we saw back then was, well, even though that had been true for decades, it wasn't going to be true anymore because as Marc wrote, software's eating the world. And so what that meant is that there was going to be no industry, no company that wasn't going to be better if there was a great software team at its core. And, you know, that's even evolving now into AI. And so basically every interesting company that gets started is a technology company now. And so there are many, many more interesting companies, and so in order to reach it, you had to be able to scale your organization. Now, here's the problem. If you were one of those venture capital firms that started all those years ago, you were likely set up, as I said, as a partnership with shared economics and shared control. If you have shared control, it's very hard to reorganize. Because, and you know this David as a CEO, to reorganize you need a single decision-maker. There is no way to reorganize if everybody gets a vote. It's going to be bad. I could write a whole book on just reorgs. But reorgs are essentially redistributions of power. When you redistribute power, people are mad. If they get a vote, they're going to foul that reorganization. And you can't scale without reorging. So as a result, like, we've been able to scale because we have centralized control, but you know, most of our kind of competitors have not been able to scale. Like, a very few have. And so I think what we have now is large firms who can cover all the technological areas and then specialized firms that are: "Okay, I'm a specialist in AI infrastructure," or "I'm a specialist in bio," or "I'm a specialist in crypto," or "I'm a specialist in games," but I'm not doing all of that. And the kind of firms in the middle, I think, are getting squeezed out. I think that's right. People sometimes ask me, like, "How do you work at such a massive firm, you know, 600 plus people?" And I say, "Look, well, we're actually a bunch of really small teams that leverage a bunch of shared platform services, much like our portfolio companies." And that allows us to move with urgency but have the might of a very, very large organization behind us. I think one of the other things that I think is interesting that people don't always realize about the firm is there's probably more CEOs as general partners inside the firm than there's got to be than any other firm. And I think all of us recognize: having a hierarchy and a benevolent dictatorship is really appealing. You know, it works. Yeah, it works. And, you know, it makes our life easy. My job: focus on American Dynamism. It's very simple. You know, Martine's job: focus on infra and being the best you can be, figure out what we need. And then it really is a dynamic that I think these death-by-committee kind of venture firms just cannot adapt to. No, definitely. You've talked in a recent podcast about how the media landscape has shifted, how reputation can shape markets. We had Alex Karp here earlier today. He is phenomenal about going direct to his customers. How do you think that shift... it seems like we're seeing that shift in some ways in the government, Emil Michael going on X directly. How would you coach founders and even maybe coach people that are in the government, where narrative and trust is paramount in terms of communicating and thinking about media and communications? Yeah, so look, I think that the media world has changed and it can be confusing because I think that you don't need to think about it in terms of necessarily old media and new media, in that everybody's in the same game, but the rules of the game have changed. And what I mean by that is in the old world, the key to a media strategy was defense. And the reason that the key was defense is: there's a limited number of channels. Those channels have very strict formats. So if you're in the New York Times or the Wall Street Journal, you can get a few quotes in and then you'll be characterized on the rest of what you say. If you're on CNN, you get, like, a short and hostile conversation with a bright light in your face, and so forth. And then because there were a limited number of channels, if you said something wrong, you couldn't erase it. It was kind of like on the permanent record. And there are kind of many cases of these old-school gaffes, like Howard Dean and so forth, where you could never erase it and you could never get over it and so forth. In the new kind of world, there's unlimited channels, there's unlimited formats. And so the key to winning isn't not making a mistake, it's being interesting. Like if you don't say anything that's interesting, it's not going to get any pickup because there are too many other interesting things out there. People like Alex Karp and Donald Trump and so forth who are just like an entertaining show... like Alex was super entertaining out here. I was captivated. I didn't know what he was going to say next. I don't know if he knew what he was going to say next, but it was really... he is very entertaining. And that wins. And then like if you make a mistake, you just do 10 podcasts tomorrow and flood the zone. And it doesn't matter whether the channel is the Wall Street Journal or a podcast or whatever. It's all the same thing now. Because that's the way... you're overwhelmed with media. And so to win you have to be interesting. And then, you know, that doesn't mean you can just be all over the place and not be on message. Alex is always very, very, very consistent, pro-America. Like that's the thing that you can always count on him to be, and that's his main message. And that's great. And he's interesting about it. “Weapons of Mass Disruption” with NYT’s Tom Friedman - 05/08/26 | Audio Only

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·25:38 ·1.3K views · 2026-05-08
Tom Friedman (Columnist, New York Times) Dan Murphy (Reporter, CNBC) Andrew Ross Sorkin (Host, CNBC)
As military tensions escalate between the U.S.
TL;DR As military tensions escalate between the U.S. and Iran in the Strait of Hormuz, columnist Tom Friedman warns that the shift from the "Age of Information" to the "Age of Intelligence" necessitates an urgent U.S.-China AI arms control agreement to prevent "weapons of mass...

TL;DR

As military tensions escalate between the U.S. and Iran in the Strait of Hormuz, columnist Tom Friedman warns that the shift from the "Age of Information" to the "Age of Intelligence" necessitates an urgent U.S.-China AI arms control agreement to prevent "weapons of mass disruption" from enabling catastrophic global cyberattacks.

Geopolitical Volatility and the "Nacho" Trade

  • Strait of Hormuz Conflict: Dan Murphy reports a direct exchange of fire between U.S. and Iranian forces, including missile and drone attacks on U.S. Navy destroyers.
      • The "Nacho" Trade: Market analysts have moved from the "Taco" trade to the "Nacho" trade (Not A Chance Hormuz Opens), pricing in higher oil prices for longer as skepticism grows regarding a quick resolution.
      • Project Freedom: Saudi Arabia and Kuwait have granted the U.S. military increased access to bases, potentially allowing the U.S.-led effort to secure the Strait to restart as early as this weekend.
  • U.S.-Iran Diplomacy: President Trump characterized recent military strikes as a "love tap" while warning Iran of more violent retaliation if they do not agree to a proposed 14-point framework.

The AI Revolution: "Weapons of Mass Disruption"

  • Phase Change in Geopolitics: Tom Friedman argues the world is moving from the "Age of Information" (human-led tools) to the "Age of Intelligence" (intent-based systems), which changes the "physics of geopolitics."
  • The "Mythos" Model: Friedman highlights Anthropic’s new model, which can identify and exploit code vulnerabilities at a speed and scale no human can match.
      • Risk of Proliferation: With natural language instructions, a single actor with a laptop and a Starlink terminal could potentially compromise critical infrastructure like water or electricity systems.
  • The Case for Arms Control: Friedman compares the current AI landscape to the 1940s nuclear race, but with private companies (Meta, OpenAI, Google) holding the "weapons." He advocates for a U.S.-China limited domain agreement similar to Cold War-era nuclear arms control.
  • Surging Support for Regulation: Paul Tudor Jones recently noted that while only 20% of AI industry leaders supported regulation last year, that figure has jumped to 80% today.

Corporate AI Milestones and Valuations

  • Anthropic’s Massive Valuation: The company is reportedly fielding interest for a fundraise that could value it at $900 billion, potentially surpassing OpenAI.
      • Revenue Growth: Anthropic’s annualized revenue is projected to grow from $9 billion at the end of last year to over $45 billion shortly.
  • OpenAI’s Cyber Defense: OpenAI is rolling out a vetted cybersecurity model designed to help teams identify vulnerabilities and analyze malware, responding to the competitive pressure from Anthropic's "Mythos."
  • Apple’s Hardware Evolution: Apple is reportedly developing AirPods with built-in cameras to act as "eyes" for Siri, signaling a move toward "persistent AI helpers" that can see and process a user's surroundings in real-time.

U.S. Trade Strategy and the "Turns" of History

  • Trump’s Tariff Setback: A U.S. trade court ruled that the President’s 10% global tariff is not allowed under Section 122 of the Trade Act of 1974. However, the ruling currently only affects two private importers during the appeal process.
  • European Ultimatum: Trump has given the EU until July 4th to ratify a trade agreement, threatening significantly higher tariffs if a deal is not reached.
  • Upcoming China Summit: President Trump will visit Beijing next week accompanied by a "slimmed-down" delegation of CEOs, including Kelly Ortberg (Boeing) and Jane Fraser (Citigroup).
  • Energy and Innovation: Friedman laments the U.S. falling behind China in "Clean Tech" (EVs and batteries).
      • Winning in the Turns: Friedman cites the Indian tech firm Infosys’s philosophy that companies "win in the turns"—major technological shifts. He warns that while the U.S. leads in AI, it is being overtaken in the green energy "turn."

"Unacceptable": Marco Rubio says Iran must never get nuclear weapons in briefing to media

Sky News(9.2M) ·23:08 ·7.4K views · 2026-05-08
Marco Rubio (Secretary of State, United States)
> "The red line is clear.
TL;DR: Secretary of State Marco Rubio declared that Iran’s pursuit of nuclear weapons and attempts to control international waterways are "unacceptable," while affirming that the U.S. will maintain a policy of immediate military retaliation against any threats to American...

TL;DR: Secretary of State Marco Rubio declared that Iran’s pursuit of nuclear weapons and attempts to control international waterways are "unacceptable," while affirming that the U.S. will maintain a policy of immediate military retaliation against any threats to American personnel or assets.

Iran and Global Maritime Security

  • Nuclear Stance: Rubio emphasized that preventing Iran from obtaining nuclear weapons is a global imperative, noting that while many presidents have stated this, the current administration is taking "concrete" action to prevent it.
  • Negotiation Status: The U.S. is currently awaiting a response from Tehran regarding a potential negotiation process. Rubio described the Iranian system as "fractured and dysfunctional," which may be delaying the diplomatic timeline.
  • Operation Epic Fury: Rubio clarified that while the offensive phase of "Operation Epic Fury"—which targeted Iranian missile launchers, naval assets, and factories—is over, the U.S. continues to engage in defensive strikes.
      • Defensive Retaliation: Rubio dismissed calls for a ceasefire in the context of self-defense, stating that any drone or missile fired at U.S. destroyers will be met with immediate force.
  • International Waterways: The Secretary raised alarms over Iran's attempts to "normalize" control over international straits.
      • Precedent Risk: He warned that allowing Iran to control the Strait of Hormuz would set a dangerous precedent for other nations to seize international waterways.
      • UN Action: The U.S. is pushing a UN Security Council resolution to condemn the mining and seizing of the straits.

Diplomatic Relations with the Vatican

  • Cordial Meeting with the Pope: Despite past friction between the Vatican and the administration, Rubio described his meeting with Pope Leo as "productive" and "very cordial."
  • Strategic Information Exchange: The briefing focused on the Church’s unique insights into global regions where it has a deep presence, including:
      • Africa: Concerns regarding the spread of radical Islamic terrorism threatening growing Christian populations.
      • Lebanon: The plight of Christian communities and the role of the Church in facilitating peace.
      • Venezuela: Insights from Venezuelan bishops who had recently visited the Vatican.
  • Humanitarian Partnership: Rubio highlighted the Catholic Church (specifically Caritas) as a vital interlocutor and facilitator for U.S. humanitarian aid in regions where direct government-to-government cooperation is failing.

U.S. Military Posture and NATO

  • Troop Presence in Europe: No specific decisions have been made regarding the withdrawal of troops from Italy or a departure from NATO, as these remain presidential prerogatives.
  • Base Usage Concerns: Rubio expressed frustration with NATO allies (specifically citing Spain) who denied the U.S. use of bases for recent "contingencies."
      • Strategic Re-evaluation: He argued that if NATO bases cannot be used for power projection during emergencies, the U.S. must examine the cost and utility of those deployments.
  • German Deployment: He clarified that the recent withdrawal of troops from Germany (representing less than 14% of the presence there) was a pre-programmed shift that returned troop levels to 2022 status.

Regional Policy: Cuba, Lebanon, and Sudan

  • Cuba Sanctions and Aid: Rubio defended U.S. policy toward Cuba, clarifying that sanctions specifically target GAESA, a military-controlled holding company that "steals" from the Cuban people.
      • Blocked Aid: He revealed the U.S. offered $100 million in humanitarian aid to Cuba, but the regime has refused to allow its distribution.
  • Lebanon and Hezbollah: Rubio identified Hezbollah as a "100% Iranian agent" that victimizes the Lebanese people. He called on Italy to help equip the legitimate Lebanese government to confront this threat and cut off illicit financing.
  • Sudan Crisis: Rubio characterized the war in Sudan as one of the world's worst humanitarian disasters, acting as a proxy war for multiple nations. The U.S. is currently pushing for a "humanitarian ceasefire" and safe haven communities for aid distribution.
  • Taiwan Stability: Ahead of an upcoming trip to China, Rubio stated that U.S. policy remains unchanged: the U.S. opposes any forced or compelled change to the status quo and advocates for stability in the Taiwan Strait.

Notable Quotes

Marco Rubio BREAKS DOWN America’s GLOBAL GEOPOLITICS in MASTERFUL Press Conference

Diario AS(2.5M) ·23:03 ·152 views · 2026-05-08
Marco Rubio (Secretary of State, United States)
Secretary Marco Rubio emphasized a "U.S.
TL;DR Secretary Marco Rubio emphasized a "U.S. interest first" foreign policy, issuing a stern warning to Iran against seizing international waterways while leveraging the Catholic Church’s global influence to facilitate humanitarian aid in Cuba and Africa. Meeting with Pope...

TL;DR

Secretary Marco Rubio emphasized a "U.S. interest first" foreign policy, issuing a stern warning to Iran against seizing international waterways while leveraging the Catholic Church’s global influence to facilitate humanitarian aid in Cuba and Africa.

Vatican Relations and Humanitarian Strategy

  • Meeting with Pope Leo: Secretary Rubio described a "cordial and productive" meeting focused on shared global interests, including the protection of Christian populations in Africa and the Middle East.
      • Institutional Insights: Rubio noted that the Church serves as a unique "interlocutor" and information source in regions like Venezuela and Lebanon where U.S. direct access may be limited.
  • Humanitarian Aid in Cuba: Rubio highlighted the U.S. offer of $100 million in humanitarian aid to Cuba, which he claims the current regime has refused to distribute.
      • Targeted Sanctions: He clarified that U.S. sanctions specifically target GAESA, a holding company run by Cuban generals, rather than the Cuban people.
      • The Church’s Role: The Catholic Church (specifically Caritas) remains the primary vehicle for distributing the $6 million in aid that has successfully reached the island.

Iran and Maritime Security

  • The "Red Line" on Aggression: Rubio stated that while "Operation Epic Fear" (the offensive phase) is over, the U.S. will maintain a strictly defensive but lethal posture.
      • Direct Warning: Rubio asserted that any Iranian forces threatening U.S. destroyers would be "blown up," regardless of existing "ceasefire" talk.
  • Strait of Hormuz: Rubio expressed grave concern over reports that Iran is attempting to establish a regulatory agency to control traffic in the straits.
      • International Precedent: He warned that "normalizing" a single nation’s control over an international waterway is "unacceptable" and would set a dangerous global precedent.
  • Negotiation Status: The U.S. is currently awaiting a formal response from Tehran to initiate a serious negotiation process, though Rubio noted the Iranian system remains "fractured and dysfunctional."

NATO and European Troop Deployments

  • Base Access Friction: Rubio signaled a potential reassessment of U.S. troop presence based on the cooperation of host nations.
      • The Spain Example: He cited instances where NATO allies, specifically Spain, denied the U.S. use of bases during "important contingencies," arguing this undermines the primary logistical reason for a U.S. presence in Europe.
  • Troop Shifting: He clarified that recent troop withdrawals in Germany (representing less than 14% of the total presence) were "pre-programmed" and merely returned levels to those seen in 2022.
  • National Interest: Rubio maintained that while he supports NATO, military resources are not unlimited and must be assigned based on current national interests and the stance of member nations toward the U.S.

Regional Conflicts: Lebanon, Sudan, and Ukraine

  • Lebanon and Hezbollah: The U.S. goal is to empower the legitimate Lebanese government to confront Hezbollah, which Rubio characterized as a "100 percent Iranian agent" victimizing the Lebanese people.
  • Sudan Crisis: Rubio detailed U.S. involvement in the "Quad" process, seeking a humanitarian ceasefire. He accused various third-party countries of using their territories to ship weaponry, turning the conflict into a "proxy engagement."
  • Ukraine Mediation: Rubio stated that U.S. mediation efforts have "stagnated" but reaffirmed that the U.S. stands ready to facilitate a diplomatic resolution whenever both sides are prepared for a "fruitful outcome."

Notable Quotes

"The red line is clear: if they threaten Americans, they're going to get blown up... Only stupid countries don't shoot back when they're shot at, and we're not a stupid country." — Secretary Marco Rubio
"Are you going to normalize a country claiming to control an international waterway? Because if you normalize that, you've set a precedent that's going to get repeated in a dozen other places." — Secretary Marco Rubio
"Hezbollah wouldn't exist without Iran's support... Lebanon should be governed by the Lebanese government; it should not have a terrorist group operating within its national territory." — Secretary Marco Rubio

President Donald Trump delivers remarks at the White House — 5/8/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·20:25 ·12.8K views · 2026-05-08
Donald Trump (President, United States)
President Trump delivered a Mother’s Day address honoring "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms" while highlighting his administration’s claims of achieving zero illegal border crossings, drastic reductions in drug prices, and new financial incentives for American families.
TL;DR President Trump delivered a Mother’s Day address honoring "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms" while highlighting his administration’s claims of achieving zero illegal border crossings, drastic reductions in drug prices, and new financial incentives for American families. ...

TL;DR

President Trump delivered a Mother’s Day address honoring "Angel Moms" and "Gold Star Moms" while highlighting his administration’s claims of achieving zero illegal border crossings, drastic reductions in drug prices, and new financial incentives for American families.

Border Security and "Angel Moms"

  • Zero Illegal Entries: The President claimed that for the past 11 months, illegal border crossings have reached "zero," contrasting this with previous "open border" policies that he alleged allowed 25 million people and nearly 12,000 murderers into the country.
  • Angel Moms Recognition: Trump honored "Angel Moms"—mothers who lost children to crimes committed by undocumented immigrants—specifically naming Mary Ann Mendoza, Tammy Nobles, and Jacqueline Medina.
  • Policy Shift: He attributed the lack of current crossings to a shift in messaging and enforcement, stating that migrants no longer attempt the journey because they know "they’re not going to get through."

Drug Epidemic and Interdiction

  • Supply Reductions: Reported that fentanyl flow across the border is down by 59%, while illegal drugs entering by sea have dropped by 97%.
  • HALT Fentanyl Act: Highlighted the signing of the HALT Fentanyl Act as a primary tool in stopping the "poison" that causes an estimated 200,000 American deaths annually.
  • Cartel Influence: Noted that while the sea routes are largely secured, land routes remain a challenge due to Mexican cartels "ruling" the region.

Gold Star Families and Military Remembrance

  • Abbey Gate Tribute: Trump honored mothers of service members killed during the 2021 Abbey Gate attack in Afghanistan, including Alicia Lopez and Paula Knauss Selph, while criticizing the previous administration for "gross incompetence" regarding the withdrawal.
  • Marine Captain Jesse Melton: Shared the story of Captain Melton, who died in Afghanistan in 2008 after volunteering to take a fellow Marine's place so his comrade could be present for his daughter's birth.
  • Administration Personnel: Recognized female leaders within his administration, including Secretary of Agriculture Brooke Rollins and Chief of Staff Susie Wiles.

Economic and Social Policy for Families

  • Newborn Investment Grants: Announced a policy giving every American newborn $1,000 to invest for their future, bolstered by a $6.25 billion contribution from the Dell family.
  • Family Financial Support:
      • Tax Credits: Cited the expansion of the Child Tax Credit to $2,200 per child.
      • "Trump Accounts": Introduced tax-advantaged savings accounts to help families save for their children's futures.
      • Healthcare: Claimed a 70–90% reduction in prescription drug prices by implementing "Most Favored Nations" pricing, ensuring the U.S. pays the lowest global price.
  • Social Stance: Reaffirmed his administration's commitment to school choice, keeping biological men out of women's sports, and banning "transgender mutilation" of children.

Rose Garden and White House Renovations

  • Infrastructure Improvements: Described the renovation of the Rose Garden, replacing grass (which was often muddy due to the area being a wetland) with white stone and black granite to accommodate events and media.
  • Presidential Walk of Fame: Highlighted a new installation featuring portraits of U.S. Presidents from George Washington to the present.

***

Notable Quotes

"In securing our border we've also made tremendous strides in stopping the flow of illegal drugs into our country, the flood of deadly fentanyl across our border is down by 59 percent and the drugs coming in by sea are down by 97 percent."

— President Donald Trump

"We are giving every American newborn child $1,000 automatically to invest and to grow over the course of their life."

— President Donald Trump

"For 28 years drug prices and prescription drugs only went in one direction... Now we have the lowest drug prices in the world... we cut them by 50, 60, 70 percent."

— President Donald Trump

"America's moms are raising... the future of our country. You have the most important job there is in America or anyplace else."

— President Donald Trump

S&P Hits New Highs as Tech Earnings Crush Recession Fears | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(172.0K) ·27:41 ·6.6K views · 2026-05-08
Steve Eisman (Host, The Weekly Wrap)
> "The overall story, in my view, continues to be a K-shaped market, reflecting the underlying K-shaped economy...
TL;DR (1-2 sentences): Despite geopolitical tensions in Iran and elevated valuations, the S&P 500 continues to hit new highs because all 11 sectors are beating revenue estimates and profit margins have reached an all-time high of 20%. The market is currently "K-shaped," driven...

TL;DR (1-2 sentences): Despite geopolitical tensions in Iran and elevated valuations, the S&P 500 continues to hit new highs because all 11 sectors are beating revenue estimates and profit margins have reached an all-time high of 20%. The market is currently "K-shaped," driven upward by dominant tech, industrial power, and financial sectors while the lower-end consumer and software-specific stocks struggle.

Macroeconomic Health & The "K-Shaped" Reality

  • Recession Indicators: Steve Eisman argues that a recession is not imminent because revenue and earnings growth continue to exceed analyst expectations across the board.
      • Revenue Growth: Actual S&P 500 revenue growth reached 10.5% for Q1 2026, significantly higher than the 7.5% predicted at the start of the year.
      • Margin Expansion: S&P 500 operating margins are at a record 20%, up from 16% in 2023. Eisman notes it is difficult to be bearish when margins expand alongside revenue.
  • The K-Shaped Economy: Market strength is concentrated in specific "upward" sectors while others languish.
      • The "Up" Side: Led by Tech, Industrials related to power, and Financials.
      • The "Down" Side: Middle to lower-end consumers are struggling, and the housing market remains "effectively flat."
  • Market Concentration: Info Tech now represents 35% of the S&P 500; when adding tech-adjacent firms like Google, Meta, and Amazon, the total reaches nearly 50%. This concentration means the index can rise as long as tech performs, even if the broader consumer environment is weak.

Tech Performance: Hardware Wins, Software Struggles

  • AI Hardware Strength: Companies building the infrastructure for AI continue to report massive growth.
      • AMD: Reported Q1 sales growth of 38% and projected strong Q2 sales of $11.2 billion, sending the stock up 15%.
      • Arista Networks: Posted a 34% increase in EPS, though the stock cooled due to high prior valuations.
      • Rockwell Automation: Reported a strong beat with 9% organic sales growth, benefiting from the build-out of AI data centers.
  • The "Software Problem": Despite strong numbers, software stocks are being punished by "AI fears" and valuation resets.
      • Palantir: Revenue grew 85% and EPS rose 154%, yet the stock remained pressured by a negative AI narrative.
      • Shopify: Down over 30% YTD due to slowing revenue guidance and general software sector malaise.
  • Payment Sector Volatility: Competition is intensifying as Apple and Google Pay erode traditional players.
      • PayPal & Fiserv: Both companies reported poor guidance and organic growth issues; Eisman views Visa and Mastercard as the only "impregnable" franchises in the space.

Logistics, Energy, & Corporate Disruptions

  • Amazon vs. The World: Amazon is opening its internal distribution network to non-Amazon sellers, directly competing with FedEx and UPS in freight and parcel shipping.
  • The GameStop/eBay Bid: GameStop made a surprise $60 billion bid for eBay (a company four times its size).
      • Expert Reaction: Eisman expects eBay to reject the deal; notably, Michael Burry sold his entire GameStop position following the news due to concerns over debt levels.
  • Energy & Geopolitics: Oil prices surged following attacks in the Strait of Hormuz but settled below $100 as the market adopted a "glass half-full" view of the Iran conflict.
      • Diamondback Energy: Increased production targets due to higher prices, with EPS expected to grow over 100% in Q2.

Financials & Private Credit Risks

  • Apollo’s Transparency Move: In a major industry shift, Apollo announced it will provide daily pricing for its investment-grade corporate fixed income and direct lending assets by September.
  • Private Equity Warning: Eisman remains concerned about private equity's overexposure to software.
      • Valuation Gap: With public software stocks down significantly, many private companies may be worth half of their original investment, potentially forcing lenders to demand more equity.
  • FICO Controversy: Eisman highlighted a 1,500% increase in the price of FICO reports over the last five years, calling it one of the most "egregious monopolistic acts" in the lending ecosystem.

Notable Quotes

"The overall story, in my view, continues to be a K-shaped market, reflecting the underlying K-shaped economy... As the lower part of the K spending shrinks, the percentage impact on the S&P shrinks because tech dominates the S&P to an incredible degree." — Steve Eisman
"It's more difficult to take a negative view on the economic outlook when margins are expanding with revenue." — Steve Eisman
"Anything, anything in software is problematic because of AI fears... even though the company reported mostly a good quarter, the negative AI narrative still dominates." — Steve Eisman (on Palantir and Shopify)
"The most important statement I can make about Disney is that the stock price is where it was 10 years ago." — Steve Eisman

Let's look at recession probability from a different angle: revenue and earnings growth and margins. The first quarter of 2026 is almost completely printed. And a great measure of market direction and economy strength is to look closely at how well or poorly the analysts predicted the actual revenue growth and earnings growth of all the companies in the S&P. By May 4th, Monday of this week, over 300 of the 500 companies in the S&P had reported, which gives us a great opportunity to compare what the analysts thought would happen. In every sector, revenue growth beat estimates. The clearest way to say it is that all 11 sectors—I'm going to say that again—all 11 sectors of the S&P posted stronger 1Q growth in revenue than what analysts had assumed on January 1st. The pessimism on revenue growth that some investors had turned out to be unwarranted even in the context of the war in Iran. Now if we look at earnings as opposed to revenue, we see a similar pattern. The earnings of eight out of 11 of these sectors mirrored the revenue growth and beat the market's estimates. Only three sectors—staples, energy, and healthcare—disappointed relative to where earnings estimates were on January 1st. These three sectors were the hardest to handicap because of the war, tariffs, and structural problems in healthcare. Now let's look at margins. The estimate for the next 12 months S&P 500 operating margin now stands at an all-time high of 20%. It was 16% in 2023 and has been in a straight upward trajectory ever since. If only revenues were expanding, an argument could be made that the channels are being stuffed. It's more difficult to take a negative view on the economic outlook when margins are expanding with revenue. The overall story, in my view, continues to be a K-shaped market, reflecting the underlying K-shaped economy. The market and GDP growth are led upward by tech, industrials related to power, and financials. Yes, the middle to lower end of the consumer is having a difficult time. The info tech sector represents 35% of the S&P, and if you then add companies that are not technically in info tech, like Google, Meta, and Amazon, you quickly get to around 50%. Looking forward to your response! Warm regards, Mert." These are great questions. My answer: There is no question that by any traditional measurement, the market is expensive. The Shiller PE, the Market Cap to GDP metric, or even just the plain current market multiple, they all point to the same thing: expensiveness. But there is a reason why the market looks expensive. Tech has become an increasing percentage of the market. Today, info tech is 35% of the S&P, an all-time high. If you add companies like Amazon, Google, Meta and other tech-related names like I said before that are not in info tech, you get to around 50%. If we just focus on info tech, in April 2016, it was 20% of the S&P. In April 2020, it was 25%, and now it is 35%. Because they are growth stocks, tech stocks sell at higher multiples. Thus, as tech has become a higher percentage of the market, by definition the market multiple, no matter which metric you choose, has to increase. That's why I'm not overly concerned that the market looks expensive. As long as tech continues to perform, the market will be fine and the indices heavily weighted with tech will be fine as well. BONUS: Nebius’s Arkady Volozh | On building in the agentic era | Spotlight On | AccelVC

Accel(26.6K) ·31:52 ·1.6K views · 2026-05-08
Arkady Volozh (Founder, Nebius) Matt Weigand (Partner, Accel)
Nebius is positioning itself as a vertically integrated "AI-native hyperscaler," controlling the entire stack from land and power to agentic software to provide cost-effective, high-performance infrastructure for the next generation of AI developers.
TL;DR Nebius is positioning itself as a vertically integrated "AI-native hyperscaler," controlling the entire stack from land and power to agentic software to provide cost-effective, high-performance infrastructure for the next generation of AI developers. The Five-Layer...

TL;DR

Nebius is positioning itself as a vertically integrated "AI-native hyperscaler," controlling the entire stack from land and power to agentic software to provide cost-effective, high-performance infrastructure for the next generation of AI developers.

Full-Stack Vertical Integration

  • The Five-Layer Strategy: Arkady Volozh defines Nebius’s competitive edge through its ownership of every layer of the AI value chain:
      • Layer 1: Land, Power, and Shell (LPS), including industrial permits and electrical equipment.
      • Layer 2: Proprietary hardware, specifically building their own GPU racks.
      • Layer 3: Bare metal services and Kubernetes layers for wholesale clients.
      • Layer 4: Infrastructure-as-a-Service (IaaS) and platform services for retail cloud users.
      • Layer 5: High-level AI services, including inference (Token Factory) and agentic tools.
  • Efficiency Gains: By avoiding third-party margins for data center construction and rack building, Nebius claims superior economic and engineering efficiency, optimizing the system "down to the last bolt."

Strategic Financing via Hyperscale Partnerships

  • The Meta Commitment: Nebius recently secured a $27 billion commitment from Meta, which Volozh clarifies is structured as two distinct parts:
      • $12 Billion: Direct bare metal capacity provided to Meta.
      • $15 Billion: A "backstop" agreement that Nebius uses as collateral to secure bank financing for its own independent cloud capacity.
  • The Microsoft Precedent: A similar $17 billion+ deal with Microsoft serves as a financial engine, allowing Nebius to use long-term, credit-worthy contracts to build out "spot market" capacity for smaller AI startups.
  • NVIDIA Partnership: Beyond capital, the deepening partnership with NVIDIA involves deep engineering collaboration to optimize systems, positioning Nebius as a preferred partner for large-scale GPU deployments.

Shifting Focus: From Bare Metal to the Agentic Era

  • AI-Native Demand: Nebius is moving beyond selling "GPU hours" to selling "tokens" and agentic outcomes.
      • Inference Growth: Through its "Token Factory," Nebius serves frontier labs and open-weight model developers who are currently capacity-constrained.
      • Agentic Services: The recent acquisition of Taviely (agentic search) highlights a shift toward serving "agents" rather than human software engineers.
  • New User Profiles: Volozh notes that modern AI developers are often multi-cloud and "agentic," choosing platforms based on measurable efficiency and reliability rather than legacy ecosystem loyalty.

Scaling to Meet "Objective" Demand

  • Capacity Targets: Nebius is planning for a footprint measured in hundreds of thousands—and eventually millions—of GPUs, with a power pipeline reaching into the gigawatt range.
  • Capital Requirements: Fulfilling current programs for 3+ gigawatts of capacity will require over $100 billion in total investment.
  • Market Outlook: Volozh views the current market as a "blue ocean" rather than a "red ocean." He argues that demand is "totally objective" because AI allows industries to do things 10 times cheaper or 10 times faster, creating a structural shift in the global economy.
"Demand is so high, coming from so many directions. Whatever is built is consumed. And demand is totally objective. Everybody sees how much new value this thing generates. You can do either 10 times more things or 10 times cheaper, and it's a huge, huge industrial next step." — Arkady Volozh
"We use these big bare metal contracts not as our main line of business, but as one of the ways to finance building our own cloud for the rest of the market." — Arkady Volozh
"We are building the cloud for this new, today I would say agentic world... these new users are choosing the platform which is measurably better, more reliable, more efficient, cheaper." — Arkady Volozh
Demand is so high, coming from so many directions. Whatever is built is consumed. And demand is totally objective. Everybody sees how much new value this thing generates. You can do either 10 times more things or 10 times cheaper, and it's a huge, huge industrial next step. Welcome to Spotlight On, a podcast about how companies are built, from the people doing the building, one exhilarating decision at a time. Arkady, I am thrilled to be back together discussing Nebius with you. You and I actually set out to have this conversation one year after our first podcast, but with everything going on inside of Nebius, I think this was the first window of time we could find. Just in the last two months, you guys have announced large-scale customer commitments, rapid capacity expansion, a deepening partnership with NVIDIA, major financings, and continued build-out of your AI cloud platform. A story that illustrates why, when put together, all of these announcements are far more strategic than they may appear at first glance. To get at that story, I want to take us back to where we left off. It's mid-2024. Nebius has emerged on the scene as the newest entrant in the AI infrastructure race. What is it that the market maybe got wrong or didn't understand about what it was you're building? I think back then we had a clear vision. We started with a tiny 25-megawatt data center, and we had a clear vision that this is a huge build ahead of us. We knew we would need to build a lot, both in terms of scale and in terms of new functionality, new features, and new tools which need to be built for this new era. We saw our niche from the very beginning as helping people build AI. We had this experience. We were building AI in the previous 20 years, and we built a big infrastructure for that, both physical and tooling. And we realized that in this new world, there will be needed hundreds, if not thousands of times more than we have built before, and it will require a completely new set of tools. With our experience both in hardware and software, and building scale systems, we left behind hundreds of megawatts of built data centers. At the same time, we're a totally new startup on the market, and we understand very well the life of startups and the lives of enterprises trying to go into this new uncharted territory of AI. By understanding them deeply, we understand what tools they will need. When we started almost two years ago, there was this vision that we need to build something very, very big. We should own the full stack. We should build through the whole stack. We need to optimize it, which means that we need to own the data centers, we need to build our own racks, and we need to build all the software layers on top of it, from basic cloud to these all-new services. For today, we can say inference or agentic, whatever comes next. We felt that we are, by our qualification, one of the few teams in the world who can build it, both in scale and functionality. We understand what needs to be built. And I think when we started, people just didn't look at us this way. But again, there were different times. People probably didn't understand well what kind of things we're facing. Now it's two years later, and people understand that whatever we will build will be consumed. And we need to go to space for that, maybe even. And people also understand that it should be a new thing. It's not just an old classical cloud and tooling reshaped for this new era. It's a completely new set of tools for a completely new set of users. It's not just software engineers who are using it now. They need different tools. And that's what we are building: big scale, full stack, optimized, and cost-effective. Yeah, I think, you know, unpacking that a bit, I think people may underestimate just how much depth is behind the team inside of Nebius. I mean, what you just articulated requires domain expertise from land and power acquisition through to hardware development of racks, through to a large corpus of engineers going and scaling the software primitives on top of that infrastructure. Your team didn't start from zero. Could you tell us a bit about the team that you've built inside of Nebius and why it's unique for such a young company? Two years ago, as we started, we were quite an unusual startup because we came with a lot of experience. We had 20 or 30 years of experience building large-scale infrastructure for machine learning before it was called AI. We worked with NVIDIA many years before this company was created. Again, we started from a very small installation which we inherited, just 25 megawatts. Eighteen years later, we already were running 10 times of this, and this year we're building and growing again. Nebius will be this, hopefully, big, huge platform with hundreds of thousands of GPUs of capacity, and layers and layers of services tailored and built from scratch for this new AI era. We are building for developers serving the workloads which are demanded by them now, not what was demanded a decade ago. You mentioned the long-standing relationship with NVIDIA, so I want to transition us to some of these big announcements that we've seen from Nebius over the last couple weeks. Help us connect all of them, and why don't we start with NVIDIA. It's not just investment, or just money for us. It's actually much deeper work which is going on behind this investment with NVIDIA, where we together develop and optimize systems. We help them optimize their systems. It's a lot of engineering work between the teams in the background. Again, it's a sign of a deeper collaboration with NVIDIA, and we're extremely proud, of course, that they become our partner and shareholder. But again, it's not about money, just money. It's good to have $2 billion, but what we're building requires tens and hundreds of billions of dollars. Let's shift focus to another big announcement that you guys have made, which is a large commitment from Meta. The headline of that commitment is $27 billion, but really I think what's most interesting is how you architected that relationship. Can you talk a bit about what that $27 billion really means to Nebius and what it means to the overall platform that you're building for the cloud? When you see the news about this 27 or so billion dollars, you should actually understand that in this particular contract, there are actually two contracts inside. One is $12 billion when we really provide bare metal capacity to Meta for themselves to use. And for this, we are getting the second part of the contract, which is $15 billion of backstop. What it means for us is we will be building, and we will use actually Meta's readiness to offtake it, go to the bank, and finance this capacity for our own cloud. These are much higher levels of the cloud which we will be selling on-spot to thousands, or tens of thousands of smaller clients. We're all watching those charts showing GPU-per-hour rates go through the roof. And I guess what I'm hearing from you is that $15 billion contract is locked in at today's long-term rates should you not be able to sell it. But what you're saying is you're going to take that $15 billion of capacity and bring it to your core customer, that retail customer that leans on your software, services, and tooling on top of the infrastructure and pays what you call spot rates in the market—much higher rates. This effectively means that $15 billion could mean 50% or 100% more to Nebius over the life of that contract. Isn't this a similar thing to what we saw happen with Microsoft as well? Different, but a similar thing happened with our Microsoft contract last year. It was a 17-plus billion dollar contract where Microsoft actually got bare metal capacity from us and we're building bare metal capacity for Microsoft AI Lab. But it allows us to go to the banks and use this contract to finance construction of our own cloud. So we use these big bare metal contracts not as our main line of business, but as one of the ways to finance building our own cloud for the rest of the market. That's interesting. You have said that before, that these large deals with some of the big hyperscalers are a great way to finance what we do. I think getting off the blocks to be able to deliver capacity to small startups—startups that frankly don't have a contract that a bank cares to lend against—has been a big kind of chicken-and-egg problem for the ecosystem. I know you have a number of thoughts around how many GPUs are actually serving that end of the market, the core end of the market that Nebius is targeted. You mentioned how we got in there and how we're trying to find ways to finance our way through it. But talk a bit more about that core customer to us and how we serve them. So we see the main market for us, our main business model, in serving the AI-native startups and enterprises going online. And these are, again, thousands of different clients. They don't have behind them these huge piles of capital to build their own capacity or their own thousands of GPUs. We're building a cloud for them. This should be a big cloud. What we're building is actually even this year will be measured in hundreds of thousands of GPUs. So it's basically the same scale as the other big hyperscalers. It's shorter-term, smaller contracts but in large, much larger amounts of clients who actually need much more services from us, which is important. We don't just provide them with GPUs or bare metal. The big clients like Microsoft and Meta, they don't need our software stack because they have their own. These guys, they don't have their own stack, and they need a lot of help from us. And we're building tools for them. And this is our main business. This is what we're building, and we're building it at scale. To be there, you need to provide scalable, very efficient, very performant, and cost-effective systems. That's what we're building. Two thoughts on the capacity side of things. One, Nebius thinks about scaling capacity maybe slightly different from some of the other clouds or neo-clouds you might be compared against for this exact reason. Delivering a multi-tenant hosted cloud experience to the torso of the market requires a different type of capacity planning and scaling than you may see out of a large hyperscale deal on bare metal. So I'd be curious to hear a bit about capacity planning when you think about your core end customer, and how you think about whether we need a 5-gigawatt facility or we need a 30-megawatt facility, or both. Bring us inside the capacity planning of Nebius. To build what we want to build, you need to be very efficient, and you need to start building from scratch, from the land. You need to understand everything from land permits and electricity permits up to how to build data centers, how to build racks, and what software to run. If you outsource it, first of all, it will never be efficient. You pay margins to data center constructors, to rack builders, and to other software companies. We build it through the whole stack. It's a full-stack build, which gives you economic efficiency, but more importantly, it gives you technical efficiency and engineering efficiency. It is optimized to the last bolt. It's optimized on all the layers, and it works together as a system. This is what we build. Again, it should be built cheaply and optimally, performantly, and it should be built in scale. It should be big and very efficient. Looking at the capacity today for Nebius, we have both owned locations as well as co-located locations. What I'm hearing from you sounds like the direction of travel is to own more of our infrastructure from the land all the way through to the software. Well, when we started, we took everything. We took even small colocation sites although they are more expensive because we needed to start quickly. As we build, we build more and more of our own capacity. It's usually larger sites. It's hundreds of megawatts, sometimes gigawatt-plus sites, what we're building. But not only that. As I said, we will be working with any capacity we could find. So let's pull the financing thread a bit because I know people love to talk about how you come up with tens of billions of dollars as a 30 to 40 billion dollar company. Because for a startup like us, with all our public NASDAQ status and size, we're still a young startup for the banks, working in a very risky new part of the economy. But on a five-year contract, it's like 20% of extra margins. It's a huge difference. So we're looking for affordable financing in pretty big volumes. Tens of billions, probably hundreds of billions of dollars. Very helpful. Very helpful. So if we just take a step back, these announcements, embedded in these announcements to have engineering partnerships to help with the software that sits on top of our infrastructure, you have financing available to us to be able to build for the vertically integrated platform we hope to build over time. You talk about the layers of infrastructure. You talk about everything from land through to software. Can you illustrate those layers for us? Because I think a lot of these announcements give us the capability to compete at every layer. And then I want to take us to the platform on top, but I first want to understand the layers. Because there's very few, maybe only the hyperscalers, that have actually stated a vision to own all of those layers the way you have. So walk us through what those layers are. It starts from LPS: Land, Power, Shell. It means land with an industrial permit, power reserved either from the grid or self-generated, and the shell—the data center itself. And it's not just shell, not the walls, it's all the electrical equipment in it. This first layer actually costs, let's say, 20% of total CapEx. And then you need to put the system inside: the racks. We build our own racks. Then there's a layer when you just, let's call it a bare metal service, when you put on, I don't know, a Kubernetes layer on top and you provide it, usually in big chunks for the long-term, to people who have their own vertical systems. It's big tech companies. Bare metal services, usually wholesale volumes, big volumes, to several customers. That's the business of a typical neo-cloud. We are not there. We are building layers on top. On top of this bare cluster software, there's a platform layer, or infrastructure as a layer—just basic services which you would find in any classical hyperscaler like AWS, Azure, GCP, or Oracle. On top of this basic IaaS level, there's a platform with higher-level services. And again, you can choose to sell bare metal at wholesale prices, pretty low prices, but big volumes, or you can go in kind of the retail on-spot market and start offering services which those companies need. They don't develop them themselves. Be it basic services like cloud services, and higher in the level it could be inference, like our Token Factory, or agentic services like agentic search with Tavily, which we recently bought. These services are completely different from what a classical cloud would provide 10 or 15 years ago. It's not just different services. What is really different is the end user of these services. What we're building, we're building it from scratch and we're not building it for the previous generation of customer. We're building it for this new generation of customers who actually have completely different requirements. They don't need a hundred of different tools to stitch together in their cloud. They don't think in these terms. They think agentic. They are usually multi-cloud, they don't care actually where compute is happening, where different services are, or from where different services are consumed, because they don't do it manually. It's the agents who do it. And the agents, they are optimizing efficiency. These new users are choosing the platform which is measurably better, more reliable, more efficient, and cheaper. Yeah, I notice you shy away from the term neo-cloud. And in many ways, you do it because of the five layers we talked about. When you actually own the land, the powered shell, the rack, the software on top of that, and you're not just delivering Kubernetes in a bare metal experience but delivering inference post-training and fine-tuning on top of that cloud, now you look like agentic services, the additional services. That's the AI-native hyperscaler that you're talking about. But I think what's underappreciated today is how you actually compete with hyperscalers. Because neo-clouds aren't your competitors; you're well beyond the scope of neo-clouds. Hyperscalers are your competitors. Hyperscalers have been building a platform for over 10 years, and they have many, many software tools and primitives that sit on top of their infrastructure already. What you're articulating with this agentic world and today's AI-native developer is potentially a window into how Nebius becomes a true competitor to today's hyperscalers. Maybe double-click on that experience for a developer today as far as why they would choose Nebius over one of these embedded, large, proven hyperscalers. Why work with Nebius? When we talk about competition here, it's not a red ocean. It's totally a blue ocean. It's a total new area. They need to move there as well. Also, when we talk about competition, it's not like we're gonna compete and kill any of them. They will continue to exist, they will be dominant. There is an opening now. We see it as an opening. There's something where they don't have any protective moat. These new requirements and these new users—it's all new to the market. And we're talking not about taking their business, we're talking about building new business together with them. The market shares will be changing. And it's, again, it's not a consumer market where the winner takes all. It's a B2B market: prices are important, efficiency is important, everything is important. And there is an opening now which I think has a market share available for players like us. I don't see too many players as of now who are building, A, at this scale, or B, at this depth or heights of the platform. It's interesting how many people in the market talk about delivering a cloud and what that has actually started to mean to the market. And I think your definition of a cloud is about as broad as anyone's in the market in terms of what you want to cover. We actually do compete, I would say, at every layer of that cake, and there are very large businesses being built at every layer of those five layers of infrastructure. And as you build higher and higher, you eat the margin of each layer. So although there are very large businesses built there, you aspire to take all of that margin all the way up to the top. Let's stop at the top for a moment because you mentioned something interesting around what has emerged as potentially one of the largest revenue opportunities in the world of AI, which is delivering inference. This is actually getting value out of all of these models we're training on these huge clusters. You have Token Factory. What is Token Factory and what is your right to win in the world of inference? There is a range of companies who provide inference services. People are training models for then these models to be used for something, to generate value in other industries. And using models is inference. Theoretically, if in the beginning most of capacity was spent on training new and new models, eventually there will be more and more usage of these models and more and more inference. There are frontier labs—we have now, what, five of them in the Western ecosystem—which provide inference services, be it Anthropic, OpenAI, or others. And there is a range of newcomers who provide inference on open-weight models. People like Together AI, Fireworks, Base Ten—there is a range of them. Frontier labs, of course, they're much bigger, and their limiting factor is capacity. They have said many times that their revenues would grow even faster if they had more capacity, and they're getting and buying capacity from everyone. These other players working with open models, their main challenge is again to get capacity. They don't build their own capacity; they're buying it on the market from hyperscalers and neo-clouds. Since the capacity becomes a more and more scarce resource today, there is not enough capacity. Everybody talks about it; we cannot build as much and as fast as needed. Their business is limited. They cannot grow as fast as they could have grown if they had this capacity. They don't have it. We build our own capacity for this, and we have our own capacity for inference. And it's not just having this capacity; also, we optimize the full stack down to the ground, to the racks, and provide more efficient inference. Again, this recent acquisition of Agent is to provide even more efficient, faster, cheaper, low-latency, and higher-throughput models. I think this is why we're growing in inference so fast and why it quickly becomes the next layer of our business. Again, it's not selling bare metal or cloud, or not selling GPU by hours. TL;DR

The ongoing conflict with Iran and sustained high energy prices present significant political risks for incumbents ahead of the midterms, while the domestic debate shifts toward "Gilded Age" inequality and the role of state intervention in the economy.

Iran Conflict and Economic Stability

  • Market Resilience: Despite geopolitical chaos, markets have remained surprisingly stable. Heidi Heitkamp attributes this to the underlying strength and innovation of the American worker, though she warns the $40 trillion national debt remains a looming "wildcard."
  • Policy Inconsistency: Heitkamp notes a lack of consistency in war execution, which is creating anxiety in "farm country" due to skyrocketing fertilizer costs and diesel prices.
  • The "Success" Metric: Mick Mulvaney defines Republican success as a total end to the Iranian nuclear program and ensuring the Straits of Hormuz remain permanently open. He argues Trump cannot exit the conflict until these conditions are met, as a closed strait is an unacceptable economic loss.

Midterm Election Pressure

  • Gas Price Vulnerability: Both speakers agree that $4.50 per gallon gas entering the August/September window is a "tough sell" for the incumbent party.
  • Managing Expectations: President Trump has reportedly begun setting expectations that gas prices will not drop significantly before the midterms, avoiding promises he cannot keep.
  • The "Boots on the Ground" Risk: Heitkamp suggests that eliminating enriched Iranian material may require a ground invasion—a move that would trigger a massive backlash from isolationist voters.

Structural Energy Challenges

  • Slow Recovery: Experts warn that reopening the Straits will not act as a "light switch" for the economy. It could take 4–5 months to see price relief.
  • Supply Deficit: 10% to 15% of Qatari gas production is currently offline and may remain so for several years, representing structural damage that cannot be quickly repaired.
  • The "Nacho" Concept: Mulvaney dismissed the "nacho" theory (the idea that the Strait of Hormuz may never fully reopen), stating the U.S. economy simply cannot tolerate that outcome.

Domestic Political Tensions: Mamdani vs. Griffin

  • Class Warfare: The public dispute between NYC Mayor Zohran Mamdani and billionaire Ken Griffin reflects a broader "Gilded Age" resentment. Heitkamp suggests billionaires must change their rhetoric from defensive to collaborative.
  • The "Communist" Label: Mulvaney characterized Mamdani’s agenda—including government-owned grocery stores—as an experiment in communism that he believes is destined to fail.
  • State-Led Services: Heitkamp defended the concept of state intervention, citing North Dakota’s state-owned bank and mill as successful examples of government filling gaps where the free enterprise system fails, distinguishing this from "Soviet-style communism."

Notable Quotes

"If you go into August to September with $4.50 gallon of gas, it doesn't bode well for the incumbent party. They need this thing to be finished sooner rather than later." — Mick Mulvaney
"We're in a Gilded Age. People are starting to be very resentful of people who aren't taking responsibility but have had tremendous success in this economy." — Heidi Heitkamp
